The Role of the Scrum Master 
Welcome to "The Role of the Scrum Master." After watching this video, you will be able 
to: Compare the Scrum Master's and Project Manager's 
role. 
Define the Scrum Master role. 
Describe the Scrum Master's role as a facilitator and a coach. 
A Scrum Master is not a Project Manager. 
In contrast, they act as Coaches, Facilitators, and Team Members. 
Unlike project managers, a Scrum Master does not plan the overall project from start to 
finish, manage budgets, control risks, or procure material. 
Nevertheless, some of their tasks are associated with project managers' tasks. 
Scrum Masters, lead teams, remove roadblocks, participate in the planning process for the 
team's work, lead meetings and Scrum events, improve processes, report to management, support the Product Owner and other stakeholders, and help the team work together. 
As a coach, a Scrum Master removes impediments and promotes a healthy team environment. 
As a facilitator, they lead the Scrum Process, plan activities, track and collect data, and 
help complete work. 
Let's look at the Scrum Master's role as a facilitator. 
Facilitators provide the team with the tools that they need to complete their tasks. 
In Scrum, teams are not driven by a fixed work package but rather determine creative 
solutions to meet customer requirements. 
While traditional project managers execute a predetermined plan to produce deliverables, 
Scrum Masters utilizes an iterative and incremental approach to complete the work. 
The Scrum Master must navigate the complexities of aligning and realigning the team to the 
Product Owner's vision and figure out what they are working on for the next six weeks 
in a Sprint. 
During the Sprint process, the Scrum Master is responsible for Scrum artifacts and tracking. 
For example, they create burndown charts. 
They also assist the Product Owner in refining the Backlog, setting realistic expectations 
on what the team can complete, creating a Sprint Backlog, and keeping the team focused 
and on track during the Sprint. 
In addition, they conduct Scrum events like the daily Scrum, Scrum Increments, and finally, 
the Sprint retrospective, and then reset the team to work on the next Sprint. 
When managing the team, the Scrum Master acts like a funnel. 
All the noise, ideas, requirements, process steps, team challenges, meetings, inputs to 
artifacts, go through them. 
Once the Scrum Master filters the information, it comes out as polished artifacts, a Sprint 
plan, refined Backlog, increments, and a retrospective. 
They concentrate on the process and prevent the Scrum Team from losing focus. 
Scrum Masters recognize that the team holds the content expertise and facilitates the 
creative process. 
In addition, the Scrum Master equips the team with the tools to resolve conflicts. 
For example, suppose the team has multiple interpretations of a requirement. 
In that case, the Scrum Master will guide the team through reconciling opinions but will avoid contributing an opinion, leading them to conclude. 
They enable continuous improvement by making the team better after each Sprint. 
As coaches, the Scrum Masters focus on the team's progress. 
A good sports coach knows when to draw the best out of their players and maximize their 
strengths to benefit the team. 
This attitude is no different with a Scrum Master. 
When a team member loses sight of the goals or hurries to finish the work and fails to 
meet the implications, the Scrum Master coaches them and brings them on track. 
They help them to recentre through support. 
This support includes helping the team answer their questions by leading them to the answer rather than giving them the answer. 
The Scrum Master leads by example and cares for the team by seeing them as individuals 
rather than resources. 
Their coaching ability allows the team to be creative and collaborative. 
They may even use this coaching ability to help the overall product development effort 
of the organization. 

In this video, you learned: Unlike project managers, a Scrum Master does not plan the overall project from start to finish, manage budgets, control risks, or procure material. 
As a coach, a Scrum Master removes impediments and promotes a healthy team environment. 
As a facilitator, they lead the Scrum Process, plan activities, track and collect data, and 
help complete work. 
Detailed Review of Scrum Artifacts 
This reading describes Scrum artifacts. The Scrum artifacts we will cover are the Product Backlog, Product Increments, and the Sprint Backlog. Tools like Burndown charts are also considered artifacts but are secondary or extended. 
What is an artifact in terms of project management? Artifact is defined as an object demonstrating human skill. It is something modified as opposed to a naturally occurring object. An artifact in Scrum is a "thing" that helps us move the project along. It is considered a "thing" because it is not a model, construct, or anything abstract. Instead, it is a way of helping us manage a host of requirements and moving parts. Artifacts help the team plan and bring the customer's vision to life. 
Have you ever been part of a project where all the pieces seem scattered, disparate, and challenging to wrap your mind around? This is where the artifacts help. Imagine being at a busy airport with thousands of connecting flights and a constantly changing reader board of departments and arrivals. You look at the screen and see the ticker constantly changing status or adding a new schedule. The ticker is like an artifact in Scrum. It helps us make sense of something that is otherwise chaotic, unrefined, and confusing. 
In this reading, you will look at some artifacts in detail, with examples, and gain a firm understanding of how apply to your Scrum teams. The team creates the artifacts in an order that leads to a well-planned and executed Sprint. From a high level, here is how it unfolds. 
The customer has a vision -->Vision begins to unfold in the form of a User Story --> User Stories become Product Backlog items --> Product Backlog is formed (artifact)-->Backlog Prioritized -->Sprint Planning occurs/Product Increment decided (artifact) --> Create Sprint Backlog (artifact) -->Sprint -->Complete work -->Retrospective occurs.
1. Product Backlog: One of the most recognizable artifacts is the Product Backlog. It helps you prioritize the work that requires completion. Imagine having a deck of cards that you fling in the air and it lands on the ground randomly. Now imagine you have project requirements or deliverables that the customer expects, and you throw them in the air and see where they land, close your eyes, and grab a random piece. This is what a Scrum would be like without the product backlog. You would have no idea where to begin or what is essential to the customer. If your Product Owner sent you an email and stated, "Here are the requirements. Please build." Your first question will be where to start and what is essential. This is what the Backlog does for you. You prepare it with the collaboration of the product owner, who decides what to do first and when. The Scrum Master helps by keeping the Product Owner on track and ensuring that the work of Sprint is realistic. 
1. What is in the Backlog? The Backlog contains whatever is vital to the customer. This could be a new item, feature, fix, update, scope change, or anything the team must accomplish to satisfy the customer. Ideally, any item that the team works on should come from the Backlog. If it is not in the Backlog, you risk missing requirements, not understanding what the customer wants, or working on something the customer does not prioritize. 
2. An item on the Backlog is open to negotiation with the Product Owner. Being on the list only means it is an option for the team to work on, but the planning process will help decide what happens on the Sprint. 
3. The team prioritizes User Stories since these can turn into requirements. 
2. Product Increment: Product Increments are the completed work. They are what the team completes from the Product Backlog. It includes the work completed on the most recent Sprint and the work completed from all other Sprints. These small chunks come out of the Sprint plus the previous pieces of work. All pieces of work are connected. It breaks the silo approach of building the pieces and putting them together. The concept is like children's building blocks. You may start with building block one and set it on the ground. This is the first Sprint. Then you start building block two and attach it to building block one. This is how you keep on incrementing the blocks. In the finale, you attach building block three to the now grouped building blocks one and two. Instead of having three separate blocks that make up the requirements, you now have one integrated group. Therefore, the work completed after each Sprint is built from the last Sprint. 
3. Sprint Backlog: The Sprint Backlog contains the items taken from Product Backlog that the team works on during the Sprint. The Sprint Backlog items are chosen through the Sprint planning process. The Sprint Backlog is like customized ordering. 
The Job Description of a Scrum Master 
Welcome to "The Job Description of a Scrum Master." 
After watching this video, you will be able to: 
Describe scrum basics and scrum roles. 
Compose a comprehensive job description of a Scrum Master. 
Each Scrum Master role fulfills a specific need for the organization. 
There are some common elements as well as nuances that make them unique. 
In other words, each Scrum Master job posting requires a skill set, experience, and training 
that aligns with the organization's needs. 
The Scrum Master's role and skill set take due consideration of the following: 
The Scrum Master is not a developer. 
They exist to work with teams, not do the work for the team, oversee, code, or execute any of the tasks of the group. 
Some job descriptions may require a technical background because it enhances your ability 
to be effective, but you, as a Scrum Master, should not be doing the team's work. 
The Scrum Master is not a project manager. 
Scrum Masters are commonly compared to a coach. 
As a Scrum Master, your job is to support the team in their work. 
The team does the work, but you may inspire them to greatness or help them figure out a better way to handle a difficult situation. 
When thinking about leadership styles, you should not be direct. 
You should always be in the mentor, coach, and support realm. 
Let's discuss some common elements of Scrum Master's job description. 
Depending on an organization's needs, the job description can be a mix any of the following 
elements. 
Supporting, teaching, and coaching Agile and Scrum best practices. 
As a Scrum Master, you play the role of the expert in Scrum. 
You are the keeper of the roadmap and will guide the team on achieving their goals by 
taking the path of Scrum. 
Facilitating important Scrum ceremonies, including Daily Scrum, Sprint Planning, Demos, Retrospectives, tracking metrics, and reporting out. 

Removing barriers or obstacles and creating an environment of innovation and creativity. Teams are under tight deadlines, and often there is no time for the team to work through their roadblocks. 
The Scrum Master always plays a key role in resolving any issues. 
Sharing across product teams. Resources are typically scarce, and a single 
Scrum Master may support multiple teams, so you need to be a good multitasker and learn 
to prioritize. 
Collaborating to create an environment to achieve success. 
This role requires you to work with stakeholders, leaders, developers, and anyone associated with Sprint across the organization. 
Leading discussions. You should keep the conversation in scope and maximize the use of time. 
Scrum Masters often have to get the conversation back on track in an effective and efficient 
way. Additionally, it means you must help the group focus. 
In addition, the Scrum Master's job description includes the following: 
Minimizing dependence on external forces. With more independence, the team will focus 
more on completing their work. 
Creating an environment of continuous improvement. Scrum Masters, the team, and the organization cannot afford to finish a project without the commitment to improve on each sprint. 
Keeping the team focused on the customer's needs. 
Ensuring the team is within the scope and overseeing product implementation. 
You, as the Scrum Master, need to ensure the team delivers the final product to the customer. 
Traveling is a possibility. 
This is a job 100% committed to the mission and may require you to be on-site. 
Managing change. Your job is to navigate through any changes. 
Qualifications are also an important component of the job description. 
The organization decides the length of experience needed for these roles. 
First and foremost, you must know the Scrum Process. 
You should have demonstrated experience working with tools like Kanban boards, a deep understanding of Scrum Artifacts, and leading teams. 
Certifications in Certified Scrum Master (CSM) 
PMP, Six Sigma Black Belt, Agile, or Lean. Working experience in Scrum environments with 
a Product Owner. Expert-level knowledge or experience in prototyping, 
testing, and usability analysis Knowledge of tools such as Atlassian. 
The key is finding the right job description that fits your interest and expertise. 
Organizations look for the right mix of qualifications and skills, and Scrum Masters should look for the most interesting projects and industries. 

In this video, you learned that: The Scrum Master requires a particular skill set, experience, and training that aligns with the organization's needs. 
The Scrum Master is not a developer. The Scrum Master is not a project manager. 
The Scrum Master facilitates important Scrum ceremonies, including Daily Scrum, Sprint 
Planning, Demos, Retrospectives, tracking metrics, and reporting out. 
The Scrum Master keeps the team focused on the customer's needs. 
The Scrum Master should be certified and must know the Scrum process. 

Expert Viewpoints: What Does a Scrum Master Do? 
You welcome to expert viewpoints, What Does a Scrum Master Do? 
Can you list the top three Scrum Master responsibilities you feel are essential 
to the success of a Sprint? 
The top three responsibilities of a Scrum Master are to be a bridge between 
the Product Owner and the development team, to clear obstacles and 
protect the team from distractions, and to keep the team focused on what matters. 
The top three Scrum Master responsibilities that are essential to the success of a sprint, in my opinion, would be facilitation, servant leadership, and coaching and mentoring. 
These responsibilities of ensuring the Scrum process is followed, removing impediments for the team and helping the team continuously improve are essential. 
So, three Scrum Master responsibilities that I feel are important to the success of a Sprint, first of all, planning. 
You're not getting anywhere without planning. 
The Scrum Master really needs to facilitate and coach in that exercise, and the Scrum Master needs to get them to do it efficiently and effectively, because you have only a certain amount of time to do that, a time-box. 
And you don't want to spend too much time planning, you want to get to the work, but 
there's a way to do it efficiently, and the Scrum Master is going to help you do that. 
Now, the Scrum Master is also an expert in doing the retrospective. 
Now, after your Sprint, you want to look back and think about how you can get better. 
The retrospective is going to help you do that because you've just done six to eight weeks of work, and you need to think about the next iteration. 
Well, before you do that, you have to look back and say, well, what worked? 
What didn't work? 
What was confusing? 
What was difficult? 
What can we change better? 
What ideas do we have? 
We need to focus on an objective and hone in on some things that we really need to improve on, why? 
Because the next Sprint is important and you want to make it better. 
And the other thing that I'd say, the third thing that I would, my opinion that is extremely important is the stand-up. 
The stand-up is that one scheduled time per day that the team gets together, they get to hear each other, what they're talking about in their entirety, so the whole team is there. 
And the Scrum Master can be a powerful tool in getting the team to where they need to be. 
The top three Scrum Master responsibilities that contribute to the success of the Sprint are number one, facilitating Scrum events such as the Sprint Planning, Daily Scrum, 
the Sprint Review, and the Sprint retrospective. 
A Scrum Master ensures that these events are well organized, time-boxed, and focused on achieving their goals. 
Number two, removing impediments or blockers, number three, coaching and mentoring team on Agile Methodologies and a Scrum framework. 

First of all, facilitator, a Scrum Master needs to facilitate multiple Scrum ceremonies, and these include scrum planning, the daily scrum. 
The success and effectiveness of these ceremonies is directly related to the skill that a Scrum Master possesses in terms of being a good facilitator. 
Secondly, a Scrum Master needs to be a good coach. 
You need to prepare the development team and other key members to be successful. 
Hopefully, through effective coaching, you can motivate the team to become Scrum Masters in the future. 
Finally, the Scrum Master needs to be organized. 
They need to truly understand the end to end Scrum process, they need to ensure others understand it, and they need to ensure that Scrum is accomplished within the guidelines of the Scrum Guide. 
The top three responsibilities of a Scrum Master I feel are very essential to the success of a Sprint, include one, being a servant leader, two, removing impediments and 
three, being a very great facilitator
Comparing Scrum Values to Project Management Methodologies 
Welcome to this module, where we will discuss how Scrum values compare with other project management methodologies. 
After watching this video, you will be able to: 
Describe the evolution of Scrum values, Describe the Scrum values that lay the foundation 
for the working of teams, List the scenarios where Scrum is used, and Explain how Scrum is mixed with other methodologies. Scrum employs a creative process to manage work in a project. 
All industries and project management methods emphasize ethics, but what makes Scrum different is that the basis of this framework is values. 
These values find their origins in the Agile Manifesto at https://agilemanifesto.org/. 
The group that conceptualized Agile recognized that software development required a change in outlook to give better results. 
Even before establishing a framework, the group decided that software development could 
not be as effective without an understanding of some basic ideas. 
The opening line in the Manifesto is that they recognized a better way. 
They focused on people over a process, functionality over documentation, collaboration over negotiation, and adaptation to change rather than adhering to a plan. 
The Manifesto set the stage for a new way of development that focused on people. 
Along the same lines, the authors came up with the Principles. 
The Principles focus on satisfying the customer, embracing change, and quick delivery of products to the customer. 
Emphasis is on building trust, working efficiently, excellence, simplicity, and delivering at 
intervals. 
Understanding this backdrop lays the groundwork for understanding Scrum Values. 
Scrum Values emphasize people, relationships, and a better way of doing work. 
The first value is Commitment. 
Commitment implies an agreement among the team to achieve goals. 
Being committed to the goal means the team is connected and working toward the same end. 
The second is openness. 
As collaborators, it helps the team to be open and honest about the difficulties and work through any disagreements. 
While there may be differences among the team members, they must display mutual respect. 
This attitude is different from following a set of ethics that define the right or wrong behavior. 
This respectful behavior lays the foundation for the existence of Scrum teams where all 
members embrace each other's individual styles, contributions, ideas, and creativity. 
Courage is unique to Scrum teams as well. 
The display of courage means teams will not be afraid to follow the right path. 
In addition, Scrum incorporates three pillars that are connected like a Venn diagram. 
These are Transparency, Inspection, and Adaptation. 
Transparency means that the team is honest and willing to be seen as they truly are, along with any mistakes, challenges, and everything that the team encounters during the process of developing and working in a team. 
Inspection is the feedback received in the interest of getting it right and addressing the comments appropriately. 
Adaptation promotes continuous improvement. 
Some examples of these values would be helping teammates to stay focused on their goals and supporting each other. 
Working in silos and being tasked or process-oriented would discourage the team-centered approach. 
In an environment of uncertainty and creativity, it is critical for the team to feed on their 
strengths. 
Openness makes them somewhat vulnerable, but the advantage is that it inspires creativity. 
It is the ability to look at ideas from different angles. 
Respect is knowing that teams may disagree but work together to figure out a solution. 
Finally, having courage means that you will pursue what is right even when it is tough. 
An example of this is supporting a team member's unpopular decision because it is right or 
generating an idea that best serves the customer. 
What sets Scrum apart from other project management and development methods, and when is the best time to use it? 
Scrum is most appropriate in development scenarios. 
Just do a quick internet search of Scrum Master job descriptions, and it will return a host 
of industries, including financial services, construction, consulting, government, military, 
automotive, and higher education. 
Practitioners most commonly apply it to software development, but in recent years, other areas like marketing, Lean, manufacturing, or any uncertain situation. 
Finally, practitioners can apply Scrum or some of the Scrum frameworks and mix them 
with other methodologies. 
You will need to understand this for the PMI-ACP exam as well. 
In fact, the Agile Practice Guide clearly states that Agile practitioners frequently mix methods. 
You may have regulatory, supply chain, and customer-specific requirements that may call 
for the use of an eclectic approach. 
The Agile Guide provides an example where you may use the Scrum framework, Kanban method, and elements of eXtreme Programming (XP) where Scrum provides guidance on the use of a backlog, a product Owner, a Scrum Master, and a Cross-functional development team, including Sprint planning daily scrum, sprint review, sprint retrospective, sessions. 
A kanban board helps the team visualize the flow of work. 
XP would inspire the use of story cards, integration, and so on. 

In this video, you learned that: Scrum values find their origins in the Agile Manifesto. Manifesto focused on people, functionality, collaboration, and adaptation to change. Scrum Values are commitment, openness, courage, transparency, inspection, and adaptation. Scrum is most appropriate in development scenarios. 
The Agile Practice Guide clearly states that Agile practitioners frequently mix methods. 
A kanban board helps the team visualize the flow of work. 
XP would inspire the use of story cards, integration, and so on. 
Key Players in the Scrum Process
The Scrum methodology has distinct players. Each team member has an essential role in the process. In this reading, we will look at the role of each, what they contribute, and give some examples. Most organizations give job titles to the employee; at other times, titles are as per the functional role. There are three roles associated with Scrum. These roles are the Scrum Master, the Scrum Product Owner, and Team Members. 
Scrum Master
The Scrum Master is the expert of the Scrum Process. The Scrum Master facilitates the Scrum Process. They are the glue that connects what the Product Owner brings to the table and the team that does the work. Without the Scrum Master, there is no connection between the team, the work, and the Product Owner. In the absence of the Scrum Master, confusion could settle in, and there would be no one to keep the Product Owner, and Team focused on the process. The Scrum Master:
* Works with the Product Owner to develop the priorities for the team 
* Facilitates the process 
* Runs the Product Backlog prioritization process and the Sprint Planning Process 
* Keeps the team motivated to see the end 
* It keeps them focused on the goal
The daily scrum enables all these activities. A full treatment of Scrum Master is found in the Role of a Scrum Master.
Scrum Product Owner
The Scrum Product Owner represents the customer to the Scrum Master and the Team. The Product Owner is a visionary who collaborates with stakeholders, knows how to stay within scope, and pleases the customer. The application of this is the ability to refine the backlog. This role takes on leadership responsibilities by making product decisions. It steers the team toward the vision. To achieve this, it should be skilled in identifying risks, constraints, and external factors that could become an impediment. In addition, they should be an expert at turning customer ideas into product ideas. This person also ensures the team focuses on the customer's needs. They work with the Scrum Master throughout the process and, at the end of the process, ensure that what the team is developing meets the customer's requirements. They can also foresee what kind of constraints a team may face in the development process. They collaborate with other key players to keep the product's vision intact. The Product Owner is aligned with market trends and other important indicators that point toward the Voice of the Customer. They may work on the specifications of the requirements, understand the pros and cons of making product decisions, and determine what value-add means. Thus, the Product Owner is the face of the customer. This person will be consulted with any product-related questions for the Scrum Team.
Team Members
If the Product Owner is the brain and the Scrum Master is the heart, the Team Members are the hands and feet of the Scrum Team. They are the players that do the work and make things happen. Once the decision is made on what work will be completed, the team brings it to life. The team can also influence defining the work of Sprint. The team decides what it thinks is executable. They are the Subject Matter Experts (SMEs), after all. 
Other key players in Scrum
While some roles may be considered non-traditional regarding Scrum, they influence the Scrum Team's objectives and what they work on. Note they do not influence the Scrum Process. Again, one of the key responsibilities of the Scrum Master is to keep the team focused on using the appropriate process. 
Coach
Although the Scrum Master and Coach are similar, the Coach has expanded duties outside the Scrum process. A Coach may work with the Scrum Master as part of an organization's big picture of product development and project management, especially on complex projects. They may mentor the Scrum Master on how the pieces fit together. For example, suppose a Scrum Team is working on developing a technology or one component for a massive aerospace project. In that case, other Agile methodologies apply as part of the program. The Scrum Team may be working on developing a concept that was created upstream. The Coach can help mentor through that process. In such situations, a project manager may also be a part of how the Scrum Team rolls up to the larger development project. 
Stakeholders
Customers: They are the driving force behind the projects. Imagine working on a project that is 75% complete, and then the customer disapproves of it, saying it does not fit their requirements. These are the worst words product developers or project managers want to hear. Such a scenario could derail the whole project and may land the team in a bit of trouble. In Lean, we talk about the Voice of the Customer because we should avoid presuming to know what the customer wants without getting their input. Hence, this is the reason we have a Product Owner to represent that voice. 
Executives: Project management in any form must account for executives. Executives are responsible for the organization's mission, values, and direction. It is important to understand that no project or team can sustain its mission if it does not align with the organization's direction. Just like conducting a Sprint meets the requirements of the Product Owner, executives ensure that the types of projects, the products developed, and the team's work add value and contribute to the overall goals. 
Regardless of your role on the Scrum team, you should have an end objective. An example of the importance of the executive is during a forecasting session for the year, the executive states that they intend to make the organization more agile using the Scrum methodology. As this becomes official, it empowers the team to move in the direction of implementing a Scrum approach. With executive support, the Scrum Master can remove roadblocks and move product development forward because it has the support of the management. Without executive support, other parts of the organization lack motivation. Even if the customer wants it, the executive team may believe it is not required and is outside the core business.
Scrum Master Defined

Keys to Success: Skills, Attributes, and Personality Traits Essential to Scrum Master Success 
Welcome to this module on how Scrum Master's skills, attributes, and personality traits 
are keys to their success. 
After watching this video, you will be able to: 
Describe the skills required by Scrum Masters to be successful. 
Describe the attributes required by Scrum Masters to be successful. 
Describe the personality traits required by Scrum Masters to be successful. 
To make a Scrum Master successful, three areas are essential: Skills, Attributes, and Personality Traits. 
Let's start with skills. 
First and foremost, the Scrum Master must be an expert in the Scrum framework. 
They must possess the technical knowledge of the Scrum Methodology. 
The team and the organization will expect the Scrum Master to know how to guide the 
organization. 
In some cases, they may be expected to lead an organization through a transformation. 
As per the Scrum Guide developed by a couple of authors of Scrum at scrumguides.org, 
Scrum Masters are true leaders who serve the Scrum Team and the larger organization. 
They should have leadership experience. 
The keyword is leadership as opposed to management. 
Leaders inspire, sell a vision, and involve the team in decision-making. 
Leaders know when to employ the right type of management in the right situations. 
For example, in a very high-stakes delivery where the team may have limited experience, 
the leader may take on a more active role in the decision-making and even direct the team. 
Whereas in other situations, the leader may support and encourage and help the team to find the answers to the problems. 
The Scrum Master serves the Scrum Team in several ways, including coaching the team 
members in self-management and cross-functionality, helping the Scrum Team focus on creating high-value Increments that meet the Definition of Done, causing the removal of impediments to the Scrum Team's progress, and, ensuring that all Scrum events take place and are positive, productive, and kept within the timebox. 
Scrum Masters must be skilled negotiators and problem solvers. 
Without these skills, it will be impossible to help the team achieve success. 
This may involve negotiation with stakeholders, the Product Owner, other managers, and anyone who can influence the team's success. 
They should know how to create opportunities to collaborate and know how to facilitate 
a team so that they stay on track. 
For example, a Scrum Master may need to bring in a different Scrum team to collaborate if 
there is an intersection between the work these teams are doing. 
If the only way to get Increments to meet the definition of Done is through collaboration 
and problem-solving, the Scrum Master must facilitate that process. 
A critical skill is being an effective facilitator. 
Scrum Masters need to be effective in facilitating the estimation process, as this requires input from multiple sources. 
As per the Scrum Guide, there are several tasks the Scrum Master as a coach and facilitator, 
helps the Product Owner to complete. 
They help find techniques for effective Product Goal definition and Product Backlog management. 
In addition, they help the Scrum team understand the need for clear and concise Product Backlog items. 
They also assist in empirical product planning for a complex environment. 
The Scrum Master is not necessarily providing the answers but showing the way. 
They set realistic goals that are achievable as they understand the capabilities of their team and know how to drill down the tasks where clarity is required. 
Finally, Scrum Masters must have excellent communication skills. 
All that we have mentioned above involves communication. 
Strong communication skills will be the channel by which the Scrum Master's coaching, facilitation, leadership, and planning ability shine. 
Now let's look at the attributes a Scrum Master must possess to be successful. 
The Scrum Master must be willing to live the Scrum Values and lead by example. 
In other words, Scrum Masters must be a Servant Leader. 
They should value the team and know how to get them involved. 
They should accept that a gray area might exist within the team but encourage the team 
members to adapt. 
They should inspire creativity and dare to think in a way that is unorthodox. 
The Scrum Master needs to be resourceful and should be empathetic. 
Scrum Master personality traits are based on Scrum Values. 
Scrum Masters must be compassionate, dynamic, and inclined to think critically. 
They should demonstrate humility and accountability to the team for helping them to move the work forward. 
When they are working with the team or the Product Owner, they must be committed and 
be able to inspire those around them.
 
In this video, you learned that: Scrum Masters should have the following skills: 
Technical knowledge of Scrum methodology, 
Leadership skills, 
Team management skills,
 Negotiation and problem-solving skills, 
Coaching and facilitation skills, and Communication skills. 
Scrum Masters should possess attributes to help the team achieve success. 
Scrum Master personality traits are based on Scrum Values. 
Scrum Master Defined
Welcome to this module on Scrum Master Defined. 
After watching this video, 
you'll be able to define Scrum Master's role, describe Scrum Master's job as servant leaders, 
and describe Scrum Master's responsibilities towards the team and organization. 
A Scrum Master is a multi-dimensional leader, teacher, coach, problem-solver, and gatekeeper. 
All roles are played by the Scrum Master. 
They can apply appropriate skills for each area when required. 
They act as leaders by relentlessly clearing the path for the team to work without interruption. 
They act as teachers and coaches because they uphold the Scrum values and instruct others on the framework of Scrum. 
They are gatekeepers because they prevent the process from being tainted by outside influence. 
They do so when they guide the team and the organizations in the way of Scrum and solve the problems that are obstacles in the path of the team. 
As a Scrum Master, you have the job of establishing Scrum in the enterprise. 
You need to be an expert in both Scrum theory and practice, not just for the team but the organization. 
This role has accountability for the team's progress and impact on the customer's requirements. 
Scrum Masters are servant leaders, they serve the organization and the team. 
This includes being a coach. 
Not all team members easily embrace what it means to be selfless on a team. 
The servant leader and coach will help guide them through this process, this could mean learning what it means to work as a team in this context or employing basic team-building techniques. 
Imagine how distracting it would be to work with a colleague who complains about the work constantly and states there's a better way to do things. 
The Scrum Master coaches such team members by supporting them and helping them to answer questions or understand their role in product development. 
The Scrum Master may be called upon to help when other areas not related to evelopment, but make an application of Scrum as a way of improving productivity. 
Again, this is not accomplished through authoritative force, but rather coaching, supporting, teaching, and guiding. 
The Scrum Master keeps the team focused on the goal of creating value to meet the definition of done. 
To achieve done, the Scrum Master relies on events to ensure what they deliver is value-add 
and their goals are accomplished. 
You must do this through disciplined employment of the Scrum methodology. 
For example, you must keep the team within the allotted time or the time-box for events. 
The Scrum Master has responsibility towards the team and the product owner. 
As a facilitator, the Scrum Master must work with the product owner on effective ways of 
defining product goals and manage the product backlog, often called grooming. 
The product backlog and sprint backlog should be refined and clear to the team to avoid miscommunication and results that fail to meet the customer's requirements. 
As the expert in Scrum, this person may work in an organizational level to train, teach, coach, and lead the organization through an implementation of the methodology at the enterprise level. 
The Scrum Master may be called upon to help in other areas not related to development, 
but make an application of Scrum as a way of improving productivity. 

In this video, you learned that a Scrum Master is a multi-dimensional leader, teacher, coach, problem-solver, and gatekeeper. 
A Scrum Master needs to be an expert in both Scrum theory and practice, not just for the team but the organization. 
Scrum Masters are servant leaders who serve the organization and the team. 
As a facilitator, the Scrum Master will work with the product owner on effective ways of 
defining product goals and managing the product backlog, often called grooming. 
Key leadership Traits essential to Scrum Master Success
Leadership defines Scrum Master success. Transformational leadership encourages adapting a value system and shifting away from the traditional comprehensive plan toward a customer-focused creativity and incremental delivery system.
The key traits that support this leadership style include:
Scrum framework expertise 
Process knowledge: Successful Scrum Masters wield a deeper understanding of the approach than other participants. Stakeholders and leaders rely on their ability to drive Scrum implementation and educate others in the organization. They apply the appropriate tools when needed and are the subject matter experts who lead the team through the development process work and the mechanics of Sprint Planning, Sprint, Sprint Reviews, and Sprint Retrospectives. 
Example: A stakeholder suggests benchmarking an unrelated development project from last year. This person believes every sprint task estimate should be approached using a bottom-up technique because a team successfully used it. The Scrum Master should thank the person for suggesting it. As per the advice they should evaluate the Sprint based on the required work and lead the team to decide the best option. 
Successful communicator 
Communication involves sending and receiving information. Successful communicators deliver information in a clear, concise manner that promotes understanding. On the other hand, you must be a good listener by validating the team's concerns and reading between the lines. 
Stakeholders, the team, and the Product Owner might communicate unclearly or only provide hints or bits of information but require you to see the warning signs of an issue arising. In such a situation, you must include the appropriate follow-up and ask the right questions. 
Example: A developer on the team indicates that the testing department had several vacancies. Nothing else is said about it, but the Scrum Master should act on this news. The interpretation here is that it could impact your Sprint timeline if you wait on the Testing department to finish its work. The Scrum Master needs to listen for signs that something is wrong. The team members may not always communicate issues directly, even in the Daily Stand-up. 
Inspirational rather than authoritative 
The Scrum team members report to managers, not the Scrum Master. Scrum Masters lead by example and coach teams by inspiring them to greatness, cultivating creativity, and equipping them to find solutions. The only tasks a Scrum Master assigns are related to the Scrum Process. 
Example: A Sprint is experiencing delays due to what appears to be a performance issue. The Scrum Master meets with the team members and discovers some coding developed by a different department that the team members did not understand. The Scrum Master requests support from the department that wrote the code to clarify the queries. For the next Sprint, the Scrum Master arranges for someone to train the employees on the code developed by the other department. This leadership approach avoids censuring the team members for their incompetency and, at the same time, enhances their skills. 
Commitment to success 
The Scrum Master removes roadblocks and mitigates risks impacting the team's success path. They ask team members daily during stand-up meetings if they have any roadblocks. Roadblocks or issues come in many shapes and sizes. The Scrum Master knows how to navigate the team around these issues or who can help resolve them. 
Example: Since team members do not report to the Scrum Master, there may be some resistance from their functional manager. A team member, Bob, is working on multiple projects, and the manager, Anne, informs the Scrum Master, Mary, that they cannot commit to the project part-time for six weeks as per the agreement at the project kick-off. This could delay the work of other team members waiting on their tasks. Mary decides to negotiate with Anne to have Bob work full-time, three days per week, for the next three weeks. Anne agrees to the arrangement. As a result, Bob finishes the work on the Sprint. 
Not everything can be anticipated or estimated, but finding a remedy is an important trait of the Scrum Master. 
Ownership 
Effective Scrum Masters follow through on their obligations by demonstrating a commitment to the team. 
Example: A team member, Grace, faces scrutiny from a different department and brings this up in the Daily Scrum. She is hoping the Scrum Master, Mary, could talk to her manager to clear things up. She shares that the last Scrum Master they worked with did not stand up and follow through on this. After getting more information, Mary contacts the manager to discuss the issue. Team members need reassurance that the Scrum Master will Invest in the team's success. 
Focus on the right place 
The Scrum Master must meet the requirements of the Product Owner. In addition, the Scrum Master must manage multiple competing priorities and develop strategies for meeting these requirements. This may include completing urgent tasks first and preventing the team from committing to unrealistic goals during Sprint Planning. 
Example: The Product Owner presents five user stories and five items from the backlog as a priority for the next Sprint. The Scrum Master points out that this number of tasks at the given level of complexity would be impossible to complete in six weeks. The Scrum Master works with the Product Owner to reorganize and push them to the next Sprint. 
Promotes a positive culture 
The Scrum Master must foster an environment of rich collaboration. As the face of the team, the Scrum Master must represent the team accurately and celebrate the accomplishments. 
Forces outside the team can influence the team negatively. The Scrum Master must be unbiased to navigate through team politics and ensure that everyone's voice is heard and they are represented fairly. 
Example: A stakeholder raises a question in front of other leaders about the team's decision to include a help tab on the homepage of a client's new website. The Scrum Master explains that this feature makes it more intuitive for new users and is a request by the Product Owner. 
Scrum applies to complicated projects, but the framework strives for an efficient way of managing uncertainty and discovers ways to add value. This iterative nature is analogous to the Plan, Do, Check, and Act approach in Lean. The difference maker is the focus on optimization in the Check and Act phases. Scrum achieves this through the Sprint and Sprint retrospective process. 
Effective Scrum Masters treat team members as creative individuals with ideas and contributions. Leadership in this context is harnessing creativity by challenging the team to apply it to the big picture. Therefore, Scrum Masters must view their team as individuals working toward agreed-upon goals instead of a collection of resources responsible for set tasks. This is called compassion, as they should be treated as real people.


Introduction to Personas 
Welcome to Introduction to Personas. 
After watching this video, you will be able to: 
Explain the need for personas. 
Describe how to make a persona. 
Describe the template to use for creating a persona. 
Describe the steps to create a persona. 
Personas are one of the most useful tools in Scrum. 
They help conceptualize the customer and their needs. 
Whether the product targets a specific demographic, or you have a diverse customer group, Personas allow you to understand them just a little more. 
You can construct someone who needs the product through research, collaboration with marketing, stakeholders, and anyone else who can prove insight to the customer. 
Having the right information will support an effective persona. 
Consider a product where you have twenty different personas, all with very specific needs. 
It would be impossible for the team to create a product that meets everyone's needs. 
Now, if you have twenty personas with similarities and aligned in their basic needs, this will 
give the Product Owner a good idea and a starting point for the creative vision. 
Personas should be simple yet have enough detail to be believable and inspire creativity. 
To bring the vision to life, create a believable person. 
They should be visual, and the Product Owner should have multiple personas. 
If the persona is true-to-life, it is the closest experience you can create to a live 
customer. 
The template should be simple and efficient. 
The template should comprise of a minimum of three columns. 
The first column is the photo and name. 
With the image, you can imagine what this person would look like and their need for 
the product. 
The second column is where you fill in the details. 
You should have information about their demographic, such as age, income, occupation, geographic location, social status and interests, lifestyle, and even a little about their personality. 
The third column can be focused on the product. 
It tells you how you will meet the needs of the persona. 
You will develop goals for the product and prioritize them. 
The first step in creating a persona is brainstorming and doing research. 
Research involves compiling data about the user. 
Try to find out as much relevant information as possible. 
You want to get to know this person. 
For example, suppose you are a software company developing an app to order curbside pick-up groceries. 
In that case, you want to understand the common issues or what prevents people from using the app. 
You should know what people like and then who the users are. 
You may need to be creative and use published data, surveys, focus groups, interviews, and 
random samplings. 
This research should aim at gathering the information you want to know about your customer. 
Now that you have the data, you can begin to analyse it. 
Perhaps you want to categorize the data to distinguish between your customers' needs. 
You should have enough categories to group them by demographic, needs, interests, challenges, and other distinctions that will yield five personas. 
Next, you can begin transforming the data into a persona. 
First, you can start populating your template. 
You should use one template per persona. 
Select a persona's name and photo, then start filling in the rest. 
The idea is to make them feel as real as possible to maximize the value of this exercise. 
Feel free to create as many drafts as needed until you feel it represents a real person. 
Now, let's look at a few examples of personas. 
First, Sally is a 35-year-old female. 
She is a mom of four and works full-time as a Vice President of Sales for a medical device 
company in Philadelphia. 
She has a one-hour commute to work daily, so her time is limited. 
Outside of work, she enjoys riding her stationary bike and cooking. 
She is also active in the kids' school PTA. 
She plans vacations for her family to visit their grandparents in California three times 
annually. 
She has difficulty planning some of the details such as, renting a car in advance, planning 
meals, booking the hotel, and planning activities. 
She needs suggestions and is frustrated when having to spend hours figuring out her trip. 
She needs a one-stop shop that can make planning the trip easier. 
Here is another example. 
Julius is a 75-year-old retired man who worked in aerospace for 40 years. 
Julius lives in Florida and enjoys playing golf three times per week. 
At this point in life, recreation is what keeps him busy. 
He prefers not to spend much time on a computer because it only frustrates him. 
And he worries about information being online and who is using it. 
Recently, his favorite golf course began requiring a membership to reserve tee times. 
He has limited computer experience and is easily frustrated. 
He needs to be able to plan his tee times with little hassle. 
Let's look at example three. 
Janet is a 24-year-old woman from Kentucky. 
She just moved to Texas and is trying to establish care for her Goldendoodle puppy with a new provider. 
She has tried some apps on her phone, but they have been complicated and unsuccessful. 
She wants to make an appointment with a new provider for her puppy easily. 

In this video, you learned: Personas help conceptualize the customer and 
what they care about. 
Personas should be simple yet have enough detail to be believable and inspire creativity. 
The template to create personas should comprise of a minimum of three columns. 
The steps to create personas comprise of brainstorming, research, analysis, and filling the template. 
Module 1 Summary: Scrum and Scrum Master Basics
Congratulations! You have completed this module. At this point in the course, you know:
* The five events of the Scrum process are Sprint, Sprint Planning, Daily Scrum, Sprint Review, and Sprint Retrospective.
* The three artifacts are Product Backlog, Sprint Backlog, and Increment.
* The Product Owner prioritizes User Stories for the development team.
* Sprint duration is usually six weeks but can vary.
* As a coach, a Scrum Master removes impediments and promotes a healthy team environment.
* As facilitators, they lead the Scrum Process, plan activities, track and collect data, and help complete work.
* Scrum values are commitment, openness, courage, transparency, inspection, and adaptation.
* Scrum Masters should possess technical knowledge, leadership, problem-solving, coaching, and communication skills.
* Scrum Masters are multi-dimensional leaders, experts in Scrum theory and practice, and servant leaders.
* Personas are fictional biographies of users based on market research.
* Personas help conceptualize customers and should be simple yet detailed.

Module 1 Glossary: Scrum and Scrum Master Basics
TermDefinitionAgileA project management approach that promotes adaptive planning, evolutionary development, early delivery, and continuous improvement.Agile ManifestoA set of values and principles that underpin the Agile approach to software development. The Agile Manifesto emphasizes individuals and interactions, working software, customer collaboration, and responding to change.ArtifactsThe tangible and visible items produced during the Scrum process, such as the product backlog, Sprint backlog, and burndown charts.Backlog refinementThe process of reviewing, prioritizing, and breaking down User Stories into tasks.Burndown ChartA visual representation of the remaining work in a Sprint or project, helping the team track progress and identify potential delays.Change ManagementThe structured approach to transitioning individuals, teams, or organizations from a current state to a desired future state while minimizing resistance and maximizing benefits.CoachA Scrum Master's role in guiding and supporting team members, helping them stay on track and maximize their strengths.Daily ScrumA daily meeting where the team discusses progress, plans for the day, and any obstacles.Definition of DoneThe set of criteria that must be met for a product backlog item to be considered complete and potentially releasable. It ensures that the team delivers a high-quality product increment.Empirical Product PlanningA flexible and adaptive approach to planning in complex environments, emphasizing the use of empirical data and feedback to inform decision-making and drive product development. It is based on the principles of empiricism, which prioritize observation, experimentation, and evidence-based learning.EpicsLarger, high-level user stories are further broken down into smaller User Stories.FacilitatorThe Scrum Master's role in leading the Scrum process, planning activities, and providing the team with the necessary tools to complete tasks.IncrementThe sum of all the completed Product Backlog items at the end of a Sprint.IterativeCharacterized by repetition and refinement through multiple cycles or iterations. Kanban BoardsVisual tools used in Agile and Lean methodologies to manage work and track progress using columns and cards.MentorA trusted advisor or guide who provides guidance, knowledge, and support to less-experienced individuals or teams.PersonasFictional biographies of users that include demographic information, usage details, and market research insights.Product BacklogA prioritized list of requirements or features for the product.Project Management MethodologiesDifferent approaches and frameworks are used to plan, execute, and control projects. Examples include Waterfall, Agile, Lean, PRINCE2, and Scrum.Project ManagerA role that focuses on planning and managing the overall project, including budgeting, risk management, and procurement.RoadmapA strategic plan that outlines the purpose and direction of the product.ScopeThe boundaries, objectives, and deliverables of a project or task.Scrum CeremoniesRegularly scheduled events or meetings within the Scrum framework, such as Daily Scrum, Sprint Planning, Demos, and Retrospectives, to facilitate collaboration, planning, and reflection.Scrum EventsThe key meetings and ceremonies within the Scrum framework, such as the daily Scrum, Scrum Increments, and Sprint Retrospective.Scrum FrameworkAn iterative and incremental agile framework for managing complex projects. It emphasizes flexibility, collaboration, and delivering high-value increments.Scrum MasterThe role responsible for facilitating and coaching the Scrum team, removing impediments, and promoting a healthy team environment.Scrum MethodologyThe disciplined approach was followed by the Scrum Master to keep the team focused on creating value and meeting the definition of 'Done.' It involves employing Scrum events and staying within the timebox for events.Scrum processThe framework that provides flexibility and consists of five events (Sprint, Sprint Planning, Daily Scrum, Sprint Review, and Sprint Retrospective) and three artifacts (Product Backlog, Sprint Backlog, and Increment).Scrum ValuesThe core principles and beliefs are the behavior and actions of individuals and teams practicing Scrum. The Scrum Values include commitment, openness, courage, transparency, inspection, and adaptation.Servant LeaderA Scrum Master who serves the organization and the team. They coach team members using team-building techniques, support,and guide distracting team members, and collaborate effectively outside the team.SprintA time-boxed period, typically lasting six weeks, where development work takes place.Sprint BacklogThe list of tasks and user stories that the Scrum team commits to completing during a Sprint.Sprint RetrospectiveA meeting where the team reflects on the functioning of the team, tools used, and status of deliverables.Sprint ReviewAn event where the team and stakeholders review the work completed during the Sprint.Team-centered approachAn approach in Scrum where the focus is on collaboration, cooperation, and shared responsibility among team members. A team-centered approach encourages collective problem-solving and leveraging each other's strengths.User Experience (UX)The overall experience and satisfaction a user has while interacting with a product, system, or service. Persona development contributes to enhancing the user experience by designing products that align with the identified needs and preferences of target users.User StoriesBite-sized requirements or features that deliver value to the end-users.XP (eXtreme Programming)A software development methodology that emphasizes close collaboration, simplicity, frequent feedback, and continuous improvement. In certain scenarios, Scrum can be mixed with XP practices to enhance the development process.
Scrum Master as A Facilitator
Module Introduction and Takeaways 
Module 2 has two lessons that cover key aspects of the Scrum Master role. Lesson one focuses on stakeholder management and the Scrum Master's role as a facilitator, along with creating a working agreement. Lesson two explores maximizing influence with the team, emphasizing the Scrum Master's role as a coach. The module provides practical tools and techniques for effective team management, stakeholder handling, and maintaining team focus during Sprints. 
By the end of this week, you will be able to:
* Identify and classify different strategies to effectively manage stakeholders.
* Analyze and evaluate various situational facilitation techniques to enhance collaboration.
* Create a comprehensive working agreement by outlining its purpose and value.
* Specify and define the acceptance criteria for the team's definition of "done."
* Explain and demonstrate a range of coaching techniques to maximize team effectiveness.
* Recognize instances where coaching is ineffective and adapt the approach accordingly.
* Facilitate a meeting to articulate and emphasize the advantages and benefits of Scrum to stakeholders.
* Apply different retrospective methods to foster continuous improvement and growth.
Engaging Stakeholders 
Welcome to this module on how to engage stakeholders. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to; 
define a stakeholder and their varying degrees of influence and importance, describe the planned and unplanned events to interact with stakeholders, describe how the Scrum Master can coach and facilitate stakeholders, describe how Scrum Masters can educate the stakeholders, and explain stakeholder management. 
A stakeholder is someone who has a stake in the effort of a project or product. 
They are anyone who has an impact or will be impacted by the project or product. 
Also, they have varying degrees of influence and importance. 
The Scrum Master engages with stakeholders at various points of the project, some planned, some unplanned. 
The influential stakeholders are the ones you may need to pay the most attention to. 
The relationship grid matrix shows how important and influential a stakeholder is. 
Stakeholders can either be low influence and low importance, high influence and low importance, low influence and high importance, or high importance and high influence. 
Depending on which quadrant a stakeholder falls, you can determine how they are to be handled. 
High importance and high influence require more involvement. 
Let's understand when you as a Scrum Master can engage the stakeholders. 
An example of a planned event where you will interact with the stakeholders is the sprint review. 
As the team member responsible for the sprint review process, stakeholders will critique and submit useful feedback. 
An unplanned event might be at any point during the sprint or when the Scrum Master is coaching the organization on the Scrum framework. 
In all situations, the Scrum Master must know how to handle difficult conversations. 
They should inspire stakeholders to think in terms of adding value. 
As a Scrum Master, you are a coach and a facilitator. 
In these roles, you explain, correct, and add technical expertise to the process. 
This might include explaining why Scrum is appropriate for a project. 
If a stakeholder doubts the efficacy of the Scrum framework, it may be because they do not understand it. 
As the expert, you need to inform them about the advantages of using Scrum. 
This coaching may require various levels of engagement depending on the stakeholders' importance and influence. 
You should be prepared to offer explanations on how the framework is used and how it offers a solution to a current problem or project. 
For example, some stakeholders may understand the Scrum Master to be a project manager and question why the team plans for each sprint. 
Just because the organization uses Scrum, does not guarantee a comprehensive understanding across the enterprise. 
Your job is to educate them. 
As a Scrum Master, you should explain using the business case. 
Explain what the organization should expect to gain by using Scrum and the value it delivers. 
Scrum is the best way to take undefined requirements and refine them. 
Then Scrum can help you prioritize those requirements, how teams can be more productive 
through ownership and creativity, and the fact that there are regular reviews and delivery throughout the process. 
Once you get them into buying into your methodology, the Scrum Master will continue to 
guide them on their journey as a coach. 
Remember, you are the expert in the methodology. 
You would teach stakeholders how the reviews are done and instill discipline to do regular rigorous reviews and keep everyone in the appropriate timebox. 
The key takeaway is that working with stakeholders does not start with these events. 
Educating stakeholders should start in advance. 
The Scrum Master should make the first move. 
Getting buy-in at the sprint review is not going to keep them engaged. 
If they're confused on how the process works, they will only be confused and distracted from the value-add exercise that you're trying to complete. 
You need to manage the stakeholders once you know who your stakeholders are. 
Stakeholder management is all about managing influence. 
The more influence, the more a Scrum Master should take an interest in the individual. 
A stakeholder with tremendous influence can easily challenge the team's process if it's not under control. 
A Scrum Master should have a stakeholder management plan. 
First, identify who they are, and then you need to figure out how to manage them. 
You need to determine how to communicate with them. 
For stakeholders who lack an understanding of Scrum, you must educate them in the methodology. 
The Scrum Master should understand the organizational strategy and what needs to be accomplished. 
They should share the vision with stakeholders and find ways for the stakeholder to add value or support the vision. 

In this video, you learned that a stakeholder is somebody who has a stake and has an impact or will be impacted by the project or product. 
Stakeholders have varying degrees of influence and importance. 
Coaching and educating stakeholders may require various levels of engagement depending on the stakeholders' importance and influence. 
For stakeholders who lack an understanding of Scrum, you must educate them in the methodology. 

Analysing Situational Facilitation Techniques to Maximize Collaboration and Various Coaching Techniques 
The "One size fits all" approach to leadership lacks the flexibility required to address different situations that the Scrum Master encounters. Situational leadership helps the Scrum Master decide how to handle various circumstances depending on the team's preparation, conditions that can unsettle the team, the most appropriate plan to address the situation, and the best opportunity for the team to grow. 
The different approaches that the Scrum Masters use are Directing, Coaching, Supporting, and Delegating. You, as a Scrum Master, should apply direct leadership to situations that require the most involvement. You can apply coaching to scenarios where the team needs help, instruction, facilitation, or ways to solve problems. You use supporting when highly capable team members require encouragement, removal of roadblocks, or help to make decisions. Finally, delegating represents the lowest level of involvement from the Scrum Master, where the teams demonstrate independence with minimum involvement in the process from leaders.
Directing
New teams generally have inexperienced team members who lack independence. This is characterized by a lack of training and confidence, unfamiliarity with the process, difficulty collaborating, and so on. This demonstrates immaturity because the team needs clear and concise instructions on how to proceed forward. Scrum Masters should apply direct leadership in such situations. You will need to direct the team on what needs to be done and provide them with clear instructions. 
These employees struggle to be creative, are nervous about making decisions, do not feel empowered, and feel more comfortable being a follower when you assign work to them. The goal is to be successful, so whether you are directing or delegating, use the most appropriate leadership style. 
For example, a manager hires Greg as an engineer and assigns him to a team. Greg comes from a company where engineers are expected to work alone and follow instructions containing every detail about the assignment. He had little room for error in his previous role if he followed the directions. Greg comes into this job expecting the same work environment. However, on his first Sprint, he experiences frustration because he feels lost working in a team where creativity makes the team successful. He approaches the Scrum Master, asks for exact details on what to program, and requires help in collaboration. In this situation, the Scrum Master should apply a directing leadership style. Brad, the Scrum Master, may provide exact directions on how to move forward. As Greg matures and learns to work independently, the Scrum Master can backoff, and creativity can replace specific directions.
Good Scrum Masters know when to apply directing leadership for the team's benefit. Imagine a development project with high stakes and little room for error. Even falling behind by a single day will delay the team and make the customer unhappy. In such a case, if the team has a good roadmap and well-defined tasks, directing leadership is the most appropriate type of leadership to apply. 
Other examples are in an application where safety is a factor, like a vehicle brake system or medicine dosage levels, the team should limit creativity and follow specifications. In such situations, you must have a specific approach with little variation. 
Coaching
As the team matures, you can coach them. At this point, the Scrum Master may back off and lead the team to arrive at the answers independently. The team will mature with training, team building, a deeper understanding of the Scrum framework, and experience working as a team. When a team reaches this level, they still require guidance and run the risk of failure without coaching. As a Scrum Master, expect to spend most of your time in this realm. 
You need to be on high alert for situations that require coaching because every situation is different, team members change, and the content and complexity of a project may change. You should use coaching leadership for teaching, gaining support for the process, challenging the team to think creatively, and staying focused on the goals. 
As an example, Chen, a developer, is skeptical about the Scrum Process and prefers to work independently. He checks in with team members, but during the stand-up meetings, the Scrum Master, Matt, notices clear discomfort while sharing. Matt meets with Chen one-on-one to share the benefits of working as a team and how his colleagues can relieve some of the burdens of a few tasks. Instead of telling Chen how it will be, Matt lets him arrive at this conclusion by sharing different scenarios where it would help to have a team member work with him, saving time. 
In another example, Michael, a Scrum Master, supports the software development group. The company knows about Scrum but has been on the fence about adopting a new approach to project management. The vice president of marketing, Sam, approaches Michael and asks him to hold a training session for the developers in his department. The developers are excited about the incremental approach to development. As the first Sprint kicks off, Michael helps the team mature in understanding collaboration, Scrum Values, and Sprint planning. 
Rodrigo works for an oil company that uses Scrum to develop applications in its IT division. Lately, leadership has debated what the Scrum methodology should look like in their organization. Rodrigo is asked to lead the effort to coach the groups on their Scrum journey. As a result, there is clarity in the team, and they feel more satisfied and finish their work efficiently. 
Supporting
In a team full of confident and competent members who understand the Scrum process and collaborate well, the Scrum Master may adjust their level of involvement and monitor the team for coaching opportunities. Nevertheless, you will experience situations that require direct involvement. Team members are human and experience burnout, lose sight of the objectives, fail to see how their decisions impact others' work, or fail to align with the team agreement. In such cases, the Scrum Master turns to a supporting type of leadership. Supporting leadership works when a team member can do the job but may need training, tools, or encouragement. 
For instance, Jose, an engineer, is creating a continuous improvement portal for a company. The Product Owner has been very specific about what he wants. Jose indicates that he could use some training in continuous improvement to understand better what the client wants. Felipe, the Scrum Master, brings in a Lean Coach to help fill in some of the process details. 
In another instance, Andy has lost sight of what he is doing and is afraid to decide on some code he is writing. He comes to the Scrum Master and asks for help. The Scrum Master enrolls Andy in a 2-day leadership and decision-making workshop. Andy returns from the workshop recharged and equipped with the tools to make the next decision.
Delegating
You should employ delegating leadership when your team demonstrates capability. Sometimes, directing, supporting, or coaching may cause the team to step back in their development. Delegating leadership style is applied in teams that have reached the highest level of maturity. Be mindful that just because a team has reached a high level of maturity, you must still evaluate each scenario.
For example, Paul is the best developer in the company, and the team enjoys working with him. The Scrum Master has the highest confidence in his ability and thinks the best way of leading him is to let him get to work. For the first two weeks of the Sprint, he has made significant progress and required very little support from the Scrum Master, who decides to check in with Paul at the stand-ups every Friday. Thus, delegation worked in this case.
