Working Agreement 
Welcome to this video on working agreement. 
Our expert, Matt Verstall, will guide you through the process of creating a working agreement. 
He will cover essential aspects such as the necessary questions to ask, guidelines to follow, and inclusion of goals and objectives within the agreement. 
Today we are going to talk about the working agreement. 
The working agreement is a critical agreement for the team. 
Essentially, it is the contract. 
It is how we decide as a team that we are going to work together. 
It is the expectation that everybody has that this is how we are going to treat each other, 
this is how we are going to handle certain situations, this is how we are going to function as a team. 
They matter in the long run because inevitably, things come up as you're working as a team. 
Inevitably, you have conflicts. 
Inevitably, you have disagreements, and there are challenges that come along. 
The working agreement should be that article, that document that you can go back to, 
and help each other figure out how to handle the situation. 
It saves you a lot of trouble. 
Because instead of trying to figure out a process to use, and later on spending a lot of time and waste on something that could have been resolved simply by referring back to the document. 
It can help you become more efficient as a team. 
It can help you concentrate on doing the actual work as opposed to going to the Scrum Master and trying to figure out how to handle a situation. 
Ultimately, it makes you a better team. 
These activities should be done in a time-boxed format where the team gets together in meets and figures out the working agreement. 
You can call it a meeting, you can call it a task session, workshop, working meeting, 
however you want to refer to it. 
Now, you want to try and capture as much as you can in this working agreement 
and anticipate things and some basic guidelines that you will inevitably face as a team. 
Now, these should be formal, but at the same time they should be relaxed and you should be able to work on it as a team. 
After all, you are setting the stage and setting the expectation how these things go. 
The Scrum Master will facilitate, he should introduce what the team agreement is, what the working agreement is to the team and explain how this process works and how the process will be put together. 
Essentially, you're asking the question, how do we want to treat each other? 
If you can answer that question, then a lot of the content of the working agreement is in that. 
It starts with that high-level question and then you can break things down further as you go along. 
You want to ask each other things like, how long do we want to take to do certain tasks? 
When should we be able to contact each other? 
How much time should we be putting into communicating as a team? 
What's the expectation for coming to meetings? 
If we get into a conflict, how do we react? 
Who do we go to? 
Who helps us with those questions? 
What kind of tools can we use? 
Who is considered a teammate? 
Who is not considered a teammate? 
For example, stakeholders. 
What time do we want to meet every day to do our daily scrums or our stand-up meetings? 
It's called an agreement because everybody should agree to it. 
It's not something that is one-dimensional. 
It is an agreement for the whole team. 
Now, what are some of the common guidelines? 
What are the goals and objectives? 
How do we maintain the agreement? 
For example, do we meet weekly and review this? 
Do we meet monthly? Do we meet before a sprint? 
Do we meet after a sprint? 
Do we talk about it in our retrospective? 
All these things should be decided in the agreement. 
But there should be some element that the Scrum Master does to work with the team to keep it going. 
Now, there's no rights and wrongs, none at all. 
You can do it however you want. 
It's your agreement, it's your team. 
Nobody can tell you how to run the team. 
But it's intended to build that productivity. 
Here's some things that I like to consider, is how do we treat each other? 
How do we solve problems? 
How do we expect each other to behave? 
How do we handle change? 
How do we know we're done? 
Let's all agree on that. 
I have a few lines here now, these are typically much larger, but we're not going to read through an entire agreement. 
But typically, we state how we're going to treat each other at all times. 
We explain how our problems are solved, and we tell each other what support means. 
In this case, we need each other. 
Developers need testers, tester needs QA, 
and we all play a role in the success of the team. 
We have that mindset and we stay on schedule. 
For communication, we over-communicate. 
This is a very simple version of a team agreement, but it can be used to give you an idea of what these look like. It's this simple? 
Yes, it should be simple. 
It should be straightforward. 
It does not need to be a very technical document, but it should be very straightforward 
so that everybody understands and everybody remembers what we agreed on as a team and how we approach the work that we do. 

Acceptance Criteria and the Concept of "Done" 
The team concludes their Sprint work by achieving Increments. An increment represents a tangible outcome adding value to the project. It is an intermediate and deliverable portion of work completed during Sprints. Consensus within the team is crucial to agree on what constitutes completion. Otherwise, it would be impossible to conclude the Sprint successfully. Teams must work on meeting customers' acceptance criteria before releasing Increments.
The team agrees on the definition of "Done" at the beginning of a sprint. The team uses this definition as a guideline to determine when the work is complete and can turn it into an Increment. The first step in determining the acceptance criteria is the requirements. The criteria depend on the functionality required. Usually, it aligns with business requirements or specific functions. 
The definition of "Done" goes beyond acceptance criteria by encompassing a comprehensive list of activities and internal tasks. It includes both customer requirements and internal needs. It is important to remember that the team defines the definition of "Done," ensuring a shared understanding of what makes the work complete.
The team follows a development process that involves reviewing the User Story and establishing criteria to add value for the customer. While the User Story identifies the value, the definition of "Done" outlines the steps to achieve it. This well-defined set of criteria is tangible and concrete evidence that the deliverable meets the customer's needs.
It is essential to have documentation for measuring and tracking completion to ensure agreement within the team. A practical method is to use a checklist that reflects the User Story. It is important to note that the definition of "Done" differs from acceptance criteria. The acceptance criteria focuses on specific attributes and characteristics, such as titanium, waterproofing, weight, greenness, or labeling.
In a development scenario, a definition of "Done" checklist may include the following:
Definition of Done ChecklistTeam:Product:Backlog Requirement:Testing completeFocus group testCode reviewed by Developers A and BQA Test 1QA Test 2Meets acceptance criteriaFunctionality checkIn conclusion, the concept of "Done" plays a vital role in Agile development. By achieving Increments and adhering to the definition of Done, teams ensure that the work they deliver adds value to the project and meets customer expectations.

Managing the Daily Stand-up Meeting 
Welcome to this module on managing the daily stand-up meeting. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to describe a daily standup meeting, explain the role of the Scrum Master, and managing the daily stand-up meeting. 
The daily stand-up meeting is a brief structured meeting held every day within the sprint where the team gathers to discuss ongoing work. 
The purpose of conducting this meeting as to save time and maintain brevity. 
As the name indicates, the participants remain standing throughout the meeting, 
lasting no more than 15 minutes. 
The Scrum Master poses three fundamental questions during this meeting. 
The first question is, what did you work on yesterday? 
The second question is, what are you working on today? 
The third question is, what are your impediments? 
Adapting questions is possible, but the underlying concept remains the same. 
Understanding progress, the completed tasks, and the challenges of the team. 
Although these meetings follow a structured format, they also allow room for impromptu questions, comments, concerns, feedback, and additional contributions. 
There should be enough time for each member to actively engage in the discussion. 
The initial question during the daily standup is, what did you work on yesterday? 
This query allows team members to discuss their completed tasks and 
the challenges they encountered, providing crucial information for the scrum master and the team. 
It can also serve as a moment of recognition. 
The Scrum Master needs to listen carefully to what the team members contribute. 
It is an opportunity for the Scrum Master to discover any sense of impediments, especially if the team member does not say it explicitly. 
It is also an ideal time for the team to seek clarifications or post questions to their colleagues regarding requirements. 
The reporting team members should be comprehensive, while the Scrum Master should actively extract information and asked unnecessary questions. 
It is important to remember that the Scrum Master's responsibility is to coach and facilitate. 
In the case of a hesitant or reserved team member, the Scrum Master should provide guidance and lead them. 
The team members could be afraid or embarrassed to disclose critical roadblocks, 
resulting in the loss of valuable information about the project. 
It is the Scrum Master's role to develop a collaborative environment, 
in which team members feel comfortable sharing their progress. 
The second question examines what the team will be working on today, 
in order to assess their progress and ensure they understand what they need to do. 
For instance, if a developer Sally, is facing a recurring issue, your role as a scrum master is 
to ask insightful questions to understand the underlying reasons and help find the solution. 
This could involve seeking assistance from other team members or addressing the matter offline. 
The purpose of this meeting extends beyond simple information gathering and task reporting. 
It is a part of the team agreement to ask for assistance when necessary. 
Do not hesitate to inquire if a team member needs help. 
A collaborative team will be willing to lend a hand as the team shares their updates, 
pay attention to the signs of frustration, hesitation, or confusion. 
As a coach picking up on these cues from their remarks is important. 
Another opportunity with this question is ensuring the team sticks to the plan. 
If you hear something that deviates from the sprint plan or is not related to the deliverables, 
it becomes crucial to step in and redirect the team. 
For example, if a developer, John, states that he will only be available for the stand-up meeting, despite having a project assigned for the next three days, this is where your role as 
the Scrum Master comes into play. 
The primary responsibility for a scrum master is removing roadblocks and clearing the path for the team to complete sprints. 
During the daily standup, the third and final question provides an opportunity for an open and honest conversation about what is not working, 
but the struggles are, and what is getting in the way of completing the work. 
Remember, there is no shame in asking these questions as everyone shares the same goal. 
Collaboration is a fundamental agreement within the Scrum team. 
Your role as the scrum master is to guide and facilitate these discussions, fostering an environment where team members feel comfortable sharing their experiences. 
It may be necessary to ask more leading questions to gain a comprehensive understanding of the roadblocks. 
In some cases, team members may not have the chance to interact face-to-face, 
making this daily stand-up the only opportunity to report any issues. 
It is crucial to be attentive and responsive to the team's responses and the daily stand-up, 
taking appropriate action as required. 
Additionally, as the Scrum Master, you are responsible for providing feedback to the group 
regarding any follow-up actions taken based on the meeting discussions. 

In this video, you learned that the daily stand-up meeting is a brief structured meeting. 
The Scrum Master asks three important questions in the daily stand-up meeting. 
The first question allows the team members to discuss their completed tasks and challenges they encountered. 
The second question provides an opportunity to assess the team's progress. 
The third question provides an opportunity for open conversation about achievements, 
challenges, and impediments among team members. 
Focusing the Team
Welcome to this module on how to maintain focus of the team. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to describe the causes for loss of focus in the team. 
Describe the approaches to keep the team focused. 
Describe the tools to keep track of the pace of work. 
Teams lose focus when they lack direction, are distracted, lose sight of goals, and misunderstand the process. 
They also lose concentration when they use an underdeveloped plan or mismanage change. 
Other causes of losing focus are poorly planned sprints, weak retrospectives with concrete action plans, and uncontrolled conflict within the team. 
The best approach to ensure the team is focused is to build a strong foundation in the beginning. 
You should start with an agreement about what you expect from the team and what the team expects from their colleagues. 
A successful team would agree to be open and honest about asking for help when required from each other. 
Let's see an example. 
Sheila is an expert programmer but lacks an understanding of test requirements. 
As a result, she worries about providing adequate specs for testing in the correct format. 
As the Scrum Master, you realize in a daily standup that she is behind schedule because of her inadequacy. 
Although she knew her colleague Bob could help her, she hesitated to ask him directly. 
This situation would have never cropped up if the team had a prior agreement of being open about helping each other out in case of any problems. 
In another scenario, a team loses focus because they disregard on how they should define done, and what should be the length of the sprint for the work at hand. 
Note that it helps to conduct a periodic review of the plans with the team to keep the team focused on the goal. 
Another foundational area that helps in ensuring the team is focused is sprint planning. 
A weak sprint plan can cause the team to lose sight of their objective by creating confusion. 
Due to this confusion, the team starts guessing their individual tasks in the project. 
This guesswork can eventually lead to frustration among the team members. 
They might even feel like they have been misguided. 
To avoid such an upheaval in the team, you should get a consensus from the team on your sprint plan. 
Take care that no one member dominates the line of action that could be questioned later. 
Let's see an example. 
In a team, all members are aware that Randy, one of the team, is an experienced developer. 
He dominates all sprint planning sessions and has a readymade plan to achieve the goal. 
The rest of the team are inhibited in expressing their opinions. 
Two weeks into the sprint, one of your other developers, Tanya, becomes frustrated because she feels Randy's approach is not so fruitful, and there are other ways to reach the goal. 
Thus, it is important to get all of the team members involved in the sprint planning, and make plans that convince everyone on the team. 
Teams must approach their work with a continuous improvement mentality. 
For Scrum teams, retrospectives provide an opportunity for the team to reflect on their work and improve for the next iteration. 
Retrospectives are a great opportunity for the team to adjust their methods and reset expectations. 
Going back to the previous example in the retrospective, Tanya recommends that each team member should have an equal number of contributions during the sprint plan, and perhaps a voting system may ensure that each person has input into the sprint plan. 
Next, you will require a well-developed definition of done. 
A consensus on the team on how to define this will eliminate ambiguity at the end of the sprint. 
If the team has definite and concrete guidelines, they can focus on their work, check off a list, and move forward. 
Change is another area the Scrum Master needs to control in conjunction with the product owner. 
As the work of the sprint commences, it is natural that ideas emerge on how to make things better. 
Hence, change happens. 
As humans, we tend to focus narrowly on what we are doing and miss the big picture. 
As a Scrum Master, you must keep the team focused on what the product owner and 
the team agreed on during planning. 
Having these requirements thoroughly defined will keep the team synchronized in their efforts. 
You may also need to listen carefully during daily scrums for signs that the team is getting off track. 
Ask questions and probe these scenarios so you know what is happening, and give the other team members a chance to respond. 
The intention of the public conversation is not to publicly shame or point fingers, but rather to make the team accountable for each other. 
In a rush to complete the project, a team might run the risk of omitting important details. 
The use of tools like burndown charts and other metrics can keep a pulse on completion. 
Also, the Scrum Master must talk to the team about their pace of work and review this in the retrospectives. 
If there is an issue during the last sprint, it should be dealt with in the subsequent plans. 
During the sprint plan, ensure that the team has planned for a reasonable amount of work. 
Once the plan is complete, changing the organization's expectations will be difficult. 
Constantly remind the team of their goals. 
A powerful way to keep the team focused is reviewing the goals as a group. 
Besides posting the goals on a board, an active exchange between team members will be a constant reminder of what they are required to complete. 
You as the Scrum Master and your team must have effective communication. 
You must create opportunities for teams to communicate, not just in meetings. 
A truly collaborative environment means they are working together to get to the definition of done, you need to contain groupthink. 
In some situations, the team has difficulty thinking for themselves. 
This is human nature. 
Nobody wants to be left out. 
The pressure of other people on the team taking a position can be overwhelming. 
Failing to agree with others may leave you feeling like you do not belong or are going against the team. 
The Scrum Master as coach must control it. 
The way to overcome this is by encouraging collaboration and creativity and upholding Scrum values so team members can express themselves. 
If your team feels confident in their ability to share and be honest, it will reduce the amount of groupthink. 
It is fine for employees to disagree with each other as long as it can be used to support the team. 
Coaches facilitate the team to see each other's viewpoints. 
Next, keep the team's eye on the prize. 
You must constantly recharge the team by focusing on what the team needs. 

In this video, you learned that, teams lose focus when they lack direction, are distracted, lose sight of goals, and misunderstand the process. 
A successful team would agree to be open and honest about asking for help when required from each other. 
It is important to get all the team members involved in the sprint planning, and make plans that convince everyone on the team. 
For Scrum Teams, retrospectives provide an opportunity for the team to reflect on their work and improve for the next iteration. 
As a Scrum Master, you need to keep your team focused on what the product owner and the team agreed on during planning. 
The Scrum Master as coach must encourage collaboration and creativity and uphold Scrum values. 

Scrum Master as a Coach
Welcome to Scrum Master as a Coach. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to explain Scrum Master's role as a coach, describe the goals of the Scrum Master as a coach, describe the elements of coaching, describe the characteristics of a good coach, and recognize the warning signs of trouble in the team. 
The concept of a Scrum Master as coach adds a new dimension to the leadership role of a Scrum Master. 
This role is unlike a managing role. 
Managing is task-oriented while coaching concerns people. 
Coaching is training people to do better as individuals and as a part of a team. 
As per Adkins, it goes beyond doing and as being. 
A coach's influence reaches further than the actions they take, but extends to their presence. 
In other words, a coach does more than teach scrum or help others solve problems, they live the scrum values. 
This attitude of the Scrum Master inspires the team to live the same scrum values. 
Eventually, this approach leads the team to achieve success. 
In some cases, to reach the desired goal you as the scrum master may be required to support the team when they are confused or require help solving a problem. 
You can give your support by mentoring the team so that they find the answers. 
At the same time, you can help the organization's effort to build the scrum framework. 
As a coach, you want to help your team improve and overcome obstacles. 
Additionally, you want the team to deliver quality efficiently. 
Quality is delivering what is important to the customer. 
You should guide the team to solve any problems they encounter while working to provide a quality product to the customer. 
Teams require coaching for various reasons. 
In some cases, the team may feel drained after a long project. 
The coach helps by preventing them from losing sight of their end objective. 
If the team loses sight, they might rush to finish a project, resulting in missed requirements or unsatisfactory deliverables. 
Coaching also aims to help the team make better choices. 
These choices should be collaborative and get them to the definition of done. 
The wheel contains the elements of a coach. 
The wheel can be seen as transformational, but notice it is a circle, implying that it's ongoing. 
Coaches are lifelong learners. 
Coaches lead their teams to do better work. 
They transform the team and the organization by teaching the principles of scrum and its values. 
They are experts in the process and serve the teams as leaders by supporting them so they're successful. 
Coaching is also a part of the creative process and helps the team to be creative. 
Guided learning is building competence. 
The outer wheel shows the further breaking down of the competencies. 
It focuses on more specific areas of how the competencies are applied. 
The Scrum Master coach enhances the team's ability to fulfil their mission. 
Even if you plan effectively, circumstances change, unexpected issues emerge and anyone on the team can make mistakes. 
You may need to inspire the team to change their thinking based on the circumstances. 
For example, if a team member is good at testing code, his services may include quality assurance rather than pass the code to a QA team. 
You must change the team's mindset as they expect the QA to test the code. 
The coach may need to draw the team member to view things differently. 
In another example, a team member, Tony, who has only worked in traditional project management scenarios, questions how effective a sprint can be if you present a plan. 
He believes it is a waste of time. 
Jeff, the scrum master, keeps aside some time to explain the effectiveness of the sprint plan to Tony. 
From past experience, Tony believes they should work at a stretch on the project and then submit the final report. 
Jeff then shares with him why it is important to submit incremental work on the project to the customer so that they can deliver quality at the end. 
Since coaches deal with people, they should always be neutral. 
You as a coach must avoid taking sides with coaching. 
When the team has an issue amongst themselves, your job as the coach is to help them resolve it. 
Providing direction will only make the issue worse, rather equip them to resolve the problem. 
As mentioned earlier, scrum masters lead by example. 
Leading by example is showing how to conclude. 
This is how compassionate coaches practice empathy and avoid treating people as resources. 
Coaches know how to communicate. 
Communication is verbal and nonverbal. 
The nonverbal type is where team members observe you acting like a servant leader. 
For example, when the team watches you lead by example, they learn by observation. 
As a more concrete example, a team member who previously worked in a top-down, 
hierarchical structure might be inspired to creativity when they feel others hear their voice and their opinion matters. 
Coaching can sometimes be challenging, and you must look for warning signs. 
For example, a team member who constantly dominates meetings and has strong opinions can derail the team by causing frustration or groupthink, upsetting the team's balance. 
Outside influence is another area that could challenge the team's ability to stay 
focused. 
Other warning signs include fatigue, frustration, and a team member who was collaborative but now has difficulty working within the team. 
Complaining is another obvious sign that something's wrong, 
if the group fails to complete their work and avoids discussion on roadblocks, be aware that there is an issue. 
In this video, you learned that coaching is meant to improve people and as a part of the team. 
As a coach, you want to help your team overcome obstacles. 
The coaching wheel includes the transformational elements of a coach coaches inspire, are neutral, and communicate verbally and nonverbally. 
A coach should recognize warning signs of upsets in the team. 

Expert Viewpoints: The Scrum Master as a Facilitator 

Welcome to Expert Viewpoints, the Scrum Master as a Facilitator. 
In this video, experts share their tips for effective facilitation and describe some common facilitation pitfalls to avoid. 
What are some tips you can share for a Scrum Master to be an effective facilitator? 
What are some common facilitation pitfalls to avoid? 
>> Some tips on facilitation, stay neutral and unbiased. 
Your job is to keep the team focused and engaged in the meeting. 
Make sure you're prepared, the agenda, understanding the purpose of the meeting, 
the right people are there, you're prepared with any materials or references that are needed. 
At the end of the meeting, summarize what was covered and what the next action items should be. 
One of the main pitfalls I've run into is letting the meeting get completely off track by allowing the conversation to veer away from the meeting purpose. 
You'll need to step in at that point, note the discussion, and put it in the parking lot. 
That way you can come back to it as you've completed the purpose of the meeting and gotten to the outcome that you need to. 
>> Some tips that I can share for a Scrum Master to be an effective facilitator include make sure you prepare and plan the agenda and desired outcomes and necessary materials needed. 
Make sure that there are clear expectations regarding the purpose, the objective, and desired outcomes of the meeting, and clearly communicate that. 
Encourage active participation and use visual aids to capture key points. 
What are some common facilitation pitfalls to avoid? 
So when facilitating meetings, some of the common pitfalls you would want to avoid is dominating the discussion. 
Don't dominate the discussion, allowing side conversations or off-topic discussions. 
Your role as facilitator is to keep the meeting on task. 
Ignoring quiet and introverted team members, make sure everybody participates. 
Ignoring people who are quiet is a common mistake people make. 
Rushing through activities and not allowing enough time, and neglecting to follow up on action items. 
>> It means stick to the process and protect your team like your flock. 
Because a facilitator will hold people accountable and hold people accountable to the process and they stick to the plan and they follow it. 
And an example of that would be a team who always has one or two people who do not want to follow the process or try to get others to lead them away from the process. 
The facilitator side of the Scrum Master can really swoop in and guide people back to the right path. 
And that may be through some questioning, never embarrassing people or putting people on trial or 
something like that, but rather guiding and correcting and helping them understand how things really are in the Scrum world. 
A lot of times facilitators, especially people new at it, will become frustrated as well if the team doesn't follow them, follow their lead. 
So, you can end up in a situation where you're embarrassing somebody. 
Totally want to avoid that because that will only get the team to lose respect for you, specially that person, but the others will see that too. 
Again, we need to lead by example as Scrum Masters. 
So we always show people a better way and help them to get there on their own. 
And as a Scrum team, we always make an agreement at the beginning that this is how the team is going to operate. 
So we try to get people to go back to that direction. 
>> To be an effective facilitator as a Scrum master, focus on creating a safe and inclusive environment, 
preparing and structuring sessions with clear objectives, fostering active listening and collaborative decision making. 
Remain neutral, manage time effectively, and seek feedback for continuous improvement. 
Some pitfalls to avoid are, dominating the conversation, ineffective time management, failure to create inclusive environment, and imposing your personal opinion. 

>> First of all, try to convey information in a simple and concise manner. 
In addition, try to show energy and passion for the subject you are facilitating. 
This energy and passion can become contagious. 
Secondly, watch the room. 
Pick up on small gestures and nonverbals that can help you better understand people's reactions and people's feelings. 
Read the room, if you will. 
Finally, try to remain neutral and treat participants equally. 
Ask rather than tell. 
First of all, lack of flexibility. 
Don't be dead set in your plan. 
Try to tailor your facilitation to meet the situation. 
And if the situation is changing, be adaptable and adjust. 
Secondly, be yourself. 
Be authentic. 
Don't try to be someone that you are not. 
If you try to portray yourself as someone you are not, you will come off as disingenuous, lose credibility, and, potentially, lose trust. 
Finally, be patient. 
Try your best not to let your emotions and frustrations show. 
If you do, then you're going to once again, lose your credibility as a facilitator. 
And in all likelihood, the outcomes you were hoping to achieve will not occur. 

>> When you join a new team as a Scrum Master, one of the first things you should do is book one on one meetings with your entire team. 
This is going to enable you to understand the workflow, and now how the team functions. 
Also, make sure that you book one on ones with your product owner and also your lead developer weekly. 
That way, you both are talking about what's happening in the team, understanding the problems that the team is facing, and also finding a way to progress through those problems. 
So that at the end of the day, you're meeting the customer needs and wishes, the end goal that the entire team has. 
Don't be that I know it all Scrum master. 
Don't assume for your team. 
Don't prevent them from talking. 
Allow the team to express themselves. 
Basically, encourage the five values of Scrum, which are courage, focus, openness, respect and commitment. 

The Sprint Retrospective 
Welcome to the Sprint Retrospective. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to explain the need for sprint retrospective, identify the focus of retrospective, define the objectives of a retrospective, define the methods and 
processes that are used in a retrospective, and describe how to overcome challenges while running a retrospective. 
Once the sprint concludes, it's time to reflect on the process and take the opportunity to refine methods for the next iteration. 
The sprint retrospective is one of the scrum events. 
These events are conducted as meetings and should have the entire group present. 
The purpose of the retrospective is continuous improvement. 
The team conducts retrospectives so they improve the next sprint. 
Consider it a planning session. 
The team decides what to analyze with the intent of acting. 
Nevertheless, there are no requirements to make it a formal meeting. 
Some organizations conduct this in a very relaxed environment. 
The key is to make it an event that inspires the team to be creative. 
Snacks, lunch, refreshments, and a relaxing location offsite are encouraged. 
A retrospective can focus on areas identified as a weakness, opportunity, or need, not just within the sprint, but certain elements of the team's work. 
For example, an area of focus could be the meetings. 
The retrospectives should be informative and value-added rather than punitive. 
It allows the team to assess how they complete the work. 
After a game, the team would debrief with the coach. 
The intent of this meeting would be to play better in the next game. 
They may watch films or plays and look for technical inconsistencies, 
lost opportunities, and ways to work as a team. 
There will always be some highs, lows and things they can do better. 
Out of this exercise, the team makes a real plan for improvement. 
As a rule of thumb, scrum master should limit retrospective meetings to a timebox 
of an hour for each week of the sprint. 
For example, if the team finishes a six-week sprint, the retrospective can last up to six hours. 
The six hours include all of the conversations and creating the improvement plan. 
The objectives of the exercise determine a good retrospective. 
The team will struggle to focus if you hold a retrospective with no goals. 
The objectives of the retrospective will drive the conversation into actionable items. 
The team might want to dive deep into how they collaborated, their approach to work, 
the team environment, and what was missing. 
Alternatively, your retrospective can be about one issue. 
For example, if the team struggled during the sprint to click as a team, the scrum master might lead the team to focus on team-building. 
It could even start as a question to the team, like, what can we improve? 
There are various ways to conduct a retrospective, but perhaps simple is the best way. 
Many teams use templates and sticky notes on the wall, meet remotely, or gather if possible. 
A simple template could be like the one below. 


Analysing your thoughts and thinking methodically to develop an action plan is important. 
The template below starts with objectives, then probes successes and failures. 
It allows the team to question what they think can be done better, encourage everyone to participate and discuss each item the team brings up. 
You may want to have a brainstorming session and allow the team to develop their ideas before formally putting the template together. 
This way you avoid groupthinking and discourage teammates from being shy. 
Nominal group technique or affinity diagrams can help the team organize their thoughts. 
Also, encourage the team to prepare and remind them to think about what bothers them before the event. 
Running a retrospective is not always easy. 
The team may be exhausted, discouraged, uninterested, busy with other things or not completely understand the process. 
Your job as a coach is critical when you run into these challenges. 
The goal is to get everyone to participate, be honest, and focus on improvement. 
Here are some ways to overcome the challenges. 
First, as a scrum master, be honest. 
Create an environment where the team feels they can be honest. 
That may start with acknowledging and recognizing the team for their hard work. 
Scrum retrospectives should not focus on calling out individuals for shortcomings. 
Rather, it's a time to reflect and be honest. 
Break the ice by recalling that they just spent the last 6-8 weeks engaged in focused work. 
Remind the team why they are there to go through the exercise and how it will benefit them. 
Could you encourage them to have fun and be open? 
If they're uninterested, it may be because they do not understand the purpose of the retrospective. 
You can help them describe what was important to them as they reflect. 
It would help if you led them with questions that inspire thought but avoided giving them the answer. 
Finally, make things actionable. 
The retrospective will be fruitless if the session turns into abstract thoughts without action. 
Stick to actionable items that the team agrees are valuable. 

In this video, you learned that retrospectives focus on improvement for the next sprint, 
our retrospective can focus on any area identified as a weakness, opportunity or need in the sprint or team's work, the objectives of the retrospective will drive the conversation into actionable items, many teams use templates and sticky notes on the wall, meet remotely or gather if possible, scrum retrospectives is a time to reflect and be honest as a scrum master. 

Module 2 Summary: Scrum Master as a Facilitator and a Coach
Congratulations! You have completed this module. In this module, you learned that:
* A stakeholder is someone who has a stake and has an impact or will be impacted by the project or product.
* Coaching and educating stakeholders may require various levels of engagement depending on the stakeholders' importance and influence.
* The working agreement serves as a reference point for handling conflicts, disagreements, and challenges that may arise during teamwork.
* By achieving Increments and adhering to the definition of Done, teams ensure that the work they deliver adds value to the project and meets customer expectations.
* The Scrum Master asks three important questions in the daily stand-up meeting:
* The first question allows team members to discuss their completed tasks and the challenges they encountered.
* The second question provides an opportunity to assess the team's progress.
* The third question provides an opportunity for open conversation about achievements, challenges, and impediments among team members.
* Teams lose focus when they lack direction, are distracted, lose sight of goals, and misunderstand the process.
* For Scrum Teams, Retrospectives provide an opportunity for the team to reflect on their work and improve for the next iteration.
* The Scrum Master as Coach must encourage collaboration and creativity and uphold Scrum Values.
* Coaches inspire, are neutral, and communicate verbally and non-verbally.
* Retrospectives focus on improvement for the next Sprint.

Reading: Module 2 Glossary: Scrum Master as a Facilitator and a Coach
Welcome! This alphabetized glossary contains many terms you'll find in this course. This comprehensive glossary also includes additional industry-recognized terms not used in course videos. These terms are essential for you to recognize when working in the industry, participating in user groups, andparticipating in other certificate programs.
TermsDefinitionVideoAffinity diagramA technique used to organize and categorize ideas or information into groups based on their relationships or similarities. It can help the team structure their thoughts and identify common themes during the retrospective.The Sprint RetrospectiveAgile ScrumAn iterative and incremental approach to project management, emphasizing flexibility, collaboration, and continuous improvement. Daily stand-up meetings are an integral part of the Scrum framework.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsBurndown chartA graphical representation of the remaining work versus time in a sprint, helping the team track their progress and identify if they are on track to meet their goals.Focusing the TeamCadenceA regular pattern or rhythm established for the flow of information or activities. In stakeholder management, establishing a cadence ensures consistent and predictable communication and interaction with stakeholders.Managing StakeholdersChange managementThe process of handling and controlling changes that occur during a project or sprint, ensuring they are managed effectively and do not disrupt the team's focus.Focusing the TeamDaily stand-up meetingA brief, structured meeting held every day in Agile Scrum methodology, where the team gathers to discuss their ongoing work. It focuses on progress, tasks, and impediments.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsDefinition of DoneA shared agreement within the team on the criteria that must be met for a task or user story to be considered complete.Focusing the TeamDone criteriaThe agreed-upon conditions or indicators that define when a task or deliverable is considered complete. Establishing clear and shared understanding of done criteria helps maintain consistency and quality in the team's work.Working AgreementFollow-up actionsActions taken by the Scrum Master or the team based on the discussions and insights gained during the daily stand-up. These actions address roadblocks, provide assistance, or facilitate the completion of work.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsGroupthinkA phenomenon where the desire for harmony or conformity within a group results in irrational or dysfunctional decision-making. Encouraging individual participation and avoiding groupthink is crucial to ensure diverse perspectives and ideas are considered.Focusing the TeamImpedimentsObstacles or challenges that hinder the progress of the team. In the daily stand-up meeting, team members share any impediments they are facing to raise awareness and seek assistance.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsNominal group techniqueA structured brainstorming method that allows team members to generate ideas individually and then collectively evaluate and prioritize them. It can help the team avoid biases and ensure everyone's input is heard.The Sprint RetrospectiveProduct OwnerA role in Scrum responsible for representing the customer or user community and managing the product backlog. The Product Owner collaborates with stakeholders to define the product vision, prioritize requirements, and ensure the delivery of business value.Managing StakeholdersRelationship grid matrixA tool used to assess stakeholders based on their importance and influence. It helps categorize stakeholders into different quadrants, such as low-influence/low-importance, high-influence/low-importance, low-influence/high-importance, and high-importance/high-influence.Engaging StakeholdersRetrospectiveA meeting held at the end of a sprint where the team reflects on their work, identifies areas for improvement, and takes actions to enhance their performance in future iterations.Focusing the TeamRoadblocksObstacles or issues that impede progress or hinder the completion of tasks. The third question in the daily stand-up encourages team members to discuss and address any roadblocks they are facing.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsScrum EventsThe time-boxed events in the Scrum framework, including Sprint Planning, Daily Scrum, Sprint Review, and Sprint Retrospective. These events provide opportunities for inspection, adaptation, and collaboration within the Scrum team.The Sprint RetrospectiveScrum FrameworkThe framework that defines the roles, events, artifacts, and rules of Scrum, providing a structure for the team to deliver value incrementally and adapt to change.Scrum Master as a CoachScrum MasterThe role responsible for facilitating the Agile Scrum process, including organizing and leading the daily stand-up meetings, removing impediments, and ensuring the team's adherence to Scrum principles.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsScrum ValuesThe core values of Scrum, including openness, courage, focus, commitment, and respect. These values foster a collaborative environment and support effective communication and teamwork.Focusing the TeamServant leaderA leadership style where the leader focuses on serving the needs of the team and enabling their success, rather than exerting authority or control.Scrum Master as a CoachSprintA time-boxed iteration in Agile Scrum methodology during which a specific set of tasks is completed. The daily stand-up meetings occur within the context of a sprint.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsSprint planA detailed outline of tasks, goals, and deliverables for a sprint. The second question in the daily stand-up ensures that team members are aligned with the plan and working on the right tasks.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsSprint planningA collaborative process where the team defines the scope, goals, and tasks for the upcoming sprint, ensuring a shared understanding and focus on the work to be done.Focusing the TeamSprint RetrospectiveA Scrum event held at the end of each Sprint to reflect on the team's process and identify areas for improvement. Its purpose is to facilitate continuous improvement and generate actionable items for the next Sprint.The Sprint RetrospectiveStakeholder managementThe process of identifying, analyzing, and engaging stakeholders to effectively meet their needs, expectations, and interests. It involves establishing communication channels, managing relationships, and aligning stakeholders with project goals.Managing StakeholdersStakeholder management planA document that outlines how stakeholders will be identified, analyzed, and engaged throughout the project. It includes strategies for communication, collaboration, and addressing stakeholder needs and concerns.Engaging StakeholdersStakeholder matrixA tool used to assess and prioritize stakeholders based on their level of interest and influence. It helps determine the appropriate level of effort and engagement required to manage each stakeholder group effectively.Managing StakeholdersTeam agreementThe understanding and commitment among team members to follow specific practices, principles, and rules, including active participation, asking for assistance, and sharing progress honestly.Managing the Daily stand-up MeetingsTime-boxA fixed duration set for an activity or event. The time-box for the retrospective is typically limited to one hour for each week of the Sprint, ensuring that the conversation remains focused and productive.The Sprint RetrospectiveTraditional project managementReferring to the traditional, plan-driven approach to project management, which is often contrasted with the iterative and adaptive approach of Scrum.Scrum Master as a CoachValue-add exerciseActivities that contribute directly to the creation of value for the project or product. Scrum Masters aim to keep stakeholders focused on these exercises and prevent distractions that may hinder value delivery.Engaging StakeholdersVision formulationThe process of collaboratively developing a shared vision for the project or product among stakeholders. It involves identifying common goals, defining the desired outcomes, and aligning stakeholders' expectations.Managing StakeholdersWorking agreementA document or contract that defines how a team agrees to work together. It establishes the expectations, guidelines, and processes for team collaboration, conflict resolution, decision-making, and communication.Working AgreementModule 3 Introduction and Key Takeaways
