Servant Leadership 
Welcome to this module on how Scrum Masters act as Servant Leaders. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to describe the features of Scrum Leadership, demonstrate with examples of the roles of servant leaders, describe the components of Servant Leadership. 
Describe the function of Servant Leaders, describe the goal of Servant Leaders. 
A basic characteristic of Scrum Masters is Servant Leadership, as a Scrum Master, you must use this leadership type to effectively coach your team. 
Scrum Masters lack their traditional manager employment relationship, so their leadership is influential rather than authoritative. 
This means that Scrum Masters lack the power to force the team members to complete tasks or entice them to complete their work. 
Rather, the influence that Scrum Masters wield is based on their ability to help the team. 
Scrum Masters remove roadblocks, encourage the team, create opportunities, and inspire creativity and collaboration among other things. 
The team follows a Scrum Master because of their ability to forge a path to success. 
Scrum Masters help others understand why they are doing the work and leads the team to figure out a better way of doing things. 
This attitude is different than a traditional manager or project manager whose authority is based on accountability. 
A manager's job is to ensure that work is completed on time, they do not bother about how to improve the team. 
Consider these examples, Danny is a project manager with a tight deadline to close a project in three months. 
He holds a meeting each day to track the progress of the task assigned to the team, while his team is facing the challenge of finding the suitable resources to complete the work. 
When the team approaches Danny for help, he returns, pointing out that as SMEs they should find the solution themselves. 
He reminds them that the project is due in 90 days. 
Danny sticks to conventional methods and makes little effort to support the team. 
On the other hand, Sean, a Scrum Master, approaches his team after a daily scrum and asks them if they have any problems regarding the project assignments. 
He is concerned because he notices that two of his team members, Anne and George, were quiet and looked worried. 
They explained to Sean that they are running up against a roadblock because the lead time might cause a delay on the Sprint. 
Sean immediately gets in touch with the right players and figures out that logistics is the issue. 
Notice the difference in examples, Danny is task-oriented and takes little interest in the team, while Sean is supportive and helps the team to remove the obstacles. 
Sean's leadership focuses on ensuring that the team is set up for success. 
This is the mark of Servant Leaders who realize that success rests with the team achieving goals because they are the ones who do the actual work. 
Influence is a major component in Servant Leadership. 
Influence is not an official designation of authority, but people naturally follow influential leaders. 
Influence for a Scrum Master is the ability to lead teams to collaborate. 
It inspires a team member who is demotivated and does not want to continue working towards the goals. 
Servant Leaders are coaches who can make even the most dysfunctional teams work together. 
They can show the team a better way without giving the answers, by getting them to believe in the process and in their abilities. 
Even in the midst of a disagreement, influential leaders show the team how to use common ground as a basis for working together. 
Servant Leaders take time to understand everyone's points of view, as they recognize that different perspectives deliver powerful results. 
They earn respect by demonstrating their trust in the team, For example, leaders may establish a set of tasks and hold the team accountable. 
They might not give the team a chance to share their opinion on how to improve. 
They may get the work done, but might be dissatisfied with the work, and the discouragement may affect productivity. 
Alternatively, as a Scrum Master, you could involve the team in the decision-making process and guide them to find the answers. 
You have subject matter expertise in the team and if you discourage them from exercising their knowledge, you limit their creativity to achieve goals. 
By definition, Servant Leaders focus on the needs of others, they must be willing to take risks for the team and remove roadblocks that might lead to failure. 
This also requires transparency and avoiding setting unrealistic expectations, your team will trust you if you're honest. 
Remember, you are working with a self-organized team where leadership is shared. 
Failing to focus on others needs is the opposite and will confound the work environment. 
An example is in the daily standups or other venues where Scrum Masters ask the team what is working and what is not. 
There is no rule that says the team must wait until they're retrospected to identify improvement opportunities. 
Servant Leaders will take part in difficult situations, even when it's uncomfortable. 
For example, if a team member identifies a way of doing something and the team agrees, but some stakeholders challenge it, the Scrum Master will find a way to get buy in from the stakeholder. 
The primary goals of Servant Leaders are to help the team and make them better, it is a team-first mentality. 
While many leaders focus on objectives and being the centre of the unit, Scrum Masters, as Servant Leaders, do the opposite. 
They have no interest in what they can gain from the relationship, only what they can do to help the team. 
They empower teams to achieve goals, this translates to sensing even the subtlest needs of the team and taking action to resolve them. 

In this video, you learned that, Scrum Masters remove roadblocks, encourage the team, create opportunities, and inspire creativity and collaboration, among other things. 
Servant Leaders realize that success rests with the team achieving the goals because they are the ones who do the actual work. 
Influence for a Scrum Master is the ability to lead teams to collaborate. 
Servant Leaders focus on the needs of others. 
The primary goal, a servant leader, are to help the team and make them better. 

Define Qualities of a Self-Organized Team and Techniques to Achieve Team Maturity 

Welcome to this module on qualities of a self-organized team and techniques to achieve team maturity. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to define what a self-organized team is, define the qualities of the self-organized team, and describe techniques to reach team maturity. 
Self-organized teams break the tradition of hierarchical manager-employee relationship. 
Consequently, they represent a flat organization. 
In a flat organization, the team works together to figure out how to accomplish the work. 
These types of teams have specific missions when organized. 
They are able to set goals, create an agreement, and determine how they're going to do the work. 
As long as they have the support of the management and are allowed to use their expertise, they can be very successful. 
For some, this approach is a big change. 
So forming a self-organized team must work correctly from the very beginning. 
Finding the right team members is critical to a good start. 
This is more than just choosing to work with the superstar performers, but 
finding the right kind of roles. 
For example, if you're on a baseball team, you would not want to have a team of all pitchers. 
Similarly, having a team of all coders or engineers but no one to do any testing may be detrimental to finishing the project. 
Finding the right team members depends on the requirements of the project. 
Step one is to identify the requirement. 
This should be done by the product owner and the Scrum Master facilitates this process while the organization defines the process. 
This is called a cross-functional team, where each member's skills will complement each other. 
Some team members are natural leaders and others are strong task-oriented individuals. 
On the other hand, if more team members with similar expertise are required to finish a project on time, then you may require multiple team members who fulfill the same role. 
For example, if the project is all about coding, then you would want to have multiple coders on the project. 
If you need someone to code and someone to test, then you will want to have one of each. 
Think about the benefits of a diverse team as well. 
Diverse perspectives only make the team better, as each person can leverage their knowledge and challenge each other team member to look at different perspectives. 
Now that the right team has been assembled, the work begins. 
The first step in this process is preparing for the work. 
Preparing for the work means that the team determines how the work will be completed 
in terms of method, communication styles, equipment needed, expectations, and goals. 
This is when the team agreement is formed. 
Regardless of who is on the team, they must have a plan to function and complete the work. 
For example, a team must decide if they only want to meet virtually, co-locate how frequently to meet and divide the duties. 
While you have a Scrum Master, natural leaders, and task-oriented team members, the burden of leadership falls on everybody. 
Scrum masters are servant leaders and lead by example. 
But everyone in the group must exhibit leadership on various occasions. 
For example, the coder may need to make coding decisions and lead the team through it. 
At other times, the tester may need to stand up, correct, and redirect to explain a new solution. 
In some cases, a team member may pull the group forward through difficult situations. 
For example, if a team member is widely considered an expert, this person may have the team's trust to make the correct decision. 
A shared leadership model means that the teams are accountable for each other and 
all share in success and failure. 
After a team has passed the preparation stage, the work has only begun. 
They must employ techniques to ensure success in a self-organized team. 
The glue that holds this team together is not a manager determining the work and 
providing a reporting structure instead, it is the common interest. 
Therefore, the basis of any technique that the team uses must be what they 
are trying to achieve. 
The techniques can be the following learn to trust, with the help of the scrum master, 
the team should be mindful of basic team building and this leads to trust. 
If the team trusts each other, then they can be more productive because there will 
be a strong sense of support among the team. 
Trust enables members to support each other even in the midst of a disagreement and keeps them focused on the goal. 
Back to basics, successful teams constantly remind themselves of their goals. 
They constantly review the reason that they're all together. 
This could be actively reviewing and updating the agreement. 
Alignment is critical to ensure everyone is reaching the same result. 
Receive coaching, self-organized teams need the voice of the Scrum Master. 
A team that trusts each other will find ways of doing better. 
Be open with each other, scrum teams should ask each other tough questions. 
For example, it is acceptable to ask a team member what you are frustrated about or what you're struggling with. 
If these feelings do not come out in the open, then the team cannot provide the support you need and the issue may never get resolved. 
Teams comprise humans with unique skills, perspectives, and abilities. 
This fact should be acknowledged as you approach the work of a sprint. 
Teams go through a developmental process where it starts with everyone being excited about working on a new project, to finding out where each one fits in, facing some conflicts, and finally understanding what it means to be a team. 

In this video, you learned that self-organized teams are able to set goals, create an agreement, and determine how they're going to do the work. 
Finding the right team member depends on the requirements of the project. 
Preparing for the work means that the team forms an agreement on how the work will be 
completed in terms of method, communication styles, equipment needed, expectations, and goals. 
The team employs techniques to ensure success such as learning to trust, returning to basics, receiving coaching, and being open with each other. 

Expert Viewpoints: Scrum Master as a Coach

Welcome to Expert Viewpoints, the Scrum Master as a Coach. 
In this video, experts provide tips for effective coaching and she had 
a strong coaching challenges. 
A Scrum Master can be an effective coach by embracing a coaching mindset of being curious, 
open-minded, and non-judgmental in your interactions. 
Take time to really understand your team members and the team as a whole, 
ask open ended thought-provoking questions, practice active listening and demonstrating empathy, 
and offer constructive and timely feedback to team members. 
To become an effective coach, actively listen with the intent to understand, not to act. Ask powerful questions. 
Helping the team to come up with their own answers rather than you providing the answers for them. 
Help them to discover the root causes of some of the pain points they're enduring, and help them work out ways to get better. 
First of all, focus on what people want. 
If you understand what matters most to those you are coaching, results will in all likelihood be achieved. 
A good coach is also a good facilitator. 
Try not to be judgmental. 
Throw away your preconceptions. 
If you are open, if you are impartial, the individual will in all likelihood be much more receptive. 
If you are judgmental, the individual will become defensive, and in all likelihood, the coaching will fail. 
To become an effective coach as a Scrum Master, focus on developing strong interpersonal skills, 
active listening, empathy, and asking the right questions. 
Provide guidance, support, continuous improvement, foster learning, self-organization, and empower the team. 
As a Scrum Master, you're already a team coach. 
To be an effective coach, my advice is to take some coaching courses, do some coaching certifications, so that you can even be better at what you do. 
When you guide people, you help them find solutions on their own. 
You don't give them the answer, but you help them find those solutions by giving them the tools to do it. 
Helping them think about a different way. 
Remember you're an outsider here. 
You're not the actual developer, but you can help them look at it from a different perspective or 
challenge them to look at it from a different perspective, and thereby giving them another opportunity to see things differently and come to a different conclusion. 
An example of a coaching challenge I had to overcome that I faced is a resistance to a new way of working. 
Some of the team members felt like Agile was a waste of time and that their current way of working was best. 
The first step here was in recognizing the importance of building trust with the team. 
I took time to understand each person's concern and motivations. 
I explained the benefits of Agile and encourage the team to take small steps toward adopting Agile and then I provided ongoing support and feedback to the team as they adopted Agile as a new way of working. 
Usually the biggest coaching challenge I run into is getting people to live past how they've always done things and trying something new. 
It can be really uncomfortable for them. 
Working with them on to experiment as a short-term change often helps. 
A huge challenge is convincing the individual they have potential. 
A good coach realizes that some individuals may actually be able to perform at a very high level, 
but they simply lack the confidence in themselves to do so. 
You need to be able to sell the individual on the potential they have and gain their motivation and commitment to unlock that potential so that they can excel. 
One coaching challenge I encountered, and while the team members struggling with self-organization and task ownership. 
Through one-on-one coaching sessions, targeted feedback, and guidance on time management, 
the team member improved their skills and became more self-directed. 
One coaching challenge I had was coaching a team from Waterfall to Agile or to Scrum, to be specific. 
This particular team was used to the step-by-step workflow. 
Coming in now and asking them to change the process that they are used to was a challenge. 
But then I made them to understand that with Scrum, we're able to do frequent releases, 
we're able to get feedback from customers frequently. 
There's that communication constantly. 
You can even change to what you want at any point in time in the process, making them understand all these advantages. 
They decided that we should try it as a team, and since that day, the team, the environment, they never looked back. 
Well, I remember one project in particular. 
I was working with a project manager who really just wanted to bulldoze and get through and finish all the tasks. 
But really didn't stop to evaluate risks and had no idea what or no concern rather for what the outcome could have been. 
As a person in a Scrum role, I was able to sit down with this person and really talk through risks and 
really help them to see what the outcomes are, when you really just don't pay attention to the consequences of your action. 

Development Models to Improve Team Effectiveness and Continuous Improvement 
Welcome to this module on development models used to improve team effectiveness and continuous improvement. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to explain the importance of continuous mprovement, describe the different models to make continuous improvement: 
plan, do, check, act, theory of constraints, Six Sigma, and Tuckman ladder. 
Scrum Masters must understand that collaboration does not come naturally to newly formed teams. 
Teams must work at success and have a good leader, facilitator, and coach to help them achieve it. 
As a Scrum Master, you must assume that the team will go through a building process. 
Avoid underestimating the need to guide the team through adequate coaching and leadership. 
The idea is to build on a foundation of goals that the team sets at the beginning of the project or development effort. 
Continuous improvement is a way of thinking rather than a set of tools. 
This means you are committed to eliminating inefficiencies and waste from processes. 
Waste would include anything that distracts the team from meeting their goals, inhibits creativity and collaboration, or activities that are outside of the work plan for each sprint. 
For example, holding two extra meetings per day to talk about something that could be 
covered in the daily stand-up. 
This could include meetings that other stakeholders tried to set up with team members. 
An example of inefficiencies include work that could be automated, but the team member decides to do it manually. 
As Scrum Masters, you must create an environment for the team to be productive and actively find ways to improve their process. 
You should empower the team to implement solutions that make them better. 
For example, while writing code, if a team member suggests a faster way of testing the code, let the team try it. 
Another example is where you organize the team in close proximity because it encourages fewer steps throughout the day, and will help the team work more efficiently. 
Let's look at some development models to improve the effectiveness of teams as a continuous process. 
Plan, do, check, act is a commonly used approach to improvement looking at the process as an ongoing effort. 
Notice the circular nature, that indicates that there is no end to the improvement cycle. 
Once an improvement is made, you make it even better or move on to the next opportunity. 
There should never come a time when you were satisfied and stop looking for improvement opportunities. 
The inability to see beyond the current state is dangerous and lacks vision and blocks the creative process. 
Using the plan, do, check, act method will make the team think innovatively. 
For a team to be effective, there has to be a plan. 
Planning requires the team to do work together to set expectations and agree on a course of action. 
Scrum teams do this in the sprint planning and retrospectives. 
Events such as the daily stand-up are organized opportunities for the team to improve. 
In the plan phase, you identify the root of the cause of an issue or an area you want to improve. 
You are gathering data, testing it, generating a hypothesis, and coming to a consensus with the team on a way forward. 
In the do phase, the team implements, collects data, and uses a scientific approach to 
monitor the progress of the improvement. 
Moving onto check, you make conclusions about your data and find ways to optimize and conform even more to the goal. 
Act is the implementation of the improvements you made. 
The theory of constraints runs on the idea of eliminating bottlenecks in the process. 
For example, if an approval process would be taking three days and causing delays for the team, you would use the theory of constraints to relieve pressure. 
Theory of constraints comprises of the following steps: identify, exploit, subordinate, 
elevate, and repeat. 
First, you identify the issue or process step that is causing the bottleneck. 
Next, you exploit. 
That means instead of stopping the process, keep it moving and try to find ways to improve it. 
For example, if the testing process seems to delay the team, let it run its course, but look for ways to optimize. 
Next, you can try subordinate, where you would make the other processes fall in line with the bottleneck step. 
Ensure that this is being properly fed by the rest of the process. 
Elevate happens if there is still a problem. 
Then you look at eliminating the bottleneck. 
For example, if a machine constantly breaks down on a production line despite all the maintenance to support it, perhaps a new capital investment is needed to replace the machine. 
Repeat is simply moving on to the next process step. 
Once you eliminate a bottleneck, there will always be a new one. 
You must continue the cycle, never settling, but always reaching to achieve higher standards of excellence. 
Six Sigma is about removing variation from the process. 
Process should be consistent and must be monitored to ensure that you get the expected results each time. 
For example, if you're writing code and have different results in each iteration when you would get blue as an outcome and you expect a purple background. 
The Six Sigma works on the five-step methodology, DMAIC, that is define, measure, analyze, improve, control. 
Define includes identifying the issue and understanding what the problem is. 
Measure is about data collection and getting to know the process and listen to what the data tells you. 
Analyze is using quantitative methods and the scientific method to find the root cause of an issue. 
Improve means implementing a solution. 
Finally, control is the steps taken to ensure that what you improve stays improved. 
A way of understanding team development and figuring out the appropriate tools to apply is the Tuckman ladder, which has five phases. 
Forming. Scrum Masters must build the team. 
The forming stage for Scrum is a proper team agreement. 
Ground rules and goals are established and the vision is delivered. 
Scrum Masters must take action and get the group to buy in. 
Storming. Perhaps this is where the team starts to experience reality and they get to know each other. 
Leaders begin to emerge and opinions become stronger. 
Scrum Masters must be prepared as a facilitator when the team is in this phase. 
Scrum Master should be confident and providing honest feedback, 
encouraging collaboration, building the team, and celebrate early successes. 
Norming. The team begins to see the value as a team and understand each other's opinions. 
There will be less focus on the individual and more focus on what can be done to support the team and vision. 
At this stage, Scrum Masters should continue to provide feedback and coaching to help the team improve. 
Performing. During this phase, the team supports each other and work towards the goal. 
Decision-making is a team effort as well. 
Scrum Master should continue to coach and listen for signs of any issues that the team may have. 
Adjourning. The team now disbands and moves on to the next project. 

In this video, you learned that continuous improvement means that you are committed to eliminating inefficiencies and waste from processes. 
Plan, do, check, act is a commonly used approach to improvement and has a circular nature. 
Theory of constraints comprises the steps: identify, exploit, subordinate, elevate, and repeat. 
The Six Sigma works on the five-step methodology, DMAIC, that is define, measure, analyze, improve, and control. 
Tuckman ladder has five phases: forming, storming, norming, performing, and adjourning. 

Product Owner Collaboration
Scrum Master and the Product Owner Collaboration
Welcome to Scrum Master and Product Owner Collaboration. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to: compare the Scrum Master and Product Owner roles and describe how the Scrum Master supports the Product Owner during the different scrum events including product backlog refinement, sprint backlog creation, sprint, and sprint review. 
The product owner represents the customer and focuses on turning the product vision into reality. 
The Scrum Master focuses on ensuring the team works within the scrum framework, following the required processes. 
In addition, the Scrum Master help stakeholders understand the scrum process and the goals set by the Product Owner. 
Together, the Scrum Master and Product Owner steer the team to build a successful product. 
Scrum Masters and Product Owners collaborate to accomplish various scrum events. 
Let's start with product backlog refinement. 
The product owner is responsible for creating and refining the product backlog. 
The Scrum Master and development team may help the Product Owner define and estimate level of effort for the backlog items. 
During the product backlog refinement, the product owner clearly specifies the product vision. 
The Scrum Master, on the other hand, ensures everyone is working towards the same goal. 
While product backlog refinement process is continuous, the Product Owner sets 
priorities for different backlog items. 
When the sprint backlog is created, the Scrum Master helps ensure the sprint backlog is 
within the limits of what is achievable during the sprint. 
The Scrum Master ensures alignment of the sprint to the product owner's vision for the product. 
During the sprint, the Product Owner clarifies any questions from the development team. 
The Scrum Master helps facilitate the query resolution process and ensures the product owner is available. 
The Scrum Master helps to manage product owner and stakeholder expectations during the sprint. 
For example, if a Product Owner requests a new feature to be added to the sprint, the Scrum Master will ask the development team to review and accept or reject the addition. 
Further, the Scrum Master will need to coach the Product Owner on the limits and success. 
Finally, the Scrum Master facilitates the sprint review, put the Product Owner and team to 
evaluate the items from the sprint backlog. 
Depending on the evaluation, the Product Owner decides that the work can be considered a product increment. 
The Product Owner is responsible for the product vision and what the customer wants. 
The Scrum Master does not influence the product vision or customer-related decisions. 
Instead, the Scrum Master helps the Product Owner to facilitate better processes. 

In this video, you learned that the Product Owner brings the roadmap and the vision while the Scrum Master ensures that everyone in the team adheres to the scrum framework. 
The Product Owner is responsible for creating and refining the product backlog. 
The Scrum Master helps to define and estimate product backlog items. 
During the sprint backlog creation, the Product Owner sets priority for backlog items while 
the Scrum Master helps ensure that the sprint backlog is within limits. 
During the sprint, the Product Owner is responsible for clarifying questions. 
The Scrum Master helps facilitate the query resolution process. 
During the sprint review, the Scrum Master facilitates the review. 
The Product Owner evaluates the sprint backlog items and decides if the work is a product increment. 
Expert Viewpoints: Servant Leadership
Welcome to Expert Viewpoints: Servant Leadership. 
In this video, experts will describe how Scrum Masters serve as servant leaders and 
learn how these Scrum Masters effectively use servant leadership in their jobs. 
How does the Scrum Master serve as a servant leader? 
A Scrum Master prioritizes the needs of the team. 
They actively listen and empathize and support team members in achieving their goals. 
They remove impediments and create an environment that enables the team to perform at their best. 
My go-to thing to say to my team is what or who do I need to block or tackle for you today? 
The Scrum Master serves as a servant leader by prioritizing the team's need, facilitating growth, and removing impediments. 
Unlike traditional leadership, servant leadership emphasizes collaboration and empowerment. 
A servant leader must employ three critical leadership behaviors. 
Let's walk through them one at a time. 
First and foremost, a Scrum Master is a barrier or obstacle remover. 
Servant leaders maximize delivery by removing blockers or obstacles that may impede the team's process. 
Secondly, a Scrum Master who is a servant leader acts as what we refer to as diversion shield. 
Servant leaders protect the team from internal and external diversions that may redirect the team away from their current objectives and reduce their overall productivity in the sprint. 
Finally, they encourage and provide development opportunities. 
They provide tools, encouragement, and they do all that they can to keep the team satisfied and most importantly, productive. 
Often, we think of a leader as someone who takes control, tells others what to do. 
A Scrum Master is a servant leader in that they're there to serve the team, remove obstacles, enable growth, and support the team's goals. 
They're there to enable the environment where the team can flourish. 
One way they do this is to encourage the team to experiment, making sure that they understand that if that experiment fails, it's okay because they're learning and still getting better. 
This encourages trust and respect, as well as encourages the team to be more innovative, which benefits the company and the customer. 
The Scrum Master is there for the best interest of the organization, the team, the product owner. 
They're there to help and guide them, especially through the Scrum process. 
And when you talk about servant leadership, you're really talking about teaching. 
It's one thing to reprimand somebody because they didn't either understand the process or they didn't perform as well. 
But maybe they didn't have the skills, maybe they didn't have the training. 
They're doing their very best. 
You, as the coach are going to get them to that place where they need to be to perform their best. 
It could be something simple or complex, but your job as a coach to help them with that. 
Also, servant leaders again show compassion because they are treating people like people rather than treating people as robots or resources. 
As a Scrum Master, being a servant leader means that you're putting your team's needs ahead of yours so you're there for the team. 
You're making the team to understand that you're there for them. 
They should trust you and they can come to you with everything, 
with every problem, every need that they have. 
That way, once the team trusts you, they are able to progress, move forward. 
Sprint goals are going to be constantly made with little or no hurdles. 
Can you provide an example of when servant leadership was effective? 
So an example of when servant leadership is effective is encouraging feedback culture by leading regular retrospectives. 
And reflect on processes and identify improvement areas and celebrate success. 
This will create a psychologically safe environment where team members could provide constructive feedback to one another. 
For example, when team members faced a complex issue, I listened, understood their challenges and provided right support. 
By fostering collaboration and empowerment, the team achieved their goals successfully. 
One of my mentors was an exceptional servant leader. 
He worked in a high-pressure environment. 
However, despite the high-pressure environment, despite the scheduling pressures that individual was under, this individual always took the time to learn what motivated each member of our team. 
And they found ways to reward each individual for the great work they accomplished in a way that was meaningful for them. 
I joined an environment one time where they were very rebellious towards me. 
Their understanding of being a Scrum Master was that I was coming in as 
a manager or as a lead to monitor them. 
So I could sense that they did not want me there. 
So what I did was having one on ones with them. 
In those one on ones, I made sure that the very first few minutes of our conversations had nothing to do with work. 
It was all about their lives, what they were passionate about aside from work. 
And then during those meetings, I did realize that myself and most of them, we had so many things in common. 
Then they started liking me and saying that no, I was not just there to monitor them or to control them. 
I was there because we have a common goal to succeed. 
And that made them to warm up towards me. 
And everything became smooth after that. 
I've done this with some of the teams that I work with, and that really is being there in the trenches with them. 
What you want to avoid is having the team feel like they're alone, lost, without support. 
And if you're just there to listen to the team member and really find a way to help them, because they're going to turn to you, they're going to come to you and ask for help. 
And the most powerful thing you can do for your team is to support them. 
Now that may be sitting there physically with them, jumping on a call with them, being in another meeting with them where they have to explain something. 
Talking to the product owner, but even just being heard sometimes just being heard, talking yourself through the problem, you find the solution.

Develop a Product Vision and Determine Product Goals
 Welcome to this module on assisting the development of a product vision and determining of product goals. 
After watching this video, you will be able to explain the need of product vision, define a product vision, identify the customer's needs, explain how to address customer's requirements, demonstrate how to develop a product vision with an example. 
The product owner translates the customer's needs into a vision that the team can build. 
While the Scrum Master as a facilitator, ensures that the process that develops a product vision works within the prescribed framework. 
The product owner ensures that the product vision aligns with what the customer wants to buy. 
The vision describes the end product as per the customer requirements. 
In addition, it determines the part to be included in the product's development and the part to be excluded as the scope does in other scenarios, all stakeholders refer to the product vision when they embark on the development path. 
The product vision is the basis of the product backlog and the sprint backlog. 
Looking at vision, the team should be able to visualize the missing components, the sprints and the resulting progress in the development should support the product vision. 
What is a product vision? 
The product vision can be summarized as the vision for the future state of the customers 
needs transformed into something tangible. 
In other words, if a customer has a need, the product owner is responsible for turning that into something they can use. 
For the team and stakeholders, it becomes the goal. 
The product vision starts with the product owner developing it with adequate time before a product backlog emerges. 
Your product backlog should be built after the product vision has been developed and efined. 
The product owner develops the vision starting with a need. 
From the need, the product owner should be able to envision the product that will meet the requirements. 
They should make multiple attempts at refining the need. 
Should understand the market and have a clear understanding of how the product will fulfill what the customer wants. 
Product owners must always base the product on need. 
The end results should inspire and excite the team and other stakeholders. 
Fulfilling a need in the right market increases your chances of success. 
The team will appreciate the opportunity to be a part of a winning development effort. 
Once you identify the need, understand how the product will satisfy the customer. 
To satisfy the customer, you need to answer the customer's questions. 
For example, some questions you may address are, how do you process your data? 
How do you manage meetings? 
How can we improve our measurement system? 
How can we improve timely response to customer feedback? 
Then you should also understand who your target customer is. 
If you can identify your customer, you know how to influence and hold the interest of the customer group. 
For example, you can avoid mistakes like misaligning an expensive project with a small, marginally interested group of customers. 
To better understand your customers. 
You can create Scrum personas. 
Once you become familiar with the customer, you can determine the customer's needs. 
The product vision should convince the team and should win your customer. 
A very basic example is a courier service that has a lot of data to process. 
The data is coded by number, and includes the type of vehicle driven, duration of each trip in miles, and amount of fuel consumed in gallons. 
For instance, one represents the van, 22 is the number of miles, and three gallons is the amount of fuel consumed. 
The management of this delivery industry wants to automate the tracking process rather than rely on the driver's report or an ABS report. 
Your development company has been appointed to address the need. 
The product owner develops the following vision. 
Product vision: an automatic mileage and fuel tracker by classification of vehicle. 
It solves the issue using a manual and flawed process. 
The vehicle self-reports when it's idle for more than 15 minutes. 
The tracker can be in narrative form, but it can be constructed in a matrix as shown for ease of tracking.

a vision that the team can build. 
The product vision can be summarized as the vision for the future state of the customer's needs transformed into something tangible. 
From the need the product owner should be able to envision the product that will meet the requirements. 
If you can identify your customer, you know how to influence and hold the interests of the customer group. 
The product vision should convince the team and should win your customer. 

Collaborating with the Product Owner to refine the backlog 
Welcome to how to refine the backlog and collaboration between Product Owners and the Scrum Master. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to, explain the need for collaboration in refinement of the backlog. 
Describe the different types of backlogs. 
Explain the need for refinement of a backlog. 
Describe progressive elaboration. 
Refinement of the backlog requires both the Scrum Master and the Product Owner. 
These two members of the team should come together before the work of the developers, SMEs, or any other member of the team begins. 
The reason is because the vision alone is not granular enough or structured in a way that would make the team successful. 
Thus, the backlog occurs at specific points in the process. 
The backlog starts with the product backlog. 
The Product Owner brings the roadmap and vision. 
However, the implementation of the vision by the team into deliverables requires collaboration between the Scrum Master and Product Owner. 
The team will help in breaking down the vision into deliverables as they are responsible to do the work. 
However, what is eventually delivered is the decision of the Product Owner. 
Ensuring the process is done efficiently and successfully is the job of the Scrum Master. 
The product backlog should occur after the vision has been explained. 
The backlog will determine what the team will complete during the Sprints. 
Sprints occur in a meeting facilitated by the Scrum Master, and the length depends on how long the Sprint is. 
If the Sprint is four weeks, then the refinement should be about six hours. 
A two week Sprint should take about three hours, which is the minimum duration for 
any length of Sprint. 
The Sprint backlog is another refinement process where everyone is aligned on what will be the focus of each Sprint. 
The Product Owner needs to agree on what the team works on, and the team must agree on what they think is possible during a Sprint. 
Refinement is critical or else the team will have no direction. 
It is unfair to expect the team to take a vision and create what the Product Owner has in mind. 
The team must have manageable chunks, whether they are Epics or user stories, which give definite meaning to the vision. 
These chunks are a reasonable expectation of what the team will deliver. 
Refinement is also necessary because it clarifies the expectations. 
Even if the work is broken down in the user stories, the team needs to understand what is expected. 
Progressive elaboration should be considered when it comes to the refinement process. 
The point of doing this exercise is to get better each time the planning process occurs. 
Each meeting helps to improve the understanding of their requirements. 
If the Product Owner knew exactly what to build, there would be no need for a scrum process. 
The deliverables become more defined and precise with each iteration. 

In this video, you learned that, refinement of the backlog requires both the Scrum Master and the product owner. 
The Product Owner brings the roadmap and vision. 
Sprints occur in a meeting facilitated by the Scrum Master and the length depends on how long the Sprint is. 
The team must have manageable chunks, whether they are Epics or User Stories which give definite meaning to the vision. 
In a progressive elaboration, the deliverables become more defined and precise with each iteration. 
Refining the Backlog 
Welcome to this module on Refining the Backlog. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to: define refinement and it's types, use an example to explain the need for refinement, identify the questions and techniques used to achieve effective backlog refinement. 
Refinement, also known as backlog refinement, is a collaborative practice that aims to review, clarify, and prepare items in the product backlog for future development iterations. 
It involves the product owner, Scrum Master, and the team. 
Refinement occurs on two distinct occasions, the sprint backlog and the product backlog. 
Sprint backlog refinement occurs during an active sprint to refine the backlog items planned for the upcoming sprints. 
The product backlog includes the product owner's vision and aims to break it down into deliverables that the team can understand. 
At the end of the sprints, the team achieves these deliverables known as increments in the form of epics and user stories. 
By engaging in the refinement process, the team gains the clarity and granularity necessary to fill in the details of the big picture. 
To illustrate this, let's consider an example of an assembly line scenario. 
In this scenario, workers are assembling clocks. 
Worker A starts by providing parts to worker B who assembles two pieces before passing them on to worker C, worker C then remove sharp edges and attaches two additional pieces. 
Finally, worker D takes over and well, it's the last two components resulting in a complete clock unit ready for shipment to the customer. 
At the beginning of this assembly line scenario, the input may seem disconnected and their purpose is unclear to the shipping department. 
However, as these inputs progress throughout the refinement process of assembly, the true nature and purpose of the product becomes evident. 
The team gradually transitions from a high level of vision, viewing the process from a distant 50,000-foot perspective to a more detailed understanding at 10,000 feet. 
The team can still grasp the big vision while recognizing logical breakpoints they must addressed within a sprint. 
In this refining process, the product owner takes the role of the leader and organizer, guiding the team through the desired outcome. 
The Scrum Master helps the product owner by removing obstacles and facilitating refinement meetings. 
While they may provide guidance and service process experts, they never dictate what the vision should look like to the product owner. 
Their role is to support the product owner's vision and ensure that the necessary individuals
are present in the refinement meetings. 
Effective backlog refinement involves asking the right questions. 
Here are some key inquiries to consider. 
What are they trying to achieve? What are their goals? 
What will be the overall benefit? 
What would this require from the team in terms of effort? 
Who will get value? What value will come out of the backlog item? 
In addition to asking the right questions, there are several effective techniques that can 
facilitate a smooth refinement process. 
Here are a few effective techniques that help to conduct the refinement process smoothly. 
Avoid a massive backlog. 
It is important to keep the backlog manageable to prevent the team from losing control. 
Rule out overanalysing a single backlog item. 
Spending excessive time on a single item can hinder progress. 
Try to set a time limit such as 20 minutes for discussing and refining each backlog item. 
Prepare the team and provide an agenda. 
Before the refinement session, inform the team about the items that will be covered. 
This helps prevent over-complication and ensures everyone is on the same page. 
Use a splitting technique freely. 
If a backlog item seems it could be developed separately from the current effort, consider splitting it into its own item. 
This technique is useful for managing the backlog effectively and addressing different issues individually. 
For example, a space shuttle would not be its own development effort. 
It could be split into hundreds of different efforts and have immensely better results. 
If items address a different issue, it's probably best to split them up. 
Validate user stories. 
It is essential to ensure that everyone understands the user stories. 
If the team struggles to understand the story, cannot estimate effort accurately. 
or fails to align with the product owner's vision, the story may need further refinement before validation. 
Aim for an adequate number of ready backlog items. 
As a rule, stride to have up to double the number of backlog items needed for a sprint. 
This ensures that there is enough work for the team to complete. The product owner can prioritize the most important items and any unfinished work can be carried over to the next sprint. 
Engage in an ongoing exercise. Backlog refinement is an ongoing exercise. 
Even after each sprint, the refinement process continues. 
Updates and changes may occur at any moment requiring adjustment to the backlog. 
It is a critical activity that the team and the product owner must engage in for ongoing success. 
Negotiate priorities. 
Fulfilling the product owner's vision often requires negotiation between the team's understanding, importance to the product owner, value creation, and feasibility. 
The refinement session serve as an opportunity to discuss and resolve these concerns collaboratively, considering factors like value, difficulty, priority, dependencies, and risks. 

In this video, you learned that refinement involves the product owner, Scrum Master, and the team. 
Refinement helps to understand the true nature and purpose of a product. 
Effective backlog refinement involves asking the right questions related to the goals, 
visions, value, and so on. 
There are a few techniques that help conduct the refinement process smoothly, 
such as avoiding massive backlogs, using the splitting technique and so on.

* The team must have manageable chunks, whether they are Epics or User Stories, which give definite meaning to the vision.


Reading: Module 3 Glossary: Leadership: Scrum Master as a Servant Leader 
TermDefinitionAdjourningThe final phase of the Tuckman ladder when a team disbands and moves on to the next project or endeavor.Assembly Line ScenarioAn example used to illustrate the process of refining the backlog, comparing it to the assembly of a product through different stages, involving multiple workers.BacklogA list of items that require attention or completion, often used in the context of agile project management. It includes both the Product Backlog and the Sprint Backlog.Cross-functional teamA team composed of members with diverse skills and expertise that complement each other, allowing them to tackle different aspects of a project or task effectively.Development ModelsFrameworks or methodologies used to improve team effectiveness and drive continuous improvement in the development process.DMAICDefine, Measure, Analyze, Improve, and Control; a problem-solving and improvement methodology used in Six Sigma to drive process optimization and quality enhancement.EpicsLarge, high-level user stories that capture broad functionality or features of a product.IncrementThe sum of all completed and potentially releasable product backlog items at the end of a sprint.Plan, Do, Check, Act A popular improvement approach that emphasizes iterative cycles of planning, executing, evaluating, and implementing changes to enhance processes.Product BacklogA prioritized list of features, enhancements, and fixes that need to be developed for a product. It represents the requirements and vision of the product and is owned by the Product Owner.Progressive ElaborationA principle in agile project management where requirements and solutions are developed incrementally and iteratively.Query ResolutionThe process of addressing and resolving questions or concerns raised by the team or stakeholders.RefinementThe process of continuously clarifying and detailing backlog items to ensure they are ready for implementation.Scrum FrameworkA framework for managing and developing complex products that emphasizes collaboration, iterative development, and continuous improvement.Scrum LeadershipThe leadership approach used by Scrum Masters, characterized by influence and facilitation rather than hierarchical control.Scrum Master FundamentalsA module providing foundational knowledge about Scrum Masters and their role in agile project management.Scrum MasterA role in the Scrum framework responsible for facilitating the team's progress and ensuring adherence to Scrum principles and practices.Self-organized teamA team that breaks the tradition of a hierarchical manager-employee relationship and represents a flat organization. They work together to figure out how to accomplish the work, set goals, create an agreement, and determine how they will perform the tasks.Servant LeadershipA leadership style adopted by Scrum Masters, focusing on serving and supporting the team rather than exerting authority.Six SigmaA data-driven approach for process improvement that seeks to minimize process variation and defects through rigorous measurement, analysis, and control.Splitting TechniqueA technique used to divide a backlog item into smaller, more manageable parts to facilitate development and address different issues individually.Sprint BacklogA subset of the Product Backlog selected for implementation during a Sprint. It includes the tasks and user stories that the development team commits to completing within the Sprint.SprintA time-boxed iteration in Scrum where a development team completes a set of work items and delivers a potentially shippable product increment.Team dynamicsThe interactions, relationships, and development process experienced by a team.Team maturityThe level of development and effectiveness achieved by a team in terms of collaboration, communication, and achieving shared goals.Theory of ConstraintsA management philosophy that aims to identify and eliminate bottlenecks or constraints in a process to optimize overall system performance.Tuckman ladderA model describing the stages of team development, including forming, storming, norming, performing, and adjourning, to understand team dynamics and apply appropriate strategies.
