Identifying Organizational Impediments 
Welcome to this module on how to identify organizational impediments. 
After watching this video you'll be able to define impediments and blockers, describe a few scenarios in which impediments can arise in Scrum, discuss the importance of addressing impediments for Scrum progress and within team members, and explain the role of the Scrum master in managing impediments and blockers. 
Like other methodologies, Scrum places tremendous importance on progress. 
However, it distinguishes itself by focusing on the team rather than individual tasks to pave the path to progress. 
Despite the distinguishing feature, Scrum is not immune to encountering impediments that hinder the progress. 
An impediment in essence, is anything that halts or hinders advancement. 
These impediments come in various forms and can originate from both internal and external sources. 
Among these impediments, there exist particularly formidable obstacles known as blockers, blockers pose a serious threat as they have the potential to derail the project and bring the progress to a halt completely. 
It is essential to address impediments that can hinder progress in Scrum. 
Consider these examples, Juan is waiting for Richard to resolve a problem discovered during testing. 
Richard takes the initiative to make a change and discusses it with the Scrum master, Cherry. 
Unfortunately, Cherry fails to recognize the importance of this change, and the communication between Richard and Juan is significantly limited. 
Here, impediments occur when the Scrum master fails to communicate effectively with the various team members. 
This can create a situation where the team remains unaware of the changes or issues consequently halting progress. 
These limitations in progress may arise from missing requirements or a lack of crucial information needed to move forward. 
Another example of an impediment in Scrum is the presence of future rework or technical debt. 
This occurs when the development effort fails to yield increments that meet the acceptance criteria set by the product owner. 
As a result, the work gets postponed to subsequent sprints, resulting in the accumulation of technical debt that may impact future sprints. 
But how do these impediments arise? Let's find out. 
Impediments in Scrum can arise from various scenarios. 
For instance, the absence of team members during meetings, team dissatisfaction, and quitting of one of the team members can all hinder progress. 
Additionally, assembling a team without the necessary skills required for the project poses a challenge and becomes an impediment to progress. 
Moreover, if product owners frequently change the product vision while the team is already working on it, it creates confusion and disrupts progress. 
These examples highlight the diverse nature of impediments and the potential obstacles they can present in Scrum projects. 
Addressing blockers is crucial for progress in Scrum, if left unattended, they halt advancement. 
For example, if a product owner makes major changes to the project, but the team lacks clarity or feels confused about the next steps, they will have no other choice but to halt their work. 
Similarly, if the product owner decides to upgrade features based on market research, 
and it turns out that this upgrade is incompatible with the already completed work, the team will need to stop progressing. 
Therefore, recognizing and effectively managing blockers ensures uninterrupted project flow and enables the team to navigate challenges successfully. 
Unattended impediments in blockers can have a catastrophic effect on the team, they hinder progress, limit capabilities, and erode the team's morale. 
Team members may experience panic, struggle with collaboration, make mistakes, and face challenges in getting the work accepted as increments by the product owner. 
It is crucial to address these issues promptly to maintain team morale, foster effective collaboration, and ensure successful project outcomes. 
The important task is for the Scrum master to be diligent about identifying impediments and blockers. 
Detection and prevention are two of the most important tools. 
The Scrum master must keep track of how well the team is progressing, in case the team is slower than usual, the Scrum master must investigate and talk to the team. 
To ensure progress and address impediments, the daily scrum is of vital importance, the sprint retrospective is another opportunity to identify challenges. 
Even though the current sprint may be completed, intervention is necessary for further sprints. 
Additionally, fostering collaboration and communication is crucial, allow the team to communicate issues whenever possible. 
Focusing on the goal is crucial to avoid impediments or blockers. 
Challenges arise when the team loses sight of their purpose. 
Regularly reviewing the team's agreements and identifying potential dependencies 
helps prevent confusion and ensures progress towards the goal. 

In this video, you learned that an impediment is anything that halts or hinders advancement. 
It is essential to address impediments that can hinder progress in Scrum. 
Recognizing and effectively managing blockers ensures uninterrupted project flow. 
Unattended impediments and blockers hinder progress, limit capabilities, and erode the team's morale. 
The important task for the Scrum master is to be diligent about identifying impediments and blockers. 

Dealing with Difficult Stakeholders 
Welcome to this module on how to handle difficult stakeholders. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to Explain the importance of stakeholders. 
Describe how stakeholders can influence the project development. 
Describe how to control collaboration by the stakeholders. 
Describe the different types of influence by the stakeholders. 
Describe how to mitigate issues created by stakeholders. 
And describe how to know your stakeholders. 
All projects, programs, and development efforts are impacted and influenced by stakeholders. 
Stakeholders can be internal and external, and you must identify all of them to avoid missing important ones. 
These individuals have varying degrees of interest and influence and can support or derail your effort. 
Scrum Masters and product owners will fail without managing the power they wield. 
Product owners must also consider stakeholder interest and decide how they impact the direction of the project. 
You must keep stakeholders with high influence and high interest happy before they become difficult stakeholders. 
Ongoing relationship building, communication, listening, and appropriate involvement will keep the important group happy. 
However, difficult stakeholders are to be dealt with strategically. 
In some cases, a group of influential stakeholders may contribute ideas and offer guidance to the product owner. 
They can represent various groups. 
For example, the product owner is the representative of the customer and may have very specific expertise. 
A group of stakeholders may be invited to meetings and they can offer their insight to the product owner. 
The product owner has the final say on the direction of the development and can decide how the recommendations of the stakeholders will be used. 
Not all stakeholders collaborate well, so their contribution needs to be controlled. 
Scrum Masters and product owners must look for warning signs before the situation gets out of control. 
Scrum Masters must utilize their facilitation skills and ability to help others collaborate. 
You as a Scrum Master should look for the warning signs in a sprint review or walking down the hallway, or when a stakeholder insists on joining the retrospective meeting in which they should not be included. 
For example, take it as a warning sign if stakeholders complain about the work of the team and point out that it is not good enough, even though the product owner moves it forward as an increment. 
Stakeholders may try to use their influence to challenge the product. 
They may have a genuine concern or are hoping to sneak in something that benefits them by adding work to the team. 
You should be able to recognize these signs and mitigate them. 
Stakeholders may try to impose themselves into the development process and influence the team. 
For example, someone from finance may want the team to limit the cost of materials that could influence the design. 
Someone from an engineering department may want a new feature added to save time on 
a future project. 
In other cases, stakeholders may insist on the product owner using a requirement they 
developed independently and imply that it must be as described circumventing the backlog refinement process. 
Even worse, they may try to influence what is a priority for the team. 
Only the product owner decides on prioritization. 
Stakeholders may also try to intrude into various meetings like the daily standup or 
in the decision-making process. 
This mostly happens when a stakeholder is a senior leader and tries to use their authority and influence to fulfil their interests. 
To avoid issues, the Scrum Master must facilitate the participation of the stakeholders. 
They should be firm, understanding, and flexible to ensure adequate contribution and support from the stakeholders, but simultaneously limit their influence on the team. 
Prevention is the best solution. 
Primarily, stakeholders should understand their role in the development process. 
Their support is important in the effort, but they cannot control the project. 
This expectation can be shared by setting guidelines on the roles and responsibilities of the various stakeholders. 
The guidelines should be beyond the individual project level, and they should be formulated by the organization. 
However, you cannot afford to lose the stakeholders that are important to the project. 
Try to understand their requirements. 
If you communicate and are transparent and establish a good relationship, you have a much higher chance of winning them and minimizing the chance of handling difficult stakeholders. 
Part of getting to know a stakeholder is recognizing their interests. 
You should be able to define what is important to them. 
Make an effort to get their feedback through demonstrations and other collaborative efforts. 
If the stakeholder feels they have an opportunity to offer genuine feedback, their attitude will be more favorable towards you. 
Most importantly, communicate. 
Communication means listening and being transparent. 
You should be clear about what type of interaction is preferred by the stakeholders and communicate accordingly. 

In this video, you learned that all projects, programs, and development efforts are impacted and influenced by stakeholders. 
The product owner has the final say of the direction of development and can decide how recommendations of the stakeholders will be used. 
Scrum Master and product owners must look for warning signs before the situation gets out of control. 
Scrum Master must facilitate the participation of the stakeholders. 
You should be clear about what type of interaction is preferred by the stakeholders and communicate accordingly. 

Expert Viewpoints: Product Owner Collaboration
Welcome to Expert Viewpoints: Product Owner Collaboration. 
In this video, experts describe critical features of the relationship between scrum masters and product owners. 
These experts will also share how they contributed to a product owner success. 
The relationship between a scrum master and product owner is crucial for the success of a scrum team and the delivery of valuable products. 
Critical features of that relationship include collaboration, open communication, and transparency. 
The scrum master and the product owner need to have regular communication, sharing progress, information, and challenges openly. 
This helps in making informed decisions and addressing any issues that may arise. 
Some concrete examples of how a scrum master can assist a product owner would be to provide guidance and education on agile methodologies and facilitating a backlog grooming session. 
Ultimately, it's the product owner's responsibility to groom the backlog and ensure that there's clear requirements. 
A scrum master can help by facilitating that session. 
Some critical features of the relationship between a scrum master and a product owner 
are, one, collaboration. 
You're collaborating with the product owner to refine the backlog. 
You're going to help them with that process. 
You're going to help them deliver maybe a story, an epic, whatever it may be for the team to then take and develop something that's useful for the customer. 
Now, another thing you're going to be good at is communication. 
You have to be good at communicating with your product owner. 
You have to develop that relationship communication. 
Have an agreement on how you're going to do that and really be about the process. 
The scrum master is that process expert that we need to guide the product owner. 
Now, the product owner is going to be focused on the customer's needs, 
but you need to bridge that gap between what the team needs and what the product owner needs. 
An example where I was able to assist the product owner be successful is really in the area of stakeholder management. 
You have a lot of stakeholders who have opinion and of course, the stakeholders, there's varying degrees of importance and influence, so you need to help this product owner control that and manage that because they're going to get a lot of different competing feedback. 
That's the most difficult part. 
You have very influential stakeholders with a very high interest in the project, yet they don't agree on anything. 
Well, what do you do with that? 
The product owner is going to need your help sorting that out. 
Again, as a coach, you are an expert in teaching people how to collaborate. 
You bridge that gap together and help these team members, these stakeholders, the product owner, you're going to help them be really good at collaborating and come to conclusions. 
Remember, what's at stake here is what the customers' needs are. 
Now you're not going to give them the answer. 
Scrum masters don't do that, but you are going to show them how to arrive at that conclusion. 
I've been in a lot of situations where you had two managers that did not agree on something and sometimes if you let that stuff goes unchecked, it can become very contentious. 
The most important relationship in a scrum team is that of a scrum master and a product owner. 
A product owner brings in all the requirements from the customer. 
As a scrum master, you're there to make sure that those requirements have been 
communicated to the scrum team effectively. 
That is why emphasis is always been placed on the scrum master and the product owner 
having those weekly meetings to understand what the customer wants and how the team is 
able to deliver what the customer wants. 
One time we had a product owner who had no idea about the Jira board. 
She had just transitioned from a different role to a product owner. 
I helped her to create stories, coaching her on how we prioritize them and how we get them into the sprint. 
She did learn. I also referred her to some Udemy courses that were able to help her to be 
better off in her role as a product owner. 
The relationship between a scrum master and a product owner is crucial for the success of the team. 
Effective communication, collaboration, and trust are critical. 
As a scrum master, my role is to support the product owner in maintaining a clear prioritized backlog, facilitating scrum ceremonies, and ensuring the team understands the product vision. 
Techniques like user story mapping and tools like digital Kanban boards were utilized to refine backlog management practices and improve value delivery, enhancing the overall collaboration and productivity of the team. 
The scrum master is the conduit between the product owner and the development team. 
The product owner and scrum master really need to work closely together, clarifying product vision and communicating that to the team, ensuring everyone understands the product goals. 
Backlog refinement is an area that I've found particularly important here when the product owner and the team really walk through each of the different user stories and talk about what they're for. 
In facilitating that conversation, I can ensure that everyone is on the same page about what our desired outcome is. 

Using the Force Field Analysis Tool 
Welcome to this video about the force field analysis. 
We will have our expert Matt Verstal show you how to use this tool to make a decision and determine which side of the tool influences the final decision. 
Welcome to this video about the force field analysis. 
What we are going to look at is how to use this tool. 
This is a very powerful tool and a very simple tool to use, because you can visually see the forces that are for a decision or against a decision. 
And that is why we call it the force field analysis. 
And essentially what you're trying to do is, use it to help you make a decision. 
And what we are going to do is go through an example on how to do this. 
The force field analysis should always be conducted with a team. 
You should never have the project manager, the scrum master, the product owner, or anybody doing this in a vacuum or void of any other support. 
You should have your subject matter experts working with you to help you determine a good solution. 
And this is only the beginning. 
The force field analysis should be used in conjunction with other tools in order to help you determine the decision and the direction you go. 
It gives you an opportunity to get some ideas, and then analyse thereafter. 
So let's start by looking at this example. 
If we work at a medical office, and, perhaps, management says that they want to purchase a new capital equipment. 
Perhaps, this is an MRI machine. 
And this tool can be used for anything. 
This is just an example, it doesn't have to be used for purchases. 
It can be used for other big decisions such as a new product development. 
It can be used for moving to a different office building, hiring a new person, as in, we're creating a new position. 
It can be even used for determining some of the strategic initiatives that the organization wants to do over the next couple of years. 
So don't limit yourself on just using it for one thing. 
Now, essentially what we're going to do is, on the left side, we will have the forces or pros of the decision that we want to make. 
On the right side, we're going to have the cons, the things that are, perhaps, pushing up against this decision. 
And in the middle we can state what the decision that we need to make is. 
And the idea is that we're going to see which side is stronger than the other side, and that should influence our decision. 
Now, the length of the arrow and the number of the arrows, well, the number of the arrows determines how many cons are pushing up against the pros. 
The length of the arrow will tell you how strong is issue is. 
And the longer the arrow, the more force that it has to push up against the pros, or vice versa. 
The pros could be much stronger than the cons. 
But you won't know that until you list everything out. 
And essentially, whichever has more force should give you an idea of what the issue that you need, or what side you need to land on with this issue. 
So in this case, we're wanting to purchase an MRI machine. 
We state that it's too expensive. 
We make that arrow pretty long. 
There's a huge backlog with the manufacturer, wouldn't be able to buy it for two years or have delivery until two years. 
We also have a more immediate need. 
We have a CT scanner, so we do that in our office. 
But that thing is ready to be replaced. 
And so we have that here is one of the longest runs. 
There's a pretty big need for that, and we're already offering that service. 
So we want to continue to do that, hence we need to replace the machine. 
Right now we don't have the expertise in-house to run the MRI machine. 
So that is another thing. 
We would have to start from the beginning on forming that expertise. 
And really, right now, space is a constraint. 
However, pushing back on that, we would have, one of the benefits is an increase in patient flow. 
We would be able to streamline some of these patient services without having to 
send them out to a other clinic. 
And then we can expand our scope of services, which obviously makes it better 
because we have more patients that would come to our clinic. 
And we've already identified a bank that is willing to finance us. 
None of these are really as strong of a case for purchasing this MRI machine right now. 
So essentially what happens after this, the team gets together, talks about it, perhaps we bring it up to leadership, and we decide whether we want to move forward with the purchase. 
But that is how the force field analysis is used. 
It's very simple, very straightforward, very visual, and very useful for the team. 
It can be done quickly, and it can be used to explain how we came to a decision or a consensus. 

In this video, you learned that force field analysis tool is simple, straightforward, visual, and useful. 
You can visually see the forces that are for a decision or against a decision. 
You can determine the forces which influence the final decision.

Dealing With Conflict
Conflict and the Team 
Welcome to Conflict and the Team. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to describe the different reasons for conflict in a team, describe the Scrum Master's role in a team conflict, explain the Lecioni five dysfunctions model. 
In a team conflict, is inevitable. 
When a new team forms, members try to figure out their roles and goals, while some assert themselves as leaders. 
They might even have different opinions and form alliances that can result in disagreements. 
The key for the Scrum Master is to control the conflict and prevent it from escalating. 
Disagreements can be good because they can inspire creativity and challenge presumptions 
have done in a controlled environment. 
A good team agreement should set expectations from day one, and answer major questions 
about how the team should work together. 
In addition, the agreement should also include some process questions that could be the sources of conflict. 
For example, it can entail an agreement on the development process flow. 
It remains important for the Scrum Master to prepare for conflict in the team, despite the agreement. 
Several reasons cause teams to experience conflict. 
Differences in personality maybe the root cause of disagreements rather than actions. 
For example, two opinionated extroverts may challenge each other for leadership positions 
or the team's attention. 
It may not even be intentional, but their personalities clash and may cause tension. 
Team members may pick sides or prefer one personality over the other when these disagreements happen. 
Having a burned out team is possible. 
In some cases, team members under a lot of pressure, may be burnt out from a long development effort on the project. 
They may just be ready to move on to something else or work on multiple teams. 
For example, a new team member who has experience working with a different group, 
is familiar with how they execute processes elsewhere. 
This team member may expect all teams to follow a specific method. 
Not focusing on the goal, working too fast, and losing sight of the vision could cause a team to have conflicts. 
If the Scrum Master fails to keep the team focused on the goals, it could cause the team to interpret the vision or backlog items in a way that contradicts how other team members view them. 
Sometimes some team members work quickly to meet deadlines or to finish and move on to a different project or a sprint. 
Trying to complete the work hastily may cause frustration for the other team members working diligently in such situations. 
You may even have non-cooperative team members. 
These team members may have a tough time working on teams. 
Colleagues may find working with such team members challenging and express their concerns to the Scrum Master. 
For example, Tony is on a Scrum team and prefers to work alone. 
He has never expressed this to anyone, but comes across as obtuse, passive, and aggressive. 
Tony does not function well as a team player and prefers to receive direct instructions rather than being delegated tasks. 
Another source of conflict is that the Scrum Master has failed to refocus the team when necessary. 
At the first sign that the team has lost focused or has been frustrated, the Scrum Master should have helped the team to review the agreement and the goals that they are trying to accomplish. 
When a team conflict occurs, the Scrum Master goes into coaching mode and sometimes act as a facilitator. 
The coach evaluates the situation and determines whether the team needs support and direction or whether a different technique will work. 
Their job is to remind the team why they are there and offer them the tools to improve collaboration. 
They may take the group through team-building exercises, review the group's goals, 
or meet with them individually to see how to remove roadblocks. 
The Scrum Master has a supporting role and focuses on the team. 
They use their abilities to be servant leaders and lead by example. 
They avoid pointing fingers and by remaining neutral and looking for the solutions. 
Scrum Masters must be sensitive to signs that the team may experience conflict. 
Sometimes the signs of conflict are not obvious. 
The Lecioni five dysfunctions model illustrates 
the challenges teams face as they work together and develop relationships over time. 
The dysfunctions are, absence of trust, the team members may have difficulty trusting each other, making it difficult to invest in or support each other. 
There may be fear over the other person's intentions. 
Fear of conflict. Some team members want to avoid conflict. 
They adopt an attitude that revolves around simply coming to work, doing their job, and going home. 
However, this mindset leads to a lack of commitment and a reluctance to be creative or grow because they are afraid of expressing disagreement or differing opinions. 
There is a lack of commitment and willingness to be creative and grow in the situation 
because they are afraid to disagree. 
Lack of commitment. 
In some cases, teams want to show that they are neutral and are not obligated to do the work, or they complain about everything. 
Avoidance of accountability. 
Some team members blame others for any faults or mistakes, or never want to take ownership of their work. 
Inattentiveness to team results. 
Some team members do not care about the team performance, but are only concerned about their careers or growth. 
Understanding this model can help the Scrum Master guide the team towards healthy disagreements. 
Healthy disagreements inspire growth and better ideas. 

In this video, you learned disagreement can be good because it can inspire creativity and challenge presumptions have done in a controlled environment. 
A good team agreement should set expectations from day one, and answer any major questions about how the team should work together. 
Reasons for conflict include personality differences, workflow, direction, loss of focus, 
non-cooperation, and failure to refocus. 
During a team conflict, the Scrum Master goes into coaching mode and sometimes becomes a facilitator. 
The Lecioni five dysfunctions model illustrates the challenges teams face as they work 
together and develop relationships over time. 
Expert Viewpoints: Managing Complexity and Change 
Welcome to Expert Viewpoints: 
Managing Complexity and Change. 
In this video, experts share common team, and project change issues and complexities often experienced by Scrum Masters and describe how they successfully manage these issues. 
What are some common issues that a Scrum Master might encounter when there's a change in the team or the project? 
When there is a change in the team or project, common issues a Scrum Master might encounter, such as resistance to change, lack of clarity or alignment, skill gaps, and team members adjusting to new roles and responsibilities. 
It is important for a Scrum Master to address these issues and facilitate a smooth transition. 
Change is inevitable in projects. 
Common areas that a Scrum Master might feel challenged in include starting over with a team change. 
Anytime a new team member comes in, you're essentially have a brand new team. 
It's like a sports team every year. 
The franchise may be the same, but the players are always different. 
Each time you take over a new team, because, remember your teams are formed based on their knowledge, experience and they're sort of self-managed teams, and they may not always work together. 
You may have very strong opinions on both sides of the table. 
Well, your job and something you might encounter very often is to get these two people to collaborate together. 
A Scrum Master may encounter various issues that can impact the dynamics and effectiveness of a Scrum team, including team members or stakeholders being resistant to change, team members changing, new technology that team members do not have a skill in, and a shift in priorities. 
When there are changes on the team, the velocity of the team is jeopardized. 
The new team members must be oriented into the team. 
Skill gaps must be addressed, and ground rules must be shared. 
This can slow down a team until the new team member is oriented. 
The big barrier and challenge is this. 
Launch planning revolves around velocity projections. 
The Scrum Master needs to integrate the new team members as quickly as possible while still maintaining projected launch schedules. 
Can you provide an example of a barrier or blocker you encountered and how you overcame it? 
I have definitely run into situations where there's been barriers and blockers. 
One example is working on a development project in the automotive industry. 
We had some quality issues and really weeks before the release, we had to go find a new supplier and get them up to speed and get some prototypes built in a hurry, 12, 13, 15 weeks out. 
But I'm able to pivot quickly when I need to. 
We're able to do that with the supplier and work with a different supplier to at least get prototypes done. 
We overcame it by collaboration, quick thinking, and again, leading people to find those answers. 
Teams go through different stages of maturity, often referred to as forming, storming, norming, and performing. 
The goal is to get to that performing stage where everything is humming along wonderfully. 
When there's a change, say changes to the makeup of the team or to the work, you'll usually slide back to the forming and storming phases, where there tends to be a lot more conflict. 
When this happens with teams I've worked with, I'll have us revisit our team agreements and see if there's any changes we need to make, as well as work through some more team-building exercises to rebuild trust and harmony. 
I encountered a barrier when the team faced a sudden change in requirements during a sprint. 
To overcome it, I facilitated a collaborative discussion with the product owner and the stakeholders to prioritize the new items. 
We then worked together to reprioritize the backlog, adjust the sprint plan, and ensure that we had necessary support to deliver the revised scope successfully. 
A barrier that I have encountered as a Scrum Master many times is a team member being resistant to change. 
After recognizing this, I made sure to involve them in all decision-making I could to allow them to contribute and be a part of shaping the change. 
This helped to create a shared vision. 

Recognizing Dependencies 

Welcome to how to recognize dependencies. 
After watching this video, you'll be able to describe the impact of dependencies on the team and the organization, describe the reasons for dependencies, describe the different techniques used to mitigate dependencies, list the guidelines to handle dependencies. 
The ideal situation for scrum teams is to move forward without waiting for someone else to finish their work. 
For example, if you have 10 tasks to complete and they are all independent, they could be finished without depending on someone or something to complete them first. 
However, in a real life situation, dependencies often cause delays. 
Dependencies occur when one task is waiting on something else to finish before it can proceed. 
In a team, a team member may need to wait for some item from another team member, 
which would be an internal dependency. 
You can also see external dependencies where a team member is waiting for a deliverable from another team member. 
For example, Jack is working on a project and to complete the development and testing, 
a new server must first be installed by his teammates, Tony. 
This is a case of an internal dependency. 
In another case, Greg has developed a new game, but to finally deliver it to the client, 
he needs to final approval from QA, which is a different department. 
This is an example of an external dependency. 
Few or no dependencies is better because it mitigates the risk of any delays in the team. 
When a sprint concludes and the team fails to complete the work because it's waiting on someone or some thing, the work cannot become increments. 
The product owner will not approve of the work because it is not complete, it will end up on the backlog for another sprint. 
The impact of this can be significant because it could delay important launch dates, be the source of stress for the team, product owner, and may impact the organization as a whole. 
Dependencies occur for various reasons. 
Perhaps the team lacks the skill set to progress forward. 
This is similarly why the company outsources, or you hire a contractor to work on 
your home or take a vehicle to a mechanic. 
In Scrum, you might need extra resources to finish a Sprint, so you add a new team member. 
You may also experience a situation where a team member on another team has the required expertise, so you pull in this person. 
Another scenario may be the way the your teams are structured. 
Rather than a working team where it is cross-functional, you have teams working in silos. 
For example, the coders are in one group, testers in another, and QA in another, 
they do not experience integration. 
Rather than building an integrated and collaborative group, communication and partnership is inhibited because they never see themselves as one unit. 
Massive backlogs can also be an issue. 
If the backlog grows out of control, it will inevitably create dependencies. 
For example, Wyatt, who was the product owner, is constantly adding to the backlog. 
He believes all the new features will make the product better and the customer will be excited. 
Dependencies should be mitigated, managed, or eliminated if possible. 
First, you need to understand where the dependencies exist. 
If you cannot visualize where they are, you will not know when they come up. 
Once you have a grasp on where they are, you can start to employ tools to limit the impact on the team. 
As the Scrum Master, you must ensure everyone understands the process. 
A key technique is regular communication with the team and adequate training. 
A team who lacks understanding of their impacts on other parts of the team and the project will fail to see how a dependency has a negative impact. 
Simplification in all areas as another key technique. 
For example, suppose you have a simple team structure that is integrated and cross-functional, in that case, you have a higher chance of collaboration, better communication, and visibility to what each team member is doing, eliminating the risk of a dependency popping up. 
Another tool of simplification is ensure that the team has adequate resources that they need to complete their work. 
When your team fails to complete what is on the backlog and it appears in multiple sprints, 
it can create dependencies. 
Ensure you have adequate collaboration, communication, and the right skills on the team. 
A Scrum of Scrums technique can be utilized where members of the team work together to manage the dependencies, and there is a concentrated effort to limit the impact. 
For example, they might guard against items that do not belong in the sprint from creeping in. 
They keep each other accountable to the backlog items. 
The Scrum Master should focus on making sure that the team is able to push increments through by getting to the definition of Done. 
The product owner should be proactive in making sure that there are some foresight into what the priorities are for future sprints. 
There should be a concentrated effort to do thorough backlog refinement as well. 
Breaking down or decomposing your backlog items can provide visibility to dependencies. 
There should be only one voice from the product owner. 
Having multiple product owner voices will surely create dependencies. 
Finally, when you have external partners, ensure that they are onboard with your delivery dates. 
Without this communication, any delays might cause issues. 

In this video, you learned that dependencies occur when one task is waiting on something else to finish before it can proceed. 
There can be internal as well as external dependencies. 
Few or no dependency is better because it mitigates the risk of delays in the team. 
Various techniques to mitigate delays due to dependencies include communication, simplification and Scrum. 

Evaluating and Responding to Team Interactions 
The Scrum Master should be acutely aware of how the team interacts. You should constantly assess and evaluate how the team is working together and address even the slightest suspicion of disfunction within the team. This disfunction is different than a simple disagreement. Some disagreement is reasonable as it inspires creativity and new ideas. However, the team's difficulty with its function indicates a deeper issue within the team.
There are signs to look for when evaluating team health. For example, do you notice a change in behavior with your team? Are they showing up to meetings late? Do they display visible signs of frustration? An unhealthy environment could still be brewing because a team member has held off on approaching you about an issue. Eric shows up late daily and is becoming disconnected from the team. You find out he is putting in about twenty hours of overtime per week because he is working on another project for his boss. He has become frustrated, and the team thinks he is mad at them. 
There are a few key factors to look for when assessing the team. The most objective component is the team agreement. If the team fails to carry out the agreed upon behaviors and rules they established, they should review it again with the team. Use this as your guide to ensure everyone is on the same page. For example, Paul is a new team member who thinks the agreement wastes time. He states that his job is to get the work done, and he is unconcerned about what the rest of the team does. He just wants to know what his role is and move on. 
Next, use project progress to determine how the team works together. Other factors like the agreement should always accompany this, however. If the team is struggling to meet the definition of done, perhaps there is a lack of communication or areas where they lack clarity. Perhaps a team member has made a change without communicating with the rest of the team. An example might be, you notice that the team has become disengaged during the stand-up meeting. Roger starts saying that he has nothing to report each day. You notice that Roger has started using a different tool to finish his work. Carrie, who has the next step in the process, is confused because she expects Roger's work to look different. Neither of them have communicated with each other as stated in the agreement. Roger kept quiet because he knew they would have expected him to report out, but he wanted to experiment with a new method. 
In some cases, the clues may lack an obvious cue. For example, you notice Kyle rolling his eyes whenever Alexander reports out. Kyle maintains his cool when other team members speak, but the dynamic between Alexander and Kyle has deteriorated. 
Responding to these situations requires the Scrum Master to employ Servant Leadership and coaching techniques, like supporting, delegating, and directing. You may also receive a request to facilitate the process and be more involved to get the team back on track. 
Navigating Change 
Welcome to this module on how to Navigate Change Management. 
After watching this Video, you'll be able to: describe how changes occur, describe the different models used for change management, describe how to evaluate the change and respond. 
Change is a natural occurrence in business scrum projects included. 
You must arrange and navigate change to minimize the impact on the team. 
Change can happen in the requirements, organization, process, team, and so on. 
What is important to the scrum team is how they navigate through the change. 
There are two responses available to the team, accept the change or resist it. 
The consequences of these decisions can either create a positive outcome or may have damaging effects on the team. 
Imagine a scenario where the organization decides that scrum teams now must report 
weekly progress to the managers or something drastic where the product focus transitions 
from hardware to software. 
The first scenario represents a minor change, but the second is a major change that can be complicated. 
Changes can also occur when an organization adopt scrum where project management methods had ruled the scenario. 
The outcomes can be substantial depending on how the team reacts. 
Several models may be employed when it comes to change management, every popular lean approach is the plan, do, check, act model. 
The approach offers the opportunity for the team to evaluate and plan for change, make a move, see if it works, and then recalibrate. 
This model can be applied to just about every scenario. 
It provides a process for evaluation, is simple, and has a history of being effective. 
Think of it as an ongoing process. 
This method has no end, it repeats itself, so you are constantly monitoring the situation. 
The ADKAR model may also be applied. 
This is an acronym for awareness, desire, knowledge, action, and reinforcement. 
This allows teams to analyze the situation and understand the need for change in the awareness phase and the desire phase, the team starts to gain acceptance from others and get people on their side. 
You may need to help people understand the situation. 
Scrum masters may also need to address others apprehensions about accepting the change. 
In the knowledge phase, you get the skills and information you need to make a successful transition. 
In the action phase, you employ everything you have studied up to this point. 
Finally, reinforcement means you do the lessons learned type activities and seek confirmation from others. 
Another popular application is the Kotter's model. 
In this method, you create a sense of urgency. 
This means you persuade people to take your work seriously. 
You gain acceptance that the situation requires everyone's attention. 
Then you gain support and build a coalition phase. 
Next you have to turn it into a strategy by creating a vision of a future state, 
then everyone must buy in or you will not have the support needed to succeed. 
Remove any issues or roadblocks if required. 
You can start to execute and gain wins even if they are short term. 
That will eventually lead to future success. 
You should keep the momentum going and gain velocity. 
Finally you implement change. 
With the use of any of these models, you should assess the situation before deciding how to respond. 
Your evaluation should be data-driven and comprehensive. 
In addition, collaborative leadership should support the vision and the mission that you're trying to achieve. 
Responses to change should not be done in a vacuum. 
Know your team and yourself. 
You should have a pulse on how you respond to change. 
Try not to face the situation head-on and know that even small wins count. 
This can positively impact your team and keep them energized for the next situation. 

In this video you learned that you must manage and navigate change to minimize the impact on the team, change can happen in the requirements, organization, process, team, and so on. 
The different models used in change management are Lean, ADKAR and Kotter. 
Your evaluation of change management should be data-driven and comprehensive before you respond. 
Module 4 Summary: Navigating Complexity and Change
Congratulations! You have completed this module. In this module, you learned that:
* An impediment refers to any obstacle or barrier that obstructs or slows down progress.
* Unattended impediments and blockers hinder progress, limit capabilities, and erode team morale.
* Stakeholders significantly influence projects, programs, and development efforts.
* The Product Owner has the final authority to determine the development direction and decide how to incorporate stakeholder recommendations.
* Facilitating stakeholder participation and understanding their preferred mode of interaction is important roles of a Scrum Master.
* Establishing a clear team agreement from day one helps set expectations and guide team collaboration.
* Conflict can arise due to personality differences, divergent workflow directions, loss of focus, lack of cooperation, and failure to realign.
* The Lencioni 5 Dysfunctions Model illustrates the challenges teams face as they collaborate and build relationships.
* Effective change management minimizes the impact of changes on the team.
* Common change management models include Lean, ADKAR, and Kotter.
* Dependencies occur when one task must wait for another to finish before proceeding.
* Techniques such as communication, simplification, and Scrum help mitigate delays caused by dependencies.
Reading: Module 4 Glossary: Navigating Complexity and Change
Welcome! This alphabetized glossary contains many terms you'll find in this course. This comprehensive glossary also includes additional industry-recognized terms not used in course videos. These terms are essential for you to recognize when working in the industry, participating in user groups, andparticipating in other certificate programs.
TermsDefinitionVideoADKAR modelA change management model that focuses on the five key elements of successful change: Awareness, Desire, Knowledge, Action, and Reinforcement. It provides a structured approach to managing individual and organizational change.Navigating ChangeBacklogA prioritized list of items, usually user stories or features, that need to be completed by the team. The backlog serves as a source of work for the team to select from during sprint planning.Recognizing DependenciesBacklog RefinementA collaborative activity in Scrum where the product backlog items are reviewed, clarified, and estimated. It helps ensure that the backlog items are well-defined, understood by the team, and ready for selection during sprint planning.Recognizing DependenciesBurnoutA state of physical or emotional exhaustion resulting from prolonged stress or overwork. Team members experiencing burnout may feel depleted and lose motivation, leading to conflicts or disengagement from the team.Conflict and the TeamChange managementThe process of planning, implementing, and controlling changes within an organization to minimize the negative impacts and maximize the benefits of the change.Navigating ChangeCollaborative leadershipA leadership approach that emphasizes collaboration, cooperation, and involvement of team members in decision-making and problem-solving. It promotes open communication, trust, and shared accountability.Navigating ChangeDaily ScrumA short daily meeting in Scrum where team members synchronize their work, discuss progress, and identify any impediments. The Daily Scrum helps ensure transparency, alignment, and quick problem resolution within the team.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsData-driven evaluationAn evaluation process that relies on data and objective information to assess the impact and effectiveness of a change. It involves collecting, analyzing, and interpreting relevant data to inform decision-making.Navigating ChangeDependencyA situation where one task, team member, or deliverable is reliant on another task, team member, or deliverable to be completed before it can proceed. Dependencies can be internal (within the team) or external (involving other teams or departments).Recognizing DependenciesDifficult stakeholdersStakeholders who may pose challenges or obstacles to the project due to their demands, conflicting interests, or attempts to exert excessive control. Managing difficult stakeholders requires strategic handling and appropriate communication.Dealing with Difficult StakeholdersExternal dependencyA dependency that involves other teams, departments, or external partners, where one team member is waiting for a deliverable or approval from an external entity before proceeding.Recognizing DependenciesForce evaluationBy comparing the strength of the pros and cons, the analysis helps determine which side has more force. This information can influence the final decision.Using the Force Field Analysis ToolImpedimentAnything that obstructs or hinders the progress of a Scrum team. Impediments can arise from various sources, both internal and external, and can impact the team's ability to deliver value.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsInternal dependencyA dependency that occurs within the same team, where one team member is waiting for another team member to complete a task or provide a necessary item before proceeding.Recognizing DependenciesKotter's modelA change management model developed by John Kotter that consists of eight stages, including creating a sense of urgency, building a guiding coalition, developing and communicating a vision, empowering action, creating short-term wins, consolidating the gains and anchoring the change in the organization's culture.Navigating ChangeLencioni's 5 Dysfunctions ModelA model proposed by Patrick Lencioni that describes common dysfunctions that hinder team performance. The dysfunctions include absence of trust, fear of conflict, lack of commitment, avoidance of accountability, and inattentiveness to team results.Conflict and the TeamPlan-Do-Check-Act (PDCA) modelA continuous improvement cycle that involves planning for change, implementing the change, evaluating the results, and making adjustments accordingly. It is commonly used in Lean and other quality management approachesNavigating ChangeProduct OwnerThe role responsible for maximizing the value of the product and managing the product backlog. The Product Owner works closely with the Scrum Master and the development team to ensure that the product meets the needs and expectations of the stakeholders.Recognizing DependenciesPulse on changeHaving awareness and understanding of how individuals and teams respond to change. It involves monitoring the reactions, concerns, and progress of individuals and the team during the change process.Navigating ChangeRetrospective meetingA Scrum event held at the end of each sprint to reflect on the team's performance, identify improvement opportunities, and make adjustments. Stakeholders who are not part of the Scrum team should not join this meeting.Dealing with Difficult StakeholdersScrumAn agile project management framework that emphasizes collaboration, iterative development, and delivering value incrementally. It focuses on the team's collective effort and adaptability to achieve project goals.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsScrum MasterThe facilitator and servant-leader of a Scrum team. The Scrum Master is responsible for ensuring that the Scrum framework is understood and followed, removing impediments, and fostering a productive and collaborative team environment.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsScrum of ScrumsA technique used when multiple teams are working together on a project. Representatives from each team come together regularly to discuss progress, dependencies, and coordination, aiming to manage inter-team dependencies effectively.Recognizing DependenciesServant leaderA leadership style that emphasizes serving and supporting the needs of the team members. The Scrum Master acts as a servant leader, providing guidance, removing obstacles, and facilitating the team's growth and collaboration.Conflict and the TeamSprintA time-boxed iteration in Scrum typically lasts 2-4 weeks during which a potentially shippable product increment is developed. Sprints provide a structured timeframe for achieving project goals and delivering value.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsSprint RetrospectiveA Scrum event held at the end of each sprint to reflect on the team's performance, identify improvement opportunities, and make adjustments for future sprints. The retrospective helps the team address impediments and blockers and enhance their effectiveness.Identifying Organizational ImpedimentsSprint ReviewA Scrum event where the development team presents the completed work to stakeholders and gathers feedback. It provides an opportunity for stakeholders to evaluate progress and make recommendations.Dealing with Difficult StakeholdersStakeholdersIndividuals or groups who have an interest or influence in a project, program, or development effort. They can be internal or external to the organization and play a crucial role in its success.Dealing with Difficult StakeholdersTeam agreementA document or understanding among team members that outlines expectations, goals, and ways of working together. It helps establish a common understanding and can address potential sources of conflict within the team.Conflict and the TeamTechnical debtThe concept of taking shortcuts or compromising the quality of code or technical implementation to meet immediate deadlines, which may result in the need for future rework or added complexity.Identifying Organizational Impediments
Review of Previous Modules and the Scrum Process
The previous four modules covered the flow of the Scrum Process. Module 1 starts with explaining each step of the Scrum Process. It begins with the Product Owner's vision that emerges through Epics and User Stories. The Product Owner then builds a Product Backlog, which is the first of the three Scrum Artifacts. In its current state, it is highly unlikely that the team will possess a clear understanding of how to develop it. Some parts of the Product Backlog may be too large or even qualify as independent projects. It typically requires decomposition or breaking down. 
The team plays an important role in the process. They need to review, update, and make changes to the Backlog in its raw state to ensure its effectiveness. The process is also known as refinement. The purpose of refinement is to make it work for the team to develop without altering the Product Owner's vision. The team will have a difficult time developing the Sprint Backlog, the second artifact if they do not follow the refinement process. 
The Scrum Master facilitates this process at the end ensuring that the team establishes an effective framework for constructing their Sprint Backlog. Once the Sprint Backlog is agreed upon, the team is ready to start Sprint Planning. The team builds their backlog and determines how long it will take them to complete the work. The Product Owner does not get to weigh in on this. It is the Scrum Master's job to make sure that the Product Owner and Stakeholders avoid interfering. 
While the team works through the backlog, they have regularly scheduled meetings that the Scrum Master facilitates. These meetings are commonly referred to as Daily Scrums or Stand-up meetings. Typically, the Scrum Master facilitates these by asking three questions. First, what did you work on yesterday? Second, what are you working on today? And the third is, what are your roadblocks or blockers? The objective of these questions is to foster effective communication and allow the Scrum Master to assess the team's requirements and well-being.
At the end of the Sprint, the Product Owner reviews the work to see if it meets the Definition of Done. This definition must be agreed upon at the beginning it is the responsibility of the Product Owner to evaluate the work's progression towards becoming an Increment, which is the third Scrum Artifact. Once the Product Owner decides if the work meets the Definition of Done, the team again meets to conduct a Sprint Retrospective. Only the team participates in the Retrospective with the Scrum Master. The Product Owner and stakeholders are not invited to these meetings. 
Following the Scrum process is just one aspect of the responsibilities carried out by a Scrum Master. Being an effective Scrum Master starts with being fit for the role. A good Scrum Master is someone who upholds the values and can see the importance of teamwork over processes or customer over requirements. 
Let us now explore what each module entails.
Module 1 delves into the concept of the Agile Manifesto. Scrum Masters demonstrate compassion, indicating their commitment to the team's success. This should drive their work and be the reason for pursuing this profession. 
It also supports the concepts covered in Module 2, the Scrum Master as Facilitator and Coach. As a facilitator and coach, Scrum Master manages stakeholders, promotes collaboration, helps the team to prioritize facilitating a process, and sets them up for success. They ensure the integrity of the Scrum framework and protect the team from outside influence. They utilize situational leadership techniques, such as directing, delegating, supporting, and coaching. They are able to assess a situation and employ the appropriate technique that will best serve the team. 
One of the initial responsibilities undertaken by the Scrum Master to support the team is to form an agreement. The agreement is essentially a contract or guideline the team agrees to follow that defines their internal processes and creates an understanding of what it means for the team to respect each other. 
Module 3 covers what it means to be a Servant Leader. Being a Servant Leader is a crucial aspect of the Scrum Master's role, as they hold a leadership position that is primarily functional rather than authoritative. Their leadership centers around their capability to assist the team and drive its success.
Module 4 introduces tools for the Scrum Master to remove impediments and signs to look for if the team is facing difficulty. There are common models for them to rely on. For example, one of the most famous is the Plan, Do, Check, Act model. The model allows the team to focus on creating a good plan, then implementing it, and evaluating it to see if it requires adjustments. 
The Path to Certification: PMI-ACP, DASM, and A-CSM Certification Exam
There are three popular Agile and Scrum credentials that can enhance your career: A-CSM, DASM, and PMI-ACP. We will also provide an overview of additional project management certifications that this course will assist you in achieving.
Certified Scrum Master (CSM)
Certified Scrum Master (CSM) is an entry-level Scrum certification administered through the Scrum Alliance. CSM is one of the most popular and recognized Scrum certifications you can achieve. A CSM certification acknowledges your ability to gain a solid understanding of Scrum and learn practical ways to apply it to your team and throughout your organization.
To qualify for CSM, you need to:
1. Attend an in-person 16-hour Certified Scrum Master course (CSM(r)) taught by a Certified Scrum Trainer(r) (CST(r)). The cost of training ranges from $700 to $1200 US dollars. The majority of training is conducted online.
2. Pass the online CSM(r) exam by correctly answering at least 37 out of 50 questions within the 1-hour time limit. The test is administered online and is not proctored or held in an in-person location.
3. After passing the CSM test, you must accept the CSM License Agreement and complete your Scrum Alliance membership profile.
Here is more information from the Scrum Alliance Website:
"The CSM test contains 50 multiple-choice questions based on the CSM Learning Objectives, and candidates will have one hour to complete it. The passing score for the test is 74%. You cannot pause the test, but you can bookmark questions to answer later during the 60-minute time limit. For more information about which types of topics and questions are included in the CSM test, please refer to our CSM Test Specifications Outline. It also serves as a great study guide!
After you receive the initial welcome email from Scrum Alliance that contains the account activation link, you will have 90 days to complete the CSM test for free. Students are also given two free attempts to pass the CSM test. If you fail the test twice or complete it after 90 days, you will be prompted to pay a $25 USD test attempt fee.
The CSM test is available to be taken in English, Spanish (Castilian), Portuguese, French, Italian, Latin American Spanish, German, Japanese, Simplified Chinese, Danish, Czech, Polish, and Russian."
Disciplined Agile Scrum Master (DASM)
DASM is an entry-level Scrum certification administered through the Project Management Institute (PMI). This PMI certification is relatively new and rapidly gaining popularity. To quote PMI, "The DASM certification equips you to successfully lead Agile teams, thereby future-proofing your career in a world in which Agile is fast becoming the way forward."
To qualify for DASM, you need to:
1. Complete a DASM course from a registered trainer. Courses are available using a virtual led two-day training option, or you may opt for a self-paced course. You must complete the training satisfactorily before you are eligible to take the DASM test. Costs for training range from $399 for the online self-paced course to $800.
2. Pass a test. According to PMI, "The DASM exam is administered through Pearson VUE. The exam is an online, non-proctored test including 50 multiple-choice questions. The total duration of the exam appointment is 120 minutes-30 minutes for the introduction, tutorial, and survey and up to 90 minutes for completing the exam." PMI has not provided a passing score for the DASM exam. However, a passing score is generally thought to be about 80%. Test costs are included with your training costs.
DASM is now available in Spanish and Portuguese.
Agile Certified Practitioner (ACP)
Here is the PMI overview of the ACP certification: "The PMI-ACP certification recognizes an individual's expertise in using Agile practices in their projects while demonstrating their increased professional versatility through Agile tools and techniques. In addition, the PMI-ACP certification holds a higher level of professional credibility as it requires a combination of Agile training, experience working on Agile projects, and an examination on Agile principles, practices, tools, and techniques. This global certification also supports individuals in meeting the needs of organizations that rely on project practitioners to apply a diversity of methods to their project management."
To qualify for PMI-ACP, you must submit a resume to PMI that shares the following:
1. General Project Experience: You must be able to show 12 months of experience over the last five years working on project teams if you do not possess a bachelor's degree or equivalent. If you have a degree or a current PMI project management certification, this experience requirement is waived.
2. Agile Project Experience: You must be able to show 8 months of Agile team experience. If you do not have a degree, this experience must be within the past three years.
3. Training in Agile Practices: If you do not have a degree, you require 21 hours of Agile-related training. If you have a degree, you require 35 hours of general training.
Once the resume is approved, you can schedule your test. A PMI member costs $435, and a non-member is charged $495.
To earn the PMI-ACP(r) certification, you must meet experience and education requirements and pass the PMI-ACP exam. This is a 120-question, multiple-choice exam. This is an online proctored examination. You are allotted three hours to complete the test.
You can prepare for and take the PMI-ACP exam in English, Arabic, Japanese, Korean, Portuguese, and Spanish.
This course will also help you if you are interested in attaining PMI-CAPM or PMI-PMP certification.

Expert Viewpoints: Advice for Aspiring Scrum Masters 
Welcome to Expert Viewpoints, should you become a Scrum Master? 
In this video, we will hear Agile and Scrum experts discuss who should pursue the job of Scrum Master, some of the challenges Scrum Masters encounter, and how they overcame these challenges. 
The job of a Scrum Master is ideally suited for people who have a genuine interest and passion for Agile principles in the Scrum framework. 
Those who have strong communication skills, have a servant leadership mindset, possess strong facilitation and coaching skills, love solving problems, and of course, are a continuous learner. 
Some of the common challenges a Scrum Master faces are an organization or team members who are resistant to change or just lack the Agile experience or 
understanding. 
Sometimes there's different personalities on a Scrum team that can present challenges. 
Scaling Agile can present challenges and sometimes you can lack empowerment or support from management and stakeholders. 
To overcome these challenges, you could conduct workshops or training sessions to share the benefits of Agile and Scrum. 
You can be a role model for your team, you can have effective facilitation techniques to encourage open communication among team members, and you can engage stakeholders early and often. 
And of course, seek support from fellow Scrum Masters in your organization. 
Someone who has worked in a software development environment. 
Someone who has a passion for working with teams that deliver value. 
Someone who has project management experience, someone who's just overall passionate about agility. 
If this is you, then I definitely advise that you can pursue a career as a Scrum Master. 
In my opinion, the greatest challenge is knowing where to start, so what courses to take, how to go about them. 
And finally, when you figure that out and you have a job, it's finding a team that will accept you as a new Scrum Master. 
Especially if this team is used to having a very seasoned Scrum Master as part of them, that can be a huge challenge. 
The way I overcame these challenges is first of all by understanding that I didn't know the process, I didn't know what's going on and putting in all the work. 
So I took so many courses on Udemy, I watched YouTube videos, I read so many books, a lot. 
I took a lot of time to invest in this because I knew that this was something that I was passionate about. 
And when I joined my first team, first of all, I made them to understand that I'm here to support them and not to control them, that is very important. 
Then I made sure to do my one on ones, make them to understand that I don't know the process, but I'm here to understand the process, here to learn, so that together we can all succeed and be valuable to our customer. 
To me this is simple, anyone who loves to coach with a team and stakeholders. 
If you're into the idea of building successful teams, then being a Srum Master is a perfect opportunity to do that. 
You have lots of chances to help a team who's struggling, help a team who's frustrated, help a team who is about to give up, who may not even get along with each other. 
If you're into fixing those type of issues, then you may be very successful as a Scrum Master, especially if you're good at it. 
If you're into solving complex problems, Scrum Master is a great opportunity to do that. 
If you like helping support discouraged team members, you'll do lots of that as a Scrum Master. 
You'll encounter all kinds of challenges, a little bit of everything. 
You may find some unmotivated employees, someone who thinks that their way is the best, 
a team of people who are experiencing groupthink. 
You will see all kinds of different motives from your team members. 
You'll find that teams don't even want to work together. 
And an example would be a team who cannot agree on how to complete their tasks. 
Maybe it's testing or development or some technical detail. 
You'll encounter those things and they may or may not look at you for guidance, but nevertheless, it is your responsibility to figure out those problems. 
It's simple, be true to the Scrum process, it works. 
Now, you may need to adapt to the organizational culture and things that feel natural to the team, but stay true to the framework, be true to the tools and techniques that you know that work. 
Coaching, supporting, delegating, directing, you will need to do all those different things. 
Be sensitive to what the team is telling you, this is a really key point. 
They may not even verbally tell you directly, but you may hear it in their communication, their tone of voice, their interactions with each other, how they're participating in the meetings, how they're working with each other, the rate of the tasks that they're completing, the way they're doing their work. 
You have to keep an ear to the ground and be sensitive to all those different types of indicators. 
A good Scrum Master is an enabler, supports and coaches the team, so isn't going to get much recognition. 
So if you need the spotlight, this probably isn't for you. 
If you like helping people, are a good listener, and can handle uncertainty, you'll do fine. 
Another skill you'll need is to be able to influence without authority. 
It's much easier to be able to say do it because I told you to, but that won't work here. 
So my favorite way to influence is to ask a lot of questions, tease out why decisions were made and maybe what other options they considered. 
This is very useful in coaching.

Scrum Master Capstone
Course Introduction
Welcome to the Scrum Master Capstone course. 
Congratulations on reaching this pivotal moment in your Scrum Master journey. 
Get ready to take your skills and knowledge as a scrum master to the next level with this Capstone course. 
In this course, you will apply many of the skills learned in previous courses, by playing the role of a Scrum Master. 
This capstone course is part of both the IBM IT Scrum Master Professional Certificate 
and the SkillUp Scrum Master Specialization. 
In this course, you will encounter real-world Scrum scenarios and engage in hands-on labs, 
building a portfolio of artifacts to demonstrate Scrum Master's expertise. 
You submit these artifacts in the final module for a peer-review project. 
Each module also contains a graded quiz to help you prepare for certification exams. 
Let us review what you can expect to learn, and the tasks you will perform in each of this course's six modules. 
In Module 1, you will initiate a project and identify the project stakeholders. 
During this initiation, you review an Agile project charter and explore a product roadmap for 
a commuter support business application. 
In the hands-on labs for this module, you perform a Stacey Analysis and develop a working agreement with your team. 
Next, in Module 2, you develop a product backlog, user stories for the backlog, and create the product vision to prepare for your first sprint. 
Labs include developing an agile risk register, with the force-field analysis tool. 
In another lab, you calculate velocity by estimating story points for your user stories. 
In the third module, you create the Sprint 1 backlog by developing a burndown chart 
and Kanban board with the user stories you created in Module 2. 
You reflect on the sprint in the second lesson by making recommendations for improving 
daily Scrum meetings and determining required actions following the sprint demo. 
You conduct a sprint retrospective in Module 4, and recommend changes for upcoming Sprints 2 and 3, based on those results. 
You have the opportunity to close out Sprint 1, develop your game plan for sprints 2 and 3, 
and update your Kanban board accordingly. 
Module 5, you launched the MVP. 
You analyze the first two sprints, including the launch burndown chart and customer feedback. 
You develop an execution plan for Sprint 3 in part by summarizing end-to-end product launch and final demo feedback. 
For the final module, you submit your final project by selecting specific achievements to share with your peers. 
Additionally, you assess their work with a provided rubric. A bonus lesson encourages you and guides you through developing a personal portfolio, including the assets you developed from this course. Welcome to the Capstone course. 
We are thrilled to have you join us on this learning journey.
