Module 1 Overview and Activity Requirements
Welcome to Module 1 of the IT Scrum Master Professional Certificate (PC) Capstone Course. This course allows you to apply Scrum Master skills, tools, and techniques you have learned in prior courses within the IT Scrum Master PC program. 
Module 1 introduces project initiation activities, including project initiation, stakeholder identification, and subsequent stakeholder and team engagement and management. The following is an overview of Module 1.
* Lesson 1 begins with a review of an Agile Project Charter and Product Roadmap. These documents form the foundation for various activities in the course. You and your team will work to develop a business application (App) for the Mega City Department of Transportation (DOT). Traffic congestion has increased to a level where the DOT wants to provide an App that Mega City users can use to plan and manage their daily commutes effectively. The Project Charter and Project Roadmap will define the specifics, key goals, and objectives.
* Next, we will share a method known as Stacey Analysis, which determines the optimal method for planning and executing project objectives. Stacey's Analysis suggests implementing objectives using either traditional project management methods or adaptive Scrum methods, depending on the situation. The Mega City Commuter App project is a hybrid project. Your team will evaluate the objectives in the Project Charter and apply a Stacey Analysis in a hands-on lab to determine which aspects of the project to accomplish using Scrum. 
* Lesson 1 concludes with a seven-question practice test simulating questions you may encounter on numerous certification tests, including the Certified Scrum Master (CSM) exam.
* In Lesson 2, our focus shifts to the Scrum team. We will provide reading materials outlining the Scrum Master's role in identifying, engaging, coaching, and mentoring the team. You will then meet the members of your team.
* A significant tool used by a Scrum Master to engage, coach, and mentor the team is the development of a "Working Agreement." We will review a working agreement and its components. As the Scrum Master, you will develop a working agreement for your team in a hands-on lab.
* Lesson 2 ends with a graded seven-question quiz covering key concepts in this module. This quiz mirrors the types of questions found in various certification tests, including the Certified Scrum Master (CSM) certification.
* Lastly, a Glossary of terms and a brief module summary will prepare you for the next module.
Enjoy Module 1, and welcome to the course. Your completed Stacey analysis and working agreement will be part of your portfolio, highlighting your project management expertise and readiness for more significant project management responsibilities.
Review the Contents and Purpose of a Project Charter
Welcome to review the contents and purpose of a Project Charter. 
After watching this video, you will be able to recognize the role of a project charter in 
traditional and Agile project management. 
Explain how an Agile project charter guides teams, aligns stakeholders, and ensure success, 
and analyze an Agile project charter template. 
At the start of a project, there's a key document called the project charter. 
It's an essential document that lays the groundwork for the project. 
As defined by the Project Management Institute, PMI, a project charter initiates a project, 
outlines it's high level goals, and authorizes its existence. 
Whether the project follows a traditional approach or embraces Agile and Scrum methodologies, the project charter remains a crucial starting point. 
Now, let's discuss the two main project charters, traditional and Agile. 
The traditional charter offers a high level overview of a project. 
It is approved by a sponsor who authorizes the existence of a project and grants the project manager the authority to use organizational resources. 
Contrastingly, the Agile project charter takes a more focused approach. 
It zeros in on the project's objectives, serving as a guiding document to keep the team 
on course and ensuring it doesn't stray from its purpose. 
The Agile project charter uses a specific set of objectives that are crucial for its effectiveness. 
This document aligns the expectations of stakeholders with the Scrum team's capabilities, 
forging a strong link between project intent and technical execution. 
Its principal goal is establishing clear objectives and bridging the gap between business aspirations and technical implementations. 
Now, let's look at a practical Agile project charter template. 
While not universally standardized, this template draws inspiration from leading Agile and Scrum organizations. 
The product vision answers the question, why the project exists and outlines the specific problem it aims to solve. 
It provides a concise overview of the project's purpose and the issue it seeks to address. 
The mission statement defines the what of the project and outlines the deliverables that 
contribute to achieving the product vision. 
It also describes the outcomes that this project will produce to support the realization of the product vision. 
The key deliverables clearly outline the measurable results that must be accomplished through the projects efforts. 
The key performance indicators, KPI, identify quantifiable measures of performance 
that will serve as indicators of the project's success. 
These metrics provide a way to track progress and ensure alignment with the project's goals. 
Some other categories that an Agile project charter includes are, the timeline provides a comprehensive schedule indicating the deadlines for completing key deliverables. 
You may also utilize a product roadmap to offer a clear timeline for various project phases. 
The budget presents an overview of the sources of funding and provides financial guidance for the project. 
Understanding budgetary limitations is crucial for making well informed decisions 
throughout the project's life cycle. 
The assumptions and constraints section compiles a list of assumptions that represent beliefs needing validation and identifies constraints which are limiting factors requiring consideration and management. 
Some other crucial categories that an Agile project charter includes are, risks, identify potential opportunities or threats that could impact the achievement of the project's deliverables. 
The development team lists all team members participating in the project and defines 
their respective roles and responsibilities. 
This may be supplemented with a RACI chart, providing clarity on who is responsible, 
accountable, consulted, and informed for each task. Lastly, the management team lists 
the primary stakeholders who will be influenced by the project and specifies their roles. 
By crafting a thoughtful Agile project charter, you can lay a robust foundation for project accomplishment and stakeholder satisfaction in the dynamic world of Agile project management. 

In this video, you learned that, the project charter is a vital document at the projects beginning, outlining its purpose and goals. 
It authorizes the project's existence and applies to traditional and Agile/Scrum approaches. 
Traditional charter offers an overview secured by a sponsor and grants authority to the project manager. 
The Agile charter has a focused approach, emphasizing objectives and guiding the team. 
The Agile project charter aims to form a strong foundation for project success and stakeholder satisfaction in Agile project management . 
Review a Product Roadmap
Welcome to review a Product Roadmap. 
After watching this video, you will be able to describe a product roadmap and list its goals, and analyse a sample of a product roadmap with real-world examples. 
A Product Roadmap is an essential tool that serves as a blueprint for product development. 
It's a visual roadmap that takes you from the beginning to the end of a product's development journey. 
It provides an overview of how a product will evolve. 
Typically developed by a business analyst, this roadmap is updated into a release map guiding the project's development throughout the release. 
A Product Roadmap serves several critical goals within an agile project charter. 
It offers a high level view of the plan functionality for each sprint or release, giving everyone involved a clear understanding of the project's direction. 
It is a broad guide that is open to changes and provides flexibility in adapting to evolving project needs. 
It acts as a tool for the team and management to compare planned outcomes with actual results throughout one or more releases. 
A project roadmap prioritizes User Stories in the Product Backlog to ensure immediate tasks align with the project's overarching goals. 
Lastly, it aids in selecting the appropriate project management lifecycle to achieve various levels of required product functionality. 
Here is a brief description of a Product Roadmap outlining the developmental journey of a marketing application. 
It's essential to recognize that Product Roadmaps come in various forms, each tailored to specific needs. 
Note that this instance represents a hybrid project combining different methodologies. 
Through an in-depth analysis, you will be able to understand how to accomplish some of this project using traditional project management methods. 
At the same time, some of the functionality will require an Agile or Scrum approach. 
During this initial phase, the following essential steps take place, the specific features and 
functionalities of the marketing application are outlined and determined. 
The design and layout of the marketing application are refined and settled. 
The team identifies the necessary technological infrastructure to support the upcoming development process. 
The comprehensive project plan is reviewed and officially approved for execution. 
Resources and finances required for the project's successful execution are secured. 
The second phase entails substantial technical enhancements. 
The development team carry out any required upgrades to ensure optimal compatibility and performance. 
The IT system's storage capacity is upgraded to accommodate data and functionalities seamlessly. 
Security measures and protocols are reinforced and upgraded. 
Additional skilled IT personnel are recruited or assigned to the project team to enhance its expertise and ensure efficient development. 
The third phase involves detailed development activities. 
The team conceptualizes and documents scenarios and interactions to guide the development process for users. 
The list of desired features and improvements for the marketing application is refined and prioritized. 
The development team sets the pace for completing development tasks and establishes timelines for individual development sprints to track progress efficiently. 
The actual coding and development of the marketing application launches, transforming concepts into tangible functionalities. 
The team identifies, defines and acquires as necessary data sources and feeds to power the marketing application. 
Developers craft an initial working model or prototype of the marketing application to represent its functionality visually. 
A preliminary testing phase is conducted with a select group of users to gather feedback and identify early improvements. 
The comprehensive plan for launching the marketing application outlines all necessary steps and considerations. 
Final phase concludes in the actual launch and subsequent management. 
First, the marketing application is officially launched and made available to users. 
After that, ongoing monitoring and assessment of the application's performance and user interactions ensure optimal functioning. 
Any deviations from the anticipated outcomes and user feedback are diligently documented for analysis and future improvements. 
Based on feedback and observations, the initial version of the marketing application undergoes updates and refinements. 
Preliminary planning for the next version of the marketing application starts considering future enhancements and expansions. 



Here is an example of what a Product Roadmap looks like, including all the phases and steps mentioned above for creating a marketing application. 

In this video, you learned that a Product Roadmap is a visual blueprint for product development, guiding the journey from start to finish. 
It evolves and provides an overview of planned product changes. 
It provides a high-level view of plan function finality for each sprint or release, and offers flexibility to adapt to changing project needs. 
It helps compare planned outcomes with actual results and prioritizes User Stories in the Product Backlog. 
It assists in selecting the appropriate project management lifecycle. 
Case Study: Project Charter and Product Roadmap

Welcome to case study, project charter, and product roadmap. 
After watching this video, you will be able to analyze the case study on Mega City 
and evaluate the Agile project charter , and product roadmap. 
Mega City, home to over five million people, has witnessed remarkable growth owing to favorable tax laws, and a weather-friendly location. 
Many companies have relocated to the city, particularly companies in the technology sector. 
While this growth has led to economic prosperity, it has also introduced a pressing issue, traffic congestion. 
The local traffic situation has deteriorated, posing challenges for commuters. 
The Mega City Department Of Transportation, DOT, has taken the initiative to address this issue. 
The DOT recognize the urgency of addressing this challenge, and secured state funding to develop a high-tech commuter application or app to relieve traffic congestion. 
You and your team have been hired to work with internal DOT resources to develop and 
roll out a new application in the next three months. 
This app aims to empower Mega City's commuters with the tools to efficiently plan and manage their journeys by providing real-time data from various sources, thus mitigating the escalating commute times. 
This effort will require joint support from the DOT information technology, or IT team and your firm. 
DOT developed an Agile project charter and product roadmap to support this initiative. 
Let's explore the specifics of this initiative in detail. 
The project vision lists the following; Mega City is growing rapidly and congestion is a major issue, with traffic increasing by 20% in the past year. DOT, currently relies on a website to report traffic conditions. Its goal is to provide a strategic technology solution to help Mega City commuters rapidly assess and plan their trips in a way that mitigates the rise in commute times in and around Mega City's major thoroughfares. 
It desires to launch a new app before the rainy winter weather. 
The mission statement aims to provide a downloadable app that offers commuters real-time data from multiple sources to plan their trips effectively. 
The key deliverables include; customer support functionality, DOT data collection, and real-time refresh functions, IT infrastructure compatibility, IT privacy concerns addressed, 
and state compatibility with state and federal warning systems. 


Key performance indicators, KPI, lists the following: 
We must ensure that 100% of commuters can access the app at no cost. 
The app should calculate and share commute options in less than 20 seconds, allowing customers to grade their experience and provide feedback. 
User data should be sent to DOT when the app is accessed, and real-time updates must be 
available when conditions change. 
The app should have a reliable interface with proper connection and the current DOT infrastructure, including light rail, bus schedules, and airlines. 
Emergency preparedness notifications should be sharable in real time, while commuter privacy must be protected and never disclose. 
Timeline will have specific milestones for each phase; define, design, develop, test and launch, and improve. These phases will take place over the next three months with defined start and end dates. 
The fixed budget for this project is $2.2 million. 
The assumptions include the ability to develop a viable app that local commuters will use it, 
and that data collection will enable future solutions. 
The main constraint is the fixed budget and adherence to state and federal regulatory environments. 
The team needs to be aware of potential risks, such as going over budget, design flaws, development delays, and IT capability issues. 
There will be two teams assigned to this project. 
The development team, consisting of the IT project manager, product owner, 
scrum master, and development team, and the management team comprises sponsor, DOT, and state representative. 


The team will work through four phases to bring the Mega City commuter application to life. 
Phase 1: Define and Design App. 
In this phase, the project takes shape. 
App functionality is defined, design is finalized, and IT infrastructure needs are identified. 
The project plan is approved and funding is validated. 


Phase 2: Develop App and Prototype. 
As the project gains momentum, IT infrastructure and IT security capabilities are upgraded to support the app, user stories are developed, and product backlog is refined. 
The team determines velocity and sprint schedules are established, and app development commences, concluding in an initial prototype. 



Phase 3: Test and Launch New Commuter App. 
This phase sees the app put to the test. 
An initial Beta test is conducted, user feedback is gathered, and the launch plan is finalized. 
The new app is launched, its performance is monitored, and valuable feedback is documented. 
Phase 4: Improve App V1, and Plan for V2. As the project nears completion, enhancements are made to version one of the app based on feedback. 
Simultaneously, plans are formulated for Version 2, envisioning further refinements and innovations. 
This project is a joint effort between your team and the internal DOT resources. 
The IT team will handle IT upgrades and security, while the development team will lead app development. 


In this video, you learned that Mega City's growth due to tax benefits and location has led to traffic congestion challenges. 
DOT secured state funding for a high-tech computer app to alleviate congestion. 
Your team will develop the app in three months for efficient trip planning and real-time data. 
DOT has developed an Agile project charter and product roadmap to support this initiative. 
The app's vision is to rapidly assess and plan trips, addressing congestion around major thoroughfares and the four phases of development include; define and design, develop, 
test and launch, and improve. 

Stacey Diagramming Method
Welcome to the Stacey Diagramming Method. 
Our expert, Daniel Yemens, will describe the Stacey diagramming method and its two evaluation variables. 
And guide you through efficiently analysing a completed matrix for a marketing app development project using this method. 
>> Speaker 2: The Stacey diagramming method was developed by Ralph Stacy as a means to determine the best way to effectively implement a project. 
This method is also referred to as the Stacey matrix or the Stacey method. 
The model evaluates a project or objective in two ways. 
First of all, the level of agreement on final deliverables is the first variable analysed. 
High levels of agreement normally indicate fewer changes as the project progresses. 
Low levels of agreement are an indicator of greater levels of potential change and project difficulty. 
The second variable evaluated is technical complexity. 
Higher levels of change and required innovation are more likely present when technical complexity is high. 
The model scores agreement times complexity and calculates the value. 
Based on the value, a lifecycle choice is made that best suits the project and provides the greatest chance for success. 
As a rule, projects with higher agreement and lower complexity are candidates for traditional project management techniques to include waterfall. 
As you can see in this illustration, this would be the simple and the tricky categories. 



Agile project candidates are those with less agreement and higher complexity. 
Note, there are some projects that should be reevaluated if the agreement is too low and complexity is too high. 
You'll notice on the illustration we have the complex area, the area best suited to agile projects. 
And we have an area that Stacey annotated as chaos, which means further evaluation is required. 



Let's quickly examine a completed matrix for a marketing application development project. 
We begin with our project objectives. 
We score each objective based on agreement and complexity. 
Notice our agreement scale, a low level of agreement means that we score a 5, a high level or almost 100% agreement means we score a 1. 
So in our first project objective, IT hardware and software upgrades, the level of agreement is a 2, that is about 80%. 
We do the same for all of the other objectives and notice that for our five objectives, the range is somewhere between 1 to 3.5. 
The second variable is technology complexity. 
Looking at our scale, a known technology would rate a score of a 1. 
Almost 100% new or unknown technology rates a score of 5. 
In our example, our technological complexity scores range from 2 to 4. 
Project score is calculated by multiplying the level of agreement times the technology complexity. 



In this particular case, objective number one scores a 6, 2 scores a 2, 3 scores a 4. 
If you look at the table, this puts us into the predictive and incremental area. 
The best preferred methodology based on agreement and complexity for objectives 1 through 3 would be to take a predictive or incremental approach. 
However, notice what occurs for objectives four and five. 
Now we're scoring well above the 7 point threshold. 
Normally, scores that range between 8 to 20 are candidates for Iterative or Agile or Scrum implementation. 




You and your team will have an opportunity to perform a Stacey analysis on an on hands lab. 
We will provide a blank Stacey analysis spreadsheet for use during this lab and for your evaluation. 
Thank you for your attention and best of luck in the course. 
>> Speaker 1: In this video, you learned that the Stacey diagramming method was 
devised by Ralph Stacey for optimizing project implementation. 
It assesses projects in terms of agreement on deliverables and technical complexity. 
The two main evaluation variables are the level of agreement on final deliverables and the project's technical complexity. 
The model scores agreement times complexity to calculate a value, aiding in selecting an appropriate project lifecycle. 
And notable sections in the illustration simple, tricky, complex, and chaos areas based on agreement and complexity levels. 
Stakeholder Identification and Engagement Methods
The Scrum Master's role in identifying and engaging stakeholders
A Scrum Master plays a vital role in ensuring project communications are planned, managed, and controlled throughout the project. They must communicate on a frequent basis with multiple stakeholders, including the Project Manager, Product Owner, development team, customers, managers, and more. This communication is essential to ensure all stakeholders contribute to the project's success and to address blockers and impediments as they are identified.
The Scrum Master's role in stakeholder identification
While the Project Manager is responsible for creating and approving the Project Charter and Stakeholder Register, the Scrum Master can support these efforts by providing subject matter expertise and guidance. Scrum Masters can share their expertise to identify key stakeholders early in the project and offer preferred strategies for effectively managing these stakeholders and ensuring they fulfill all required commitments.
The Scrum Master's role in stakeholder engagement
The Scrum Master is responsible for ensuring that key stakeholders stay involved and engaged throughout the project, both internal and external stakeholders. They often act as a liaison between project teams and stakeholders, planning and facilitating team meetings throughout the project. This includes sprint planning, daily scrum meetings, product reviews and demos, and retrospectives. The Scrum Master manages the agenda and attendance and ensures all required individuals participate.
The Scrum Master also collaborates with product owners and stakeholders to ensure the workload is distributed effectively if someone from the team is asked to work on multiple teams. Additionally, they engage with stakeholders to address blockers or impediments, manage conflicts, and foster a cohesive team dynamic.
Introduction to the Working Agreement
The primary focus of the Scrum Master is the development team. One of the key engagement tools they work on with the team is a "Working Agreement." A Working Agreement is a document developed by the team and for the team. It helps align goals, define shared values, agree on how work will get done, and serves as a document that ensures the entire team is working as one. 
In the upcoming content, we will share an example of a Working Agreement and demonstrate how it can positively impact team engagement.

On the other hand, Team 2 will be responsible for designing, developing, and launching the new application and using the Scrum methodology.
The success of this hybrid project relies on close collaboration between the two teams.

Review a Working Agreement 
Welcome to review a working agreement. 
Our expert, Daniel Yemen, will break down the step-by-step process of how a working agreement session delivers concrete outcomes. 
He will also go over a template for a working agreement and the key areas to keep in mind. 
Working agreements create transparency and clarity and allow teams to share common goals, expectations, and objectives. 
The Scrum Master facilitates the working agreement development meeting. 
Here is a brief step-by-step approach to help ensure you're working agreement session 
provides tangible results. 
Step 1, begin with a positive check-in question to break the ice. 
For example, ask each team member to share their thoughts about a successful team they worked on. 
Step 2, introduce the working agreement concept and template if you have one. 
Let each team member know, there are no right or wrong answers. 
Encourage the team to speak frankly and share their thoughts. 
In addition, set an early ground rule, respect each other's right to share and avoid interruptions and judgment. 
Step 3, you may wish to brainstorm areas that fit into each working agreement category. 
You may also wish to hand out post-it notes for those who are not comfortable verbalizing their thoughts. 
Step 4, once the team has shared their thoughts, open up the working agreement for a discussion, ask if there is anything to add, delete, or change. 
Finally, in step 5, finalize the working agreement with a vote. 
Common voting methods include thumbs voting, list of five, or dot voting. 
The agreement is then shared with all team members and posted in a common area or virtually. 
Let's review a template for a working agreement and the primary areas to consider. 
Note, you and your team are not limited solely to the prompts in this template. 
Add the team name and date. 
If you don't have a team name, have some fun and create one. 
Next, brainstorm shared values. 
Examples may include commitment, courage, respect, etc. 
List people and support norms. 
This includes how the team will treat others and support each other. 
Some scrum masters like to define when the team ceremonies will occur and add expectations. 
For example, everyone will attend and participate in the daily scrum meeting. 
Next, brainstorm the team approach to work norms. 
These may include expectations for ourselves, others, and a variety of additional topics. 
Define communications norms. 
This can include how we will communicate, tools that will be used by the team and more. 
Communications is a primary source of conflict. 
Take time to define the norms in this area. 
Defined done as a team ensure everyone agrees on a common definition. 
You may want to include any reviews, checks, balances, etc. 
Finally, list shared documents that must be available to everyone on the team. 
Back to step 5, when everything that needs to be in the working agreement is complete, 
vote as a team to accept this agreement as a tool that will be used to guide positive behavior and drive project success. 
You will be asked to develop a working agreement in a hands-on lab for your new scrum team. 

In this video, you learned that working agreements create transparency and clarity, 
fostering shared team goals and expectations. 
The working agreement is a tool to guide positive behaviour and drive project's success. 
The Scrum Master facilitates the working agreement development meeting and to create a robust working agreement, begin with a positive icebreaker, introduced the concept brainstorm categories, discuss and modify and finalize with a team vote for unanimous agreement.

Module 1 Glossary: Initiating the Project 
Welcome! This alphabetized glossary contains many of the terms you'll find within this course. This comprehensive glossary also includes additional industry-recognized terms not used in course videos. These terms are important for you to recognize when working in the industry, participating in user groups, and participating in other certificate programs.

Module 1 Summary: Initiating the Project
Congratulations! You have completed this lesson. At this point in the course, you know:
* The Agile project charter zeroes in on the project's objectives, serving as a guiding document to keep the team on course and ensuring it doesn't stray from its purpose. This document aligns the expectations of stakeholders with the Scrum team's capabilities. It establishes clear objectives and bridges the gap between business aspirations and technical implementations.
* The product roadmap contains the project vision, the mission statement, key deliverables, key performance indicators, timeline, assumptions, and phases of the project.
* The Stacey Analysis evaluates the project objective in two ways: level of agreement on final deliverables and technical complexity. High levels of agreement normally indicate fewer changes as the project progresses. Low levels of agreement indicate greater levels of potential change and project difficulty. Higher levels of change and required innovation are more likely present when technical complexity is high.
* Working agreements create transparency and clarity and allow teams to share common goals, expectations, and objectives. Steps to create a working agreement include:
* Check in with each team member
* Working agreement concept introduction
* Brainstorming areas that fit into each working agreement category
* Discussion
* Finalization

User Story and Product Backlog Review
Welcome to User Story and Product Backlog. 
Our expert Daniel Yeomans will describe user stories, including its two components, the role of the product backlog and its contents. 
>> Welcome to a brief demo on user stories. 
User stories are developed by the product owner and placed in a prioritized product backlog. 
User stories scheduled for completion in the next sprint are selected during sprint planning by the team. 
The team bases its selection on velocity considerations. 
Velocity is a measure of how many stories can be completed to done, in a given sprint. 
Velocity is generally based on story points. 
A user story has two parts. 
Part 1 is general information, formatted by: role, need, and value statements. 
As you can see in this example, each user story is given a title. 
In this example, the title is simply the first user story supporting the customer role. 
In Part 1, the user story should tell a story. 
"As a role, I need a capability to provide desired value." 
In this example, "As a coffee shop customer, I need easy access to orders to save time and reduce waiting." 
Part 2 of the user story lists acceptance criteria. 
This is an overview of measurable objectives the development team will build to support the user story. 
The goal of the team during the sprint, is to build in 100% of the acceptance criteria, 100% completion of a user story is referred to as done. 
In this example, our acceptance criteria to satisfy this customer is: easy access to the menu, pricing for all options, secure monetary transactions, and order confirmatons. 
You and your team will have an opportunity to create user stories and a hands-on lab later in this course. 
We provide a blank template for you to accomplish that hands-on lab. 
>> Let's now explore the product backlog and what it contains. 
>> The product backlog serves as a repository for all confirmed user stories. 
User stories are placed in the product backlog by the product owner once the story is confirmed by both the customer and the development team. 
User stories are prioritized based on value to the customer. 
User stories scheduled for development in the near term must be detailed appropriately, 
and able to be broken out into tasks that can be assigned and estimated. 
A product backlog contains the following, as you can see in this example. 
First, each user story has a distinct identifier number. 
Each user story is prioritized. 
Each user story is categorized. 
A common categorization method is simply sharing the role the user story supports. 
In some cases, a firm may employ multiple scrum teams. 
If this is the case, list the name of the team responsible for the user story. 
Next, identify the role, need, value, and acceptance criteria as listed in the user stories you developed. 
Assign story points to each user story. 
We will discuss story points in a later video. 
And finally, provide any clarifying comments. 
This example, we list the sprint when the user story will be implemented. 
You and your team will have an opportunity to create a product backlog in a hands-on lab later in this course, we provide a blank template for your use during that hands-on lab. 

In this video, you learned that user stories are developed by the product owner and placed in a prioritized product backlog. 
A user story has two parts. 
Part 1 is general information formatted by role, need, and value statements. 
Part 2 of the user story lists acceptance criteria. 
The product backlog serves as a repository for all confirmed user stories. 
A product backlog contains several specifications. 
Product Vision
Welcome to product vision. 
After watching this video, you will be able to: Define product vision. 
Describe the importance of product vision. 
Illustrate the product vision format along with an example. 
Have you ever been lost while traveling or exploring a new city? 
Without clear directions, you can lose focus. 
The same applies in business. 
You must know what you are trying to accomplish to create real value. 
To reach your goals and serve your customers, your organization needs a vision 
everyone can follow clearly. 
Product vision describes what a product or project aims to achieve in the long term. 
It serves as a guiding light for the development team and stakeholders, providing a shared understanding of the product's purpose, goals, and intended impact on users or customers. 
Product vision is crucial for several reasons. 
Overall, it provides a foundation for the entire product development process. 
There are numerous reasons why product vision is crucial to the product development process. 
First, product vision provides the development team and stakeholders with a clear direction. 
It serves as a decision-making compass, helping the team prioritize features, functionalities, and improvements aligning with the vision. 
A well-defined product vision unites the team around a common goal. 
It creates a shared understanding and a sense of purpose, fostering collaboration and synergy among team members. 
When everyone is working towards the same vision, it improves communication and 
minimizes misunderstandings. 
Product vision sets clear goals for the team. 
By understanding the desired outcomes and impact, the team can define measurable objectives that indicate progress toward achieving the vision. 
Product vision also outlines the target audience and their needs to help the team tailor the product's features effectively. 
A compelling product vision can attract investors and stakeholders. 
When the vision clearly articulates the product's potential value and impact, securing funding and support for the product's development and marketing becomes easier. 
Let's explore the product vision format. 
People commonly present the format as an elevator speech, which means the vision should be short enough to deliver during an elevator ride, typically lasting about 30 seconds to two minutes. 
It includes the following elements. 
The first element is for. 
It defines the target audience for the product, identifying the intended users. 
The second element is who? 
It defines the problem or opportunity that the product addresses. 
This element describes the pain points or needs of the target audience that the product aims to solve or leverage. 
The third element is our product. 
It defines the name of the product or category. 
This element helps to identify and position the product within its market context. 
The fourth element is that, it defines a compelling reason or benefit for stakeholders to buy the product. 
The fifth element is unlike. 
It defines the key differentiators that set the product apart from its competition. 
This element explains why the product is unique and better suited to meet the audience's needs than other alternatives. 
The last element is our product. 
It defines the ultimate value proposition or differentiator. 
This element includes the core of the product and its primary selling point. 
For example, let's hear a product vision for the product Task Z. 
Our product vision is to offer virtual project managers an innovative and user-friendly project and task management platform, Task Z. 
With its budget friendly approach, Task Z empowers workflow tracking through 
standard Kanban methodology and customizable features, ensuring a seamless and 
efficient project management experience. 
This product vision clearly states that the target audience is virtual project managers who must manage and assign multiple tasks remotely. 
The vision specifies that Task Z is a fully digital project management task management platform. 
Task Z stands out as it utilizes standard Kanban methodology and templates, 
offering a simple and intuitive user experience. 
Unlike more complicated and costly platforms, Task Z is a user-friendly and budget friendly platform. 
It is an easy to understand and affordable option. 
With Task Z, project managers can customize the platform to suit their needs and requirements, ensuring a seamless and efficient project management experience. 
In this video, you learned that product vision is a clear and inspiring description of what 
a product or project aims to achieve in the long term. 
A product vision is crucial as it provides a clear direction, unites the team around a common goal, and sets clear and measurable objectives. 
Product vision format includes : Defining the target audience, the problem the product addresses, its name or category, key differentiators from competitors, ultimate value proposition. 
Risk Management and Relative Estimation
Risk Register and Relative Estimation
Welcome to risk register and relative estimation. 
Our expert, Daniel Yamens, will describe the force-field analysis and outline the process of filling out the Agile risk register. 
He will also explain the relative estimation technique as well as the Fibonacci sequence with the help of an illustration. 
Agile risk registers are used to document risks for each user story in the product backlog, 
the product owner, Scrum Master, and development team work together to identify and qualify potential risks. 
Risks can impact the overall complexity of a user story and the level of effort required to complete it successfully. 
Failure to identify risks may lead to issues, delays, or an overall inability to accomplish user stories as planned. 
A force field analysis, sometimes abbreviated as an FFA, is a great brainstorming tool 
to identify potential risks. 
Let's review a quick illustration of a force field analysis. 


First of all, risks can be classified as both opportunities and threats. 
Driving forces are causes and events that can lead to potential opportunities. 
Opportunities may allow the team to increase scope, accelerate schedules, or reduce overall costs. 
Some examples of driving forces that may lead to opportunities could be high capacity, high skills, management support, adequate funding, and a high economic value for the product. 
Restraining forces are causes and events that may lead to threats. 
Threats can impact overall project's scope, schedules, and costs negatively. 
Some examples of restraining forces that can lead to threats could be low capacity, low skills, limited management support, inadequate funding, and an overall low economic value for the project or product. 
Once the FFA is complete, complete an Agile risk register. 
Ensure the project is identified and use version control. 


A risk register is subject to change as a project and product launch progress. 
Next, assign each risk a unique identifier for ease of tracking. 
In addition, map each particular risk to a user story in the product backlog. 
Use risk metalanguage to document a risk. 
Break out the risk by cause, also referred to as a situation or condition. 
The event that may transpire from the cause and the impact of the event. 
A risk statement should tell a story, cause, event, and impact. 
Always assign a risk owner. 
This is a team member or stakeholder who will monitor the risk throughout the project and provide support as required should the risk occur. 
Risks are qualified by probability times impact. 
Every risk register should include a scoring scale as shown on this illustration. 
Risk score is calculated by multiplying the probability times the impact. 
Note that opportunities reduce potential risk. 
Therefore, the impact is scored as a negative number. 
Some risks may have triggers. 
This is an early warning that a risk is about to occur. 
Develop risk responses. 
You may want to implement some responses before a risk occurs. 
Other responses may need to be implemented when the risk occurs. 
Total up the individual risk scores. 
The higher this number, the higher the risk exposure for the product launch. 
Look for opportunities to reduce overall risk exposure and work as a team to keep this score as low as possible. 
You will have an opportunity to perform a force field analysis and develop an Agile risk register in a hands-on lab that follows. 
Let's now explore relative estimation. 
Projects use Scrum because the agreement on requirements is low and technical complexity is high. 
Therefore, estimating user stories in hours or days is not practical. 
Relative estimation technique compares user stories in terms of complexity. 
Once the complexity is determined, story points are assigned. 
Story points are assigned based on a numbering system developed by Michael Cohn. 
He based this system on the Fibonacci sequence, which assign story points using 1, 2, 3, 5, 
8, and 13. 
The reason why Fibonacci is used is based on the assumption that user story level of effort does not increase proportionally as complexity increases. 
Let's see how this works. 
We begin with five user stories. 
The team looked at user story Number 1 and ensured that they were familiar with the role, 
the need, the value, and the selection criteria. 
They then looked at user story Number 2 and determined that user story Number 2 was less complex than user story Number 1. 
They turn their attention to user story Number 3 and determined that user story Number 3 was similar in complexity to user story Number 1. 
They looked at user story Number 4 and determined that user story Number 4 was the most complex of all of the user stories up until this point. 
And finally, they looked at user story Number 5 and determined that user story Number 5 
was relatively simple and the least complex of the five user stories. 
We now have our user stories organized in terms of complexity. 
How can we use this information? 
What we do is we place each user story in the appropriate column. 
One story point, two story points, three story points, five story points, eight story points. 
In this particular instance, there were no user stories falling within the eight story point column. 
We are using 1, 2, 3, 5, and 8 based on the Cohn model that we shared a moment ago, 
using the Fibonacci sequence. 
We can now calculate the total story points supporting this particular product or launch. 
We have one story, user story Number 5 in the one story point column. 
We have one story in two story points column. 
We have two user stories in the three story point column, and we have one in the five story point column. 
We can go ahead and add this up. 
One story point times one equals one. 
Two story points times one equals two. 
Three story points, and this happens twice, is six. 
So we add three and three and we have one user story that scores five story points for a total of five. 
When we add up our total, we have 1, 2, 3, 3, 5 for a total of 14 story points. 
This particular product will require 14 story points worth of effort to go ahead and complete the launch. 

In this video, you learned that Agile risk registers document risks for each user story in the product backlog. 
Risks can impact the overall complexity of a user story and the level of effort required to complete it successfully. 
A force field analysis, sometimes abbreviated as FFA, is a great brainstorming tool to identify potential risks. 
Relative estimation technique compares user stories in terms of complexity. 
Once the complexity is determined, story points are assigned. 
The Fibonacci sequence assign story points using 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, and 13

Velocity
Welcome to velocity. 
After watching this video, you will be able to describe velocity and its importance. 
Review velocity with the help of examples. 
Velocity measures the number of Story Points a team believes it can commit to and successfully complete in a sprint. 
It serves as a valuable indicator of a team's productivity and helps estimate the amount of work a team can accomplish in future sprints. 
It is a pivotal planning indicator that the product owner and team use to determine how long a product will take to develop before launch. 
Velocity can vary among different teams. 
For example, team A might be able to complete twelve Story Points in each Sprint. 
The duration for completing the Story Points relies on the product they are developing and the average time needed to complete each Story Point. 
On the other hand, team B may be able to complete 15 Story Points. 
The time required to complete a Story Point may be less than that of team A based on the technical complexity and scope of each User Story. 
Velocity is not intended as a competitive metric, and it naturally differs across teams. 
In the example, it's important to note that team B's velocity doesn't necessarily imply greater speed or efficiency than team A's. 
Velocity serves as a crucial indicator for estimating the duration of a launch. 
For instance, a product launch entails the fulfillment of User Stories amounting to a total of 50 Story Points. 
The team's velocity is 10 Story Points per Sprint. 
Each Sprint will require 15 days. 
Here is how velocity becomes a key planning indicator. 
The team can complete the entire launch in five Sprints. 
This means the product owner can share a schedule with management and commit to a product launch in 75 days assuming each Sprint requires 15 days. 
5 into 15 days a key to success avoid making commitments that exceed your ability to deliver. 
If you forecast accomplishing ten Story Points per Sprint, ensure you fulfill that commitment. 
As velocity quantifies the number of Story Points a team can undertake and successfully accomplish according to the Sprint plan. 
Let us review an illustration of a team that forecasts a velocity of 15 for a Sprint. 
The team successfully completed User Stories, 1, 3, and 5 as planned, achieving a total of eight Story Points according to the plan. 
The team did not manage to complete User Stories 2 and 4 as intended. 
Despite their efforts to achieve these User Stories, they could not meet the plan deliverables. 
In this scenario, the team's velocity stands at eight. 
It signifies the count of Story Points successfully delivered in accordance with the initial plan, the team must determine what went wrong with User Stories 2 and 4. 
In all likelihood, the product owner failed to perform a final validation of User Story 2 before the finalization of Sprint planning. 
In addition, the team likely estimated User Story 4 poorly or missed some key tasks. 
When identifying the required work to attain done status, the team completed 8 of 15 forecast Story Points. 
The effectiveness of this team is approximately 53%. 
It is now behind schedule if they forecast the completion of 15 Story Points over an extended number of Sprints to support a launch. 

In this video, you learned that, velocity measures the number of Story Points a team believes it can commit to and successfully complete in a Sprint. 
Velocity is not intended as a competitive metric, and it naturally differs across teams. 
Velocity serves as a crucial indicator for estimating the duration of launch.

Estimated duration: 25 minutes

* Calculate Velocity for the Next Sprint 
Sprint Analysis
A team committed to complete 21 Story Points. Here are the results of the Sprint. 

Respond to the following questions:
1. What is the team's velocity as a result of this Sprint results? 
2. What should the team forecast for the next Sprint? 
Potential Solution
The following are probable solutions. Your solutions may differ.
My solution : 
5+2+3= 10 story points, the team velocity is 10 story point per sprint, if each sprint requires 15 days, then you need 2 sprints to fulfil the commitment of 21 Story points.
 Team effectiveness is 47.6%
